THE RED PAGE
Night.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
As full as the days are of light in midsummer his
life was of Love.
Oh, the year on the mountain, the year by the
river, the year on the plain !
As full as the nights are of light when the rounded
white moon sails above,
Was his life of the Love that went from him to
never come to him again !
The mountain at sunrise rose out of the darkness
to welcome the light,
With a glitter of silvery eyes in its facets and
fissures of stone,
And op’ning of buds on the bushes and spreading
of petals of white—
But Ms welcome of Love was prostration of
heart on the steps of a throne!
The year by the river, slow-flowing, slow-flowing;
the year on the plain ;
Oh, the day-skies and night-skies were eostaeies
rolling round spirit-fed sight 1
But then the two lives fell asunder; and whirl-
winds, splashed with chill rain,
Cried above the brute chaos : “ Night, endlessly!
moonlessly, starlessly Night! ”
Sydney.
PI.
In the Clouds of Fantasy.
In reality, men and women are nothing
but balloons : they all have a globe of spirit
and a car of flesh. Sometimes—most times,
indeed—the globe never gets inflated, and
the car remains on earth—fulfilling ever so
many useful purposes and ever so few that
are beautiful. And sometimes a man has
Just spirit enough to make him a captive
balloon—he would like to soar higher if he
could ; but his fleshy car is too heavy— he is
tethered always to the ground. And rarely you
find an individual—like Blake, or Shelley, or
Keats—whose head is pure hydrogen, and
who carries so little material ballast that he has
hardly touched earth before he is away again
forever, a speck in the ether almost undis-
eemible by the grosser souls he leaves behind
him. Only sometimes he keeps on singing,
singing, singing as he mounts, and to those
who would fain follow, but who are left be-
hind like the little lame boy of Hamelin, the
mere song of what he sees is very precious.
• * * *
Richard Le Gallienne is maybe hut a cap-
tive balloon, yet his rope of fancy is long
enough to let him reach the clouds, and
sometimes for a moment, one thinks, he
must have escaped from earth wholly. His
“Prose Fancies” (there are two series of
them now) are better than his poems;
he is as puss-dainty in them, and
freer ; and when his mood is at its rarest he
sways you without labor. And for criticism
there needs only quotation.
Science says that the moon is a dead world, a
cosmic ship littered with the skeletons of its crew,
and from which every rat of vitality has long since
escaped. It is the ghost that rises from its tomb
every night, to haunt its faithless lover, the
world. It is a country of ancient silver-mines,
un worked for centuries. You may seethe gaping
mouths of the dark old shafts through your tele-
scope. You may even see the rusting pit-tackle,
the ruinous engine-houses, and the idle pick and
shovel. Or you may say that it is counterfeit
silver, coined to take in the young fools who love
to gaze upon it. It is, so to speak, a bad half-
crown.
As you will 1 but I am of Endymion’s belief—
and no one was ever more intimate with the
moon. For me the moon is a country of great
seaports, whither all the ships of our dreams come
home. From all quarters of the world, every day
of the week, there are ships sailing to the moon.
They are the ships that sail just when and where
you please. You take your passage on that con-
dition. And it is ridiculous to think for what a
trifle the captain will take you on so long a jour-
ney. If you want to come back, just to take an
excursion and no more, just to take a lighted look
at those coasts of rose and pearl, be will ask no
more than a glass or two of bright wine—indeed,
when thecaptain is very kind, a flower will take
you there and back in no time; if you wane to
stay whole days there, but still come back dreamy
and strange, you may take a little dark root and
smoke it in a silver pipe, or you may drink a little
phial of poppy-juice, and thus you shall find the
Land of Heart’s Desire ; but if you are wise and
would stay in that land for ever, the terms are
even easier—a little powder into a phial of water,
a little piece of lead no bigger than a pea, and a
farthing’s-worth of explosive fire, and thus also
you are in the Land of Heart’s Desire for ever.
I dreamed last night that I stood on the bluster
itig windy wharf, and the dark ship was there.
It was impatient, like all of us, to leave the
world. Its funnels belched black smoke, its engines
throbbed against the quay like arms that were
eager to strike and be done, and a bell was beating
impatient summons to be gone. The dark captain
stood ready on the bridge, and he looked into each
of our faces as we passed on hoard. ‘ls it for the
long voyage?’ he said. ‘Yes ! the long voyage,’
I said —and his stern eyes seemed to soften as I
answered.
At last we were all aboard, and in the twinkling
of an eye were out of sight of land. Yet, once
afloat, it seemed as though we should never reach
our port in the moon—so it seemed to me as I lay
awake in my little cabin, listening to the patient
thud and throb of the great screws, beating in the
ship’s side like a human heart. . . .
There were three friends I had especially set
out to see ; two young lovers who had emigrated
to those colonies in the moon just after their mar-
riage, and there was another. What a surprise
it would be to all three, for I had written no letter
to say I was ootning. Indeed it was just a sudden
impulse, the pistol-flash of a long desire.
I tried to imagine what the town would be like
in which they were now living. I asked the
captain, and he answered with a sad smile that it
would be just exactly as I cared to dream it.
‘Oh, well then,’ I thought, ‘I know what it
will be like. There shall be a great restless, toss-
ing estuary, with Atlantic winds for ever ruffling
the sails of busy ships, ships coming home with
laughter, ships leaving home with sad sea-gull
cries of farewell. And the shaggy, tossing water
shall be bounded on either bank with high grand
walls, and on one bank shall be a fretted spire
soaring with a jangle of bells, from amid a tangle
of masts, and underneath the bells and the masts
shall go streets rising up from the strand, streets
full of faces, and sweet with the smell of tar and
the sea. O captain ! will it be morning or night
when we come to my city ? In the morning my
city is like a sea-blown rose, in the night it is
bright as the sailor’s star.
*lfit be early morning ; what shall Ido ? I
shall run to the house in which my friends lie in
happy sleep, never to be parted again, and kiss
my hand to their shrouded window, and then I
shall run on and on till the city is behind and the
sweetness of country lanes is about me, and I
shall gather flowers as I run, from sheer wanton-
ness ; and then at last, flushed and breathless,
I shall stand beneath her window, I shall stand
and listen, and I shall hear her breathing right
through the heavy curtains, and the hushed garden
and sleeping house will bid me keep silence, but I
shall cry a great cry up to the morning star, and
say, “ No, I will not keep silence. Mine is the
voice she listens for in her sleep. She will wake
again for no voice but mine. Dear one, awake;
the. morning of all mornings has come ! ’’ ’
As I write, the moon looks down at me like a
Madonna from the great canvas of the sky. She
seems beautiful with the beauty of all the eyes
that have looked up at her, sad with all the tears
of all those eyes ; like a silver bowl brimming with
the tears of dead lovers she seems. Yes, there are
seaports in the moon; there are ships to take us
there.
* * * *
Comment on this anti-climaxes as abomin-
ably as the parson’s announcement of next
Sunday’s collection before the organ-echoes
have done ringing through the aisles ; but it
should be said that one personal reference is
to Le Gallienne’s young wife (he himself is
only 31) whom he lost not long ago after a
year of idyllic marriage. (“They learn in
suffering what they teach in song ” —and
coin their griefs to drachmas at the pub-
lisher’s.) And to close with a curious proof
of the human mind’s antipodes : compare the
beautiful ending above with another ending
also previously quoted on this page, but not
in juxtaposition—the reflection which con-
cludes Frank Swettenham’s description of a
Malayan moonrise :
There is nothing cold about this Eastern moon.
Seen, half-risen, against the dark foliage of the
mountain, it glitters like molten silver, dazzling
the eyes, and as it soars serenely upward seems
the very perfection of beauty, light, and purity.
Strange that the delight and glory of mankind
since ever the earth was peopled, the emblem of
unattainable longing, should be only a gigantic
cinder.
Notes.
William Main’s “ Bush Solitudes and
Other Verses ” (Geo. Robertson) are homely old-
man’s-rhymes, lacking intellectual value or in-
terest, but occasionally getting in a quaint heart-
appealing touch. And these rough lines stand
out sufficiently for quotation :
For a man that keeps a bush pub. can’t afford to have a
heait ;
He’s got to be a human parasite,
Manipulating poison, keeping men on till they part
Tneir last stiver—turn them out whatever night.
And if there’s any trouble, why, the constable’s at hand ;
It need not trouble him, for that’s the way
To carry on the business, in a free, enlightened land—
Kind hearts and decent grog don’t seem to pay.
Why, by-the-by, do so many old men turn to
rhyming? Is it a symptom of senile degeneration ?
or an outlet for no-longer-head-controlled emo-
tions? or a kill-time when the poor back-number
sits lonely in the chimney-corner unheeded by
Youth hurrying past in a turmoil of alien thoughts
and aspirations-? And you may note, too, that
old Scotchmen are particularly venomous : the
Irish mostly rhyme when they’re young: the Eng-
lish don’t rhyme—because they eat too much, and
keep their blood in their stomachs?
# * * * *
An article on “ Kipling in India ” in M‘Cltjre’B
for July is worth looking at. An Indian officer
wrote to a friend in Australia a while ago :
“You ask what we think of Kipling out here.
Personally I can never dissociate him from his
odious personality—a little, ugly, spectacled, sen-
suous cad ; absolutely lacking in manly qualities.
Still, there is no doubt that he is a perfect genius
in his way ; his marvellous powers of observation
and his knack of picking up the smallest details
deserve no other name. He is afraid of a horse,
and loathes the sight of one, yet he will write a
better account of a steeplechase than many a
racing man. He has seen a battery of artillery
once or twice, and perhaps talked occasionally to
gunners, and he can write pages of jargon which
no one outside the Royal Artillery can under-
stand. His writings are wonderfully true to life,
though, of course, a little exaggerated here and
there, but wonderfully little on the whole.”
A between-lines reading gets something of the
same result from E. Kay Robinson’s article.
Among Kipling’s former attributes are mentioned
his “stoop, heavy eyebrows, spectacles, sallow An-
glo-Indian complexion, jerky speech, abrupt move-
ments ... no sportsman . . . indifferent horse-
man ” —not a probably-popular compound. But
compensations are noted : and it looks as if K.’s
(and others’) knowledge of his intellectual superi-
ority accounted for the unpopularity alleged
against him by our Australian’s correspondent.
Again, Kipling’s original sketches—or many of
them —are represented as being done after a bard
day’s office-grind ; and a man who stews his brain
for 10 or 12 hours a day is almost bound
to be moody and disliked. He has no vitality
to spare for social trifles. And Kipling
worked with all his heart and soul and strength
whence, according to a favorite theory of this
page, his success. Some wise women have an idea
that an easy baby is a poor baby ; and when you
look at the physiological chances there’s some-
thing in it. For, generally speaking, big heads
mean big brains, and big brains mean big men —
wherefore, big men mean big pangs in parturition.
And isn’t there some analogy between childbirth
and bookbirth? So that—it is curious how all
the thoughts of the world, like all its material
substances, spring from a handful of elements,
perhaps (who knows?) from one element—you
come back to the maxims of much for much, little
for little, for everything payment. It is all poeti-
cally put in one of Olive Schreiner’s “Dreams”
—which are not too well-known to debar quota-
tion :
THE ARTIST’S SECRET.
There was an artist once, and he painted a picture.
Other artists had colors richer and rarer, and painted
more notable pictures He painted his with one color,
there was a wonderful red t;lovv on it; and the people
went up and down, saying, “ We like the picture, we like
the glow.”
The other artists came and said, “ Where does he get
his color from ?” They asked him ; and he smiled and
said, “ I cannot tell you ; ” and worked on with his head
bent low.
And one went to the far East and bought costly pig-
ments, and made a rare color and painted, but after a
time the picture faded. Another read in the old books,
and made a color rich and rare, but when he had put it
on the picture it was dead.
Bui l be artist painted on. Always the work got redder
and redder, and the arlist. grew winter and whiter. At
last one day they found him dead before his picture, and
they look him up to bury him. The other men looked
about in all the pots and crucibles, but they found noth-
ing they had not.
And when they undressed him to put his grave-clothes
on him, they found above his left breast the mark of a
wound —it was an old, old wound, that must have been
there all his life, for the edges were old and hardened ;
but Heath, who seals all things, had drawn the edges
together, and closed it up.
And they buried him. And still the people went about
saying, “ Where did he find his color from ? ”
And it came to pass that after a while the artist was
forgotten—but the work lived.
Picturesquely put, and calling up a host of kindred
ideas : the noxious pill-advt.’s text “For the blood
is the life ” ; vampires ; the o’d life-elixir hunter,
craving that essential ingredient, the blood of a
new-born babe (see him in Dumas) ; the blood-
sacrifice for the life of a savage chief ; that which
purchases the eternal life of religious sectaries;
and a hundred others. On what dim troglodytish
thinker did the germ-idea flash? lie would be
worth knowing.
Reprints.
[For absolute melody— the sweet succession of syllables
—“A Fountain of Tears” has no superior in English.
“Annabel Lee” equals it—no more; and maybe O’S.
owes something to Poe's influence. O'Shaughnessy, in the
strife of bards, is fairly enough claimed as a Celt., and he
had the ear and the fancy which are chief concomitants
of Celtic verse. He was born 1844, die 1881. His scope
was limited, and his performance hardly reached hit
early promise.]
A. Fountain of Tears .
If you go over desert and mountain,
Far into the country of sorrow,
To-day and to-night and to-morrow,
And maybe for months and for years ;
You shall come, with a heart that is bursting
For trouble and toiling and thirsting,
You shall certainly come to the fountain
At length,—to the Fountain of Tears.
Very peaceful the place is, and solely
For piteous lamenting and sighing,
And those who come living or dying
Alike from their hopes and their fears ;
Full of cvpress-like shadows the place is,
And statues that cover their faces :
But out of the gloom springs the holy
And beautiful Fountain of Tears.
And it flows and it flows with a motion
So gentle and lovely and listless,
And murmurs a tune so resistless
To him who hath suffered and hears—
You shall surely—without a word spoken,
Kneel down there and know your heart brokeiL-
And yield to the long curb’d emotion
That day by the Fountain of Tears.
For it grows and it grows, as though leaping
Up higher the more one is thinking ;
And even its tunes go on sinking
More poignantly into the ears ;
Yea, so blessed and good seems that fountain,
Beached after dry desert and mountain,
You shall fall down at length in your weeping
And bathe your sad face in the tears.
Then, alas ! while you lie there a season,
And sob between living and dying,
And give up the land you were trying
To find ’mid your hopes and your fears ; .
O the world shall come up and pass o’er you i
Strong men shall not stay to care for you,
Nor wonder indeed for what reason
Your way should seem harder than theirs.
But perhaps, while you lie, never lifting
Your cheek from the wet leaves it presses,
Nor caring to raise your wet tresses
And look how the cold world appears,—
O perhaps the mere silences round you
All things in that place grief hath found you,
Yea, e’en to the clouds o’er you drifting
May soothe you somewhat through your tears.
You may feel, when a falling leaf brushes
Your face, as though some one had kissed you •
Or think at least some one who missed you
Hath sent you a thought,—if that cheers;
Or a bird’s little song faint and broken,
May pass for a tender word spoken :
—Enough, while around you there rushes
That life-drowning torrent of tears.
And the tears shall flow faster and faster,
Brim over, and baffle resistance,
And roll down bleared roads to each distance
Of past desolation and years ;
Till they cover the place of each sorrow,
And leave you no Past and no morrow :
For what man is able to master
And stem the great Fountain of Tears ?
But the floods of the tears meet and gather ;
The sound of them all grows like thunder :
—O into what bosom, I wonder
Is poured the whole sorrow of years ?
For Eternity only seems keeping
Account of the great human weeping ;
May God then, the Maker and Father—
May He find a place for the tears !
ARTHUR OSHAUGHNESSY.

digestion, with oppression after food, constipation
or irregular bowels, flatulency, gravel, weakness or
pain across small of back, loss of muscular power,
gloomy, remorseful, fearful of something going to
happen, disturbed sleep, moaning, talking, grinding
teeth, tearful or want to laugh.
I have known instances in which
most of these symptoms were present
in one patient. As a rule a great
many are present. In no case are
many absent.

T. E A F!
When you go out shopping, consider, think
of the money in your pocket, and take account
of the many stores whose various attractions
are laid before you. There is A, with temp-
tation in the form of a pretty show ; there is
B, whose wiles are innumerable; and 0,
who says that he is the man for your money ;
and so on, throughout a list as long as
Geprge-street, each smiling dealer has some-
thing to say and to offer.
Please Pause!
Hearken not to the song of the syren, sing
she ever so sweetly; heed not the voice of
the charmer, charm he never so wisely.
Your pocket’s concerned; and there are
pirates about. As the poet pertinently puts
it: “ There be land-sharks and water-
sharks,” and it is for you to see that your
dealings are with none of these voracious
monsters. Take .warning in time, for it will
be no use when the new taxes come round—
and the tax-gatherer won’t take no for an
answer—for you to plead : There would have
been enough and to spare had I been wise
enough to

Various Verses,
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
AT A WINDOW.
Fair arm, that liest in pink
Upon her window sill,
Would I were at the brink.
To touch you and to thrill,
More than a song can tell !
Fair arm, why do you hide
Her face from westering sun ?
Would he with it abide
Long after day is done,
His fate and mine were one !
His parting kisses glow,
Shot level from the deep;
Ah ! he and I must go,
But thou through Love’s light sleep
Guard o’er her face shalt keep.
M.L.
HUBERT CHURCH.
WHERE ARE MY DOLLARS GONE?
Where is my cash ? With this eternal query
I’m pestered all my moments, grave and gay;
It haunts me in the midnight dark and dreary,
It dogs me at the dawn and close of day.
Where is my cash ? My watch, I know, reposes
Safe at my Uncle’s, tightly held in pawn ;
My bills are known to all the tribe of Moses—
But, where the mischief are my dollars gone ?
I lead a virtuous life. A trifle glorious
I may get, now and then, with comrades gay,
And paint the town vermilion, when uproarious,
And turn the dully night to crimson day;
But, when at home at duty’s call diurnal,
I pass my days as peaceably as John,
Our cabbage-vendor, at his toil eternal— , •
Again I ask : Where are my dollars gone ?
The dice-box —gambling? Goodness knows I hate it,
And if at nap I linger now and then
And wander home with friends a bit belated,
’Tis but as man who loves his fellow-men.
My winnings are but scant; with melancholy
I own Dame Fortune’s golden smile hath shone
But seldom on the hands my comrades jolly
Have dealt me—but where are my dollars gone ?
I drink but little. Am abstemious —very !
Though midnight suppers sometimes cost me
dear ;
My bill for Bass and Guinness, cham. and sherry—
I cut it down a dozen times a year.
’Tis not my fault that oysters through the season
Don’t grow on hedges, and the price of yon
Choice wines has waxed entirely out of- reason,
Again I ask —where are my dollars gone ?
The girls—God bless ’em ! —Bellas, Janes, and
Bessies,
They cost me little—now and then a glove,
A summer hat, a parasol expresses
To Maud a fraction of her poet’s love ;
A lady’s watch inscribed in fashion tender,
A bracelet which she sometimes deigns to don,
A brooch that gleams in simple jewelled splendor,
Poor trifles these ! Where are my dollars gone ?
P. LUFTIG.
A TRILOGY OF QUATRAINS.
From womb to tomb, what is the lot of man ?
A little while to play, to weep, to plan,
To work, to laugh and frown, to love and hate,
To curse and pray, then slumber—if he can !
From womb to tomb ! ’Tis but one letter’s chang.e
To link extremes ; and should we think it strange
If from the grave, as from Earth’s fecund womb,
We entered life of wider, grander range ?
If doomed to sleep for ever in the dust,
Or wake in resurrection of the just,
Or live again to meet more direful death,
Why vex our souls? The thing that must be,
must.
Maoriland.
J. LIDDELL KELLY.
“AMONG THE LILIES.”
I must not tell, my sweet, of what mine eyes
Have gazed upon ; the breezes must not hear —
Not even the night-bi’eezes ; they would swell
Full with the wonder of it; and the skies
Would blazon it in starlight far and near :
My heart is aching with it, but I fear
I must not tell !
I must not tell, but I may seek a rose,
A young rose pouting with a pure delight,
And bend my lips above its fair cupel.
Then my sharp sighing will perchance disclose
Some shadow of my thought in my despite;
But what I picture in its wealth of white
I must not tell!
I must not tell why that heaped cloud of snow
Should hold my eyes through all the living hours,
Or why one curving lily should compel
Hot flushes of remembrance ; even though
Sweet words of utterance fell in music-showers,
The memories wakened by those mists and
flowers
I must not tell !
Vic.
WYVIS.

communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.

the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand\ that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

SATURDAY, SEPTEMBER 19, 1890.
Griffith on Federation.
T-He Federation question has dropped into
another of its long slumbers, and for months
Past it has hardly been heard of in politics.
Almost the only event which lias happened
of late to save it from utter oblivion —in the
Southern provinces, at all events—has been
a learned and profoundly obscure deliver-
ance by Samuel Griffith, Chief Justice of
Queensland, which cast no new light on the
subject and added to it some darkness which
it didn’t previously possess. Griffith is
one of those learned men of large and hesi-
tating mind whose mission it is to shed
oblivion as they go. His intellect can find,
tlle smallest matter, innumerable
difficulties which are not visible to the
average mind, and by keeping them all in
View at once, and looking at them so care-
fully that they seem all of the same size, he
generally comes to the conclusion that every-
thing is impossible. He never, or hardly
ever, solves any problem, but he has stated
many problems from innumerable points of
view, and proved that all the points of view
were equally untenable, and that conse-
quently it. was impossible to do anything and
equally impossible to do nothing -and then
he has sat down and left them in that condi-
tion. Griffith’s contribution to Federal
iterature was about as learnedly obscure as
his contributions to the literature of other
questions, but he managed to emerge suffi-
cmnt ly from a sea of erudition to state one
real difficulty. The statement was made in
a darkly circumlocutory fashion, but it was
The retention of autonomv hv tv.,. „
so far as it is necessary to^un-enderT 6XCept
good of the Federal State, is an essential ,v 6 S' 6ll6l ' lll
must be always borne in mind in consist d iu° n wh,ch
of the general nature of a Federal Government^ 6 Subject
This question, despite the large obscuritv
which Griffith casts upon it, Ls reallv thl
great problem of Federation. Put in fill
language, the question is whether the Central
Government should have as little and the
Provincial Gc ernments as much power as
possible, or vice versa. When it comes to
defining the authority of the two institu-
tions, should the 1 ederal Government be
made supreme as regards certain defined
matters, and everything else be left to the
provincial authorities, or should the Local
Parliaments have certain limited powers and
everything else be left to the Federal autho-
rity ? There will undoubtedly be friction
between the two on many occasions—-it is
impossible to create two such authorities
which won’t quarrel and quarrel frequently ;
therefore, is the Constitution to be adjusted
with a view to the Federal or the Provincial
Legislature coming out on top as a regular
thing? Griffith holds it as an “essential
condition ” that the Federal authority should
have as little power as possible, but no one
has yet shown why it should be essential.
The chief argument in favor of Griffith’s
view of the matter is that the United States
constitution is mostly built that way, but
whether the U.S. in this respect is an argu-
ment or only a shogking example is a matter
of opinion. There the State rights are so ex-
tensive and the power of the Central Govern-
ment so limited that when Chicago was in the
hands of what practically amounted to an
insurrectionary force, it was only by a high-
handed breach of the law that troops could
be sent to guard against the possibility of the
great Pork City being burnt to the ground.
The Central Government only prevented the
disruption of the nation in 1860-65 by another
gross breach of law. It has so little real
power that it can’t prevent the colored popu-
lation of the South being disfranchised by
sheer violence, and it can’t interfere effec-
tively to abolish habitual murder by lynch-
law, and even the suppression of slavery was
arrived at by a more or less illegal process.
There is so little legal uniformity in the
Union that the divorce which is granted in
one State is often not recognised in another,
and in many other ways the doctrine of State
rights has been carried to the verge of chaos.
The U.S. constitution is certainly, in many
respects, a model to be followed, but it is by
no means a model to be slavishly followed.
In the matter of Australian Federation,
there are no conflicting racial interests to be
considered—it is wholly a question of creating
the most workable constitution. If the most
workable constitution could be arrived at by
abolishing the provincial Legislatures alto-
gether, there would be no outrage or injustice
involved in their abolition, so far as The
Bulletin can see. At present the Austra-
lians have certain self-governing powers
which they exercise through the Provincial
Parliaments. The same Australians propose
in future to exercise some of these powers
instead through the Federal Parliament. It
is merely a question how much power the
people in one capacity will surrender to them-
selves in another capacity. Therefore, even
if all the legislative power was surrendered
to the Federal Government, and if
that provincial autonomy whose reten-
tion Griffith alleges to be “essential”
was wholly destroyed, the people would have
just as much autonomy as they have now,
only of another kind. If the provincial
idea prevails, the people of Queensland or
any other province will have more power to
govern their own province exclusively and
less power to govern Australia in general;
whereas, if the power of the Federal Govern-
ment is extended and that of the local Par-
liaments curtailed, they will have less power
to govern their own province and more
authority over the continent at large—which
seems practically as broad as it is long. The
amount of authority belonging to each indi-
vidual will remain just the same ; there will
be exactly the same amount of self-governing
power, only it will be differently distri-
butecl. The question of how much power is
to be entrusted to the united provinces and
how much retained by the provinces dis-
unitedly involves no question of liberty what-
ever. The talk of “surrendering” State
rights means very little, seeing that the
people are merely surrendering their own
rights to themselves, however the matter
goes.. And the “autonomy ”of the separate
provinces doesn’t matter a straw for its own
sake ; if the Federal Constitution can be
made to run easier by having much State
autonomy or little, or none at all, it is all
one. Obviously there must be State auto-
nomy to relieve the Federal Parliament of
an unendurable mass of detail, but in The
Bulletin’s opinion there is at present
moi'e risk of having too much State autonomv
than too little.
* * * *
The State rights theory apparently starts
on the assumption that the separate pro-
vinces are natural enemies, that their in-
terests are naturally hostile, and that the
Australian in his federal capacity will natur-
ally proceed to commit brutal outrages on
himself in his provincial capacity unless he
ties himself up tightly to himself as a pre-
caution. In other words, each province
seems to assume that the first object of the
Federal Government will be to do somethin**
dreadful to it individually, and the only way
to prevent this disaster is to make the
Federal authority too weak to do anythin**
serious. The Bulletin, at all events, sees
no reason or logic in this contention. If the
Federal Parliament has very extensive powers
then the Victorian delegates will have exten-
sive authority to make laws for Queensland
just as the Queensland delegates will have
tor Victoria, and as each province will have
to live under the laws it helps to make for
for ° ne j I \ Wlll P resumabl y do as much
resnectabW r *V he P olitical machine
the Wimmera. The theory 2Statute
must be jealously preserved seems to be
largely based on the idea that wherever
there is a difference of opinion the Provincial
Government must necessarily be right while
the Federal one must necessarily be wrong,
therefore it is proposed to keep the Federal
monster strictly tied up for fear it should do
some injury.
* * * *
So far as The Bulletin can see,' it is much
better to make the Central Government too
strong than too weak. There is no special
sanctity in the provinces as they now stand ;
they were not specially outlined by Provi-
dence, and their casual boundaries don’t
represent any special sum-total of human
wisdom. The first and most important of
State rights is naturally the right of the
States to preserve their own territory, and it
is difficult to see that even this matters much
—an outline on a map is a mere outline and
nothing more. Even if the first Act of the
Federal Parliament was to abolish every
State outline, and map out all the provinces
anew, there seems to be no reason why it
shouldn’t do so, provided it mapped them
more intelligently than the last people who
did it, and it is difficult to see that anybody’s
liberty would be outraged thereby. It is
merely a question whether Griffith in one
capacity is to give himself liberty to do cer-
tain things to himself, or whether Griffith
in another capacity is to have power to pre-
vent himself doing these things to himself,
and Griffith seems about equally free and
autonomous either way. The variety of
liberty which Griffith and other provin-
cialists declare to be “ essential ” includes
the liberty of South Queensland to keep
North Queensland in partnership against its
will, and the right of Sydney to retain the
Biverina, which geographically belongs
to Victoria, and the right of New South
Wales and Victoria to drag the trade of
their far western districts away from its
natural outlet through South Australia. And
the tyranny which they want to guard
against is the policy which would give the
Federal Government power to put right
these anomalies. In other words, the State
autonomy which Griffith holds to be essen-
tial is largely the right of every State to
wallop its own nigger within its own
boundaries.
* * * *
The Bulletin’s belief, in short, is that
the Federal Government should be trusted
altogether, or not at all. It must be trusted
to make tariffs for all Australia, to manage
the currency, the defence system, and the
railways, and many other public works. It
must also be trusted to make uniform mar-
riage and divorce laws, else an Australian
will be of legitimate birth in one part of his
own country and a bastard in another. It
should be empowered to create a uniform
civil and criminal code, because one code is
quite enough for a mere handful of 4,000,000
people, and because the multiplicity of
codes makes justice more difficult without
necessarily making it any better. The fact
that the Customs law will be made by the
Federal Government naturally implies that
it will likevvise regulate excise laws, for
different excise laws would mean Protection
in one province against another. It should
also have full power to readjust State boun-
danes if a majority of the population of any
distuct demands severance from one province
or annexation to another—the liberty which
permits Melbourne or Sydney to boss out-
.Vl Ag districts against their will is not so
very essential after all. And it is an open
question whether the Federal Parliament
should not be allowed to define its own
powers and those of the Provincial Legisla-
tures also. The Federal Parliament, as the
greater institution, would naturally attract
the bulk of the able politicians from the
subordinate Assemblies. If the ablest
politicians of Australia can’t be trusted to
settle the division of power satisfactorily it
is difficult to see who else can do it. In any
case, the question between Federal and pro-
vincial authority is simply a question whether
the balance of power should rest with the
best men Australia can find to sit in the
National Council, or with the smaller men
who are left behind, and The Bulletin
would give the former the benefit of the
doubt in every case and risk it. At present
the great bulk of the provincial M’sL A
know that they are the small parochial per-
sons who will be left behind, and their
natural desire is to retain as much power as
possible for themselves when the abler men
are gone. If N.S.W for instance, sends
a ’ C '\ R , K y TII£KS ’ Lynj3 ’ See, Crick
Ashton, M‘Millan, Abbott, Wise, and
Barton to the Federal Parliament, then
it may find itself left with a Provincial Go-
vernment consisting mostly of Preacher
Smailes, Whidpon, William Wilks Gar-
rard and Poet Neild. How does it feel
about preserving State rights by entrusting
a very large measure of power to the
Smailes-Wilks Ministry ?
The Case of Thomas Suffield.
Had the case of Thomas Suffield been tried bv
.any Judge but Mr. Windeyer it would be a case
demanding enquiry. As it was tried by Judge
Windeyer, it is a case urgently demanding en-
, Suffield was charged on April 8 1801
at Maitland, N.S.W., before Judge Vindeyer
and a jury, (1) with rape, and (2) with attempted
rape. He was found guilty on the second count
and sentenced to 10 years’ penal servitude—the
maximum penalty. ine
The prosecutrix was a housemaid, aged about 18
employed by Thomas Croudace, J.P* manager of
the Lambton and Durham collieries, residing at
Lambton, near Newcastle, N.S W nv,
character was good. ' previous
Her story varied at different times Tr, „
she said she had been absent during the !, ft . enoe
and evening of Sunday, Dee. 21 1800
home a little before 10 o’clock. The
moonlight, but rather cloudy. On ton nf Tf
gates at Croudace’s grounds she saw Vh OJ !
Suffield. He said “ Good-night ” • she ,I,B*
reply. She passed him and went oii, andalinf
later she felt a blow on the back of the S
which made her silly. Suffield threw her do ™
ness! 6 "' W 1 aU herf ° rCe til] shelo «tcon B cSs.’
Mr Croudace and his son gave evidence at
singular variance with this. They said they heard
screams, ran out of the house, and found prosec
tnx lying on the ground. She was ‘‘ crouched n
and partly on her side,” and her hands were raise
as if m fear. Mr. Croudace said, “ W hat s h
mattery S he - Oh, that
He said Has the bull hurt you ? ” She replied
lam hurt all over, repeating it two or three
times Croudace then looked up and saw the
bull about 30 yards away.
Nothing about a man ! nothing about an assault'
nothing about a blow on the back of the head !
Prosecutrix was carried to the house, where Dr
Stapleton examined her an hour afterwards and
found various bruises and injuries, but none on
the back of the head. Prosecutrix remained in a
hysterical state all night, and early in the morn
mg she told Miss Croudace, who attended her
that she had been maltreated by a man.
Later on she apparently “ thought ” that it was
Suffield who had assaulted her.
Two days after she assured Mr. Croudace that
Suffield had assaulted her, and, for the pur-
poses of “justice,” it has been Suffield who
assaulted her ever since.
* * * *
No judicial attempt was made to square prose-
cutrix’s first story of assault by the bull with
her second story of assault by Suffield.
But the Croudaces’ evidence was no doubt cor-
rect: prosecutrix’s revised story will not stand
against it for a moment.
More, on a previous occasion the same bull had
attacked and injured another person.
More, on a subsequent occasion, it attacked and
injured another woman, and the doctor who
attended her is said to have remarked that her
injuries were remarkably like those attributed to
prosecutrix.
Then the bull was shot.
Judge Windeyer may scout the attack by the
bull as absurd, but, in the face of this evidence, it
seems to be he who is absurd. And a bull is
notoriously partial to a petticoat. It was in the
guise of a bull that Jove ravished Europa.
It ha da ma n T hus was one of the fruits of this
union. Doubtless Rhadamanthus would scout
aspersions on the character of his father.
* * * *
There is thus a tremendously strong probability
that the bull did the bulk of the mischief.
Did it do all the mischief ?
The doctors apparently thought there had been
connection. If this idea was communicated to
prosecutrix, it would naturally put her upon her
defence ; in the interests of her “ good name ” she
would have to show why and wherefore.
She was a woman ; the doctors added a man.
By the hypothesis, her “ virtue ” made it neces :
sary to introduce a third factor—force applied by
the man. But the Croudaces added a bull, and
while he remained in the problem the force
was accounted for. Obviously (to continue the
hypo hesis) it was necessary to eliminate the bull.
She did eliminate him. So did judge and jury.
And the bull is there yet. *
* * * *
The bull assaulted prosecutrix; did a man
assault her as well ?
The doctors’ evidence is inconclusive. They
made no microscopic examination, and, therefore,
adduced no direct proof ; and their indirect proof,
under the circumstances of the bull’s assault, has
very doubtful value.
Yet, if no man assaulted her, why did prosecu-
trix allege a man? Why did she not throw the
whole onus on the bull ?
Either there was a man ; or else the doctors’ im-
pression must have been represented to prosecutrix
as such a certainty that she felt herself under the
necessity of inventing one.
But a consciously honest girl, rather than admit
a forcible outrage by a man, would surely stick to
the obvious bull and defy the doctors ?
Yet if there was a man, and he used force, he
was a most extraordinary man. He allowed his
victim to complete her journey by a lonely
road, to come within sight of home, to enter the
grounds, to proceed within a short distance of the
house to where her cries would be plainly audible
—and then, before the lights were out, before the
household were settled in bed, he assaulted her
with brutal violence.
Is it credible ?
And the ruffian actually sat on the gate in the
moonlight to be identified—on the gate, where he
could hear the bull laughing at him !
* * * *
Nevertheless, that man existed, and that man
was Suffield. The prosecutrix said so.
There was not a scrap of evidence to show it
beyond the prosecutrix’s say-so.
Suffield did not even know prosecutrix —bad
never spoken to her. (This she admitted). But
he was a good-looking fellow ; she had seen bun
at a distance, and admired him, and asked his
name (this was clearly in evidence), and doubt-
less thought about him as girls of 18 will think
about good-looking fellows. And Suffield went
to gaol for 10 years.
It was Suffield’s word v. prosecutrix’s, and
Judge Windeyer believed prosecutrix—as usual.
Yet, in order to believe prosecutrix, you had to
get rid of the bull, and to swallow the remarkable
ruffian who sat on the gate ; while in order to dis-
believe Suffield you had to dispose of —
1. Alibi sworn by Mrs. Warren, with whom
he lodged, to the effect that from 9.30 to 10.40 p-m.
on the evening in question Suffield was in ms
bedroom lying down, with the door open; that he
was certainly there between 9.45 and 10 p.m.
2. Alibi sworn by Miss Warren, daughter of
above, aged IG, corroborating this.
3. Oath of Miss Warren that Suffield had
only two pairs of boots, of which neither was m
the bedroom on the evening in question ; so that
if he was a ruffian on a gate he was a ruffian with-
out his boots on, who had climbed out of a window
and run half-a-mile and back again barefooted on
the chance of finding a girl of whom or whose
movements he knew apparently nothing, and
whom he might try to criminally assault for five
minutes or less almost under her employer’s win-
dows at 10 o’clock in the evening !
4. Fact that Suffield had to do this without
the newly-washed moleskins and shirt which he
was wearing at the time, and which were examined


by the police, showing any spots or stains, and
without Suffield getting one scratch or bruise or
mark.
The jury, with the countenance of Judge WiN-
deyer, disposed of everything.
* * » •
Yet Suffield bore an excellent character. He
was working as an engine-driver in a mine, but
the evidence showed that he was intelligent, edu-
cated, worthy to fill what are considered better
positions.
If the statement of an 18-year-old girl (the hysteri-
cal age—especially for an English girl like prosecu-
trix, generally later in reaching puberty) can sur-
vive her contradictions, survive the incident of the
bull, survive the improbability of her story of the
time and place of the alleged assault, and blast a
good character, over-ride a double alibi strongly
corroborated, and send Suffield to prison for 10
years, it is obvious that any man in the district
might have stood in Suffield’s shoes.
If such a defence counts for nothing against
such a prosecution, what defence can count ?
This was felt at the time, and a strong agitation
arose for a reconsideration of the case, and beat
vainly against the prejudices and precedents of
the Department of Alleged Justice.
This is where the abomination of a Botany Bay
bureaucracy comes in.
In New South Wales, once a man is convicted —
rightly or wrongly—the bureaucracy pigeon-holes
him and puts him away. That man is done with.
The amour propre of the courts, of the police, of
the self-satisfied Department, closes their ears and
eyes and noses to the foulest injustice. To suggest
that—even inadvertently—an injustice has been
perpetrated is to reflect upon them.
Therefore, they all fight like devils, tooth and
nail and claw, to keep the man in the pigeon-hole,
the case from the light of day.
* * * *
The Department of Alleged Justice is fighting
so in the case of Thomas Suffield.
The accidental Minister-in-charge is a country
lawyer—narrow, obstinate, of mean capacity,
apparently incapable of a lofty thought or a warm
emotion.
He in effect says that Suffield shall stay in his
pigeon-hole—in defiance of the above-recited facts,
in defiance of the law.
For what does the law say ?
Section 383 of N.S.W. Criminal Law Amend-
ment Act declares that “whenever, after the con-
viction of a prisoner, any doubt or question arises
as to his guilt, or any mitigating circumstance in
the case, or any portion of the evidence therein, it
shall be lawful for any justice, by direction of the
Governor —on the petition of the prisoner, or
some person on his behalf, representing such doubt
or question —or by direction of a judge of the
Supreme Court, of his own motion—to summon,
and examine on oath, all persons likely to give
material information on the matter suggested.”
In Suffield’s case these conditions are all
present. There is doubt or question as to his
guilt; 6000 residents of the Newcastle district have
petitioned for examination into his case; many
members of Parliament —including those repre-
senting the Lambton and neighboring districts —
express grave apprehensions that he has been un-
justly convicted.
The Department of Alleged Justice is not asked
to set Suffield free —it is only asked to permit
enquiry into his case.
It refuses, absolutely and obstinately, to permit
enquiry.
Suffield is in his pigeon-hole, neatly strangled
with red tape, and there he may rot till the Day
of Judgment.
Damn the Department of Alleged J ustice !
The Bulletin does not know whether Suffield
is innocent or guilty; but it knows the facts
demand, and the law allows, an enquiry into his
case.
Out with Suffield from his pigeon-hole !
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Beer.
The Local Option Bill, introduced, in N.S.W.
Assembly, the other day, by P.M.G. Cook, is
the beginning of a big tight on the compensa-
tion question, and a good many other questions
as well. Minister Cook is distinctly against com-
pensation, and in that respect The Bulletin is
with Cook, but not wholly for Cook’s reasons.
When the brewer throws his dummy publican
out of his “ tied ” house, says the P. M.G., he
doesn’t compensate the victim, and when the
Government fires out the real owner, the
brewer, there is no reason why he should be
compensated either. Moreover the publican is
licensed each year to carry on his trade for one
year only. There is nothing in the license
about any right of renewal; there is no pro-
mise, direct or implied, that it will be renewed ;
the license is paid for one year only; if the
publican chooses to assume that it will be
renewed the community is no more liable to
pay for his assumption than it would be if he
assumed that he was going to be made a
K.C.M.G. Further, the country can’t honestly
or logically compensate him without compen-
sating also the vigneron, and his laborers, the
cooper, bottler, barmaid, barman, the brewer
and his employes, the liquor-importer and his
employes, the vigneron’s landlord for the loss
of a tenant, the owner of the pub. for depre-
ciation of his property, itself for loss of rates
through depreciation in the rateable value of
the property, and so on ; and as it can’t afford
to do this the compensation question drops
down dead on the spot. There are several
other matters, however, on which J. Cook is
much less logical. He proposes Local instead
of National Option, and this is a poor, weak,
hopeless device at the best. Local Option
means that here and there a prohibition district
will be created in which no liquor can
lawfully be sold, and all the liquor it
wants will come in from the next dis-
trict a few miles away, and all its
hard-faced inhabitants will go into the next
district periodically for a great satisfying
drunk. National Option means that, except
for illicit distillation, there will be no liquor to
be had nearer than the next province, and, if
illicit alcohol can be kept down, its adoption
would mean that all N.S.W. would be sober,
barring only a thin fringe of gin round the re-
mote borders. Local Option would certainly
bring business to the trains and trams, carrying
people into the drinking districts and back
again, and promote horse, bicycle, and pedes-
trian exercise for the same purpose, but, after
all, it is only like Mr. Cook habitually
washing two or three places on himself,
each the size of a half-crown, and leav-
ing the rest of himself as he was.
Further, there is no more reason why some
parts of a province should be permitted to have
alcohol and others prohibition than that one
electorate should be allowed on its own separ-
ate vote to have Freetrade or Protection or the
Single-tax or Marriage with a Deceased Wife’s
Sister, while the next one hasn’t, or that
Sydney and Newcastle should be allowed to set
up different divorce-laws. If the system that
the majority for the whole province bosses the
minority is good enough in other things, why
shouldn’t it be good enough in this matter also ?
Another question which naturally arises is this :
Seeing that the Cook party wants a referendum
so badly on its own fad is that same party
prepared to give the referendum all round, or
do the teetotallers want it for themselves only ?
Further, will they put the whole question to
the suffrage ? Instead of simply allowing the
community to vote as to whether licenses shall
be abolished or reduced or remain as they are,
will they also take a vote as to whether they
shall be increased ? And when they ask that
a Cold Tea majority shall be empowered to
compel a whisky minority to abstain from
alcohol, will they authorise a whisky majority
to compel a Cold Tea minority to drink
alcohol, so that things shall be fair all
round ? Again, are they prepared to pro-
hibit the export of beer, wine, spirits
and all other intoxicants, or do they simply
propose to shut down on local consumption as
much as possible, and let the stuff be exported
instead of drunk on the premises, and thus
permit N.S.W. to make money by passing the
Drink Curse along? And, supposing Sydney
goes in strong for Local Option, will Tooth’s
and Toohey’s and the other dozen Sydney
breweries be shut up by that same vote, or will
the capital city simply swear off drink itself
and still make money by corrupting the poor
weak brother in the interior ? Moreover, will
that same vote prevent foreign liquor being
landed at Sydney for distribution in the in-
terior, so that this branch of trade will have to
shift to Newcastle or Grafton or some other
unregenerate town, or will the city still make
all the money it can by the distributing trade
as well as by manufacture, and thereby further
corrupt the weak brother in the back-blocks ?
Lastly, what does Mr. Cook think of the policy
of the Government of which he is a member in
heavily protecting the local beer-manufacturer
in opposition to its own Freetrade principles
and to his own teetotal principles ? A few more
questions are held over for want of space.
The “ Evils of State Banking.”
A typical slab of Sydney Daily Telegraph’s
articles on the “ Evils of State Banking ” :
The answer —a most immoral and pernicious one, be-
sides being fallacious—is suggested in the paragraph from
the Labor political address already quoted : —“ A national
bank would he in its endurance coeval with the stability
of the nation.” That is to say, it would have, as an addi-
tional security to depositors, the tax-paying power of the
whole community, together with the right to pledge the
earnings of future generations if occasion required and
opportunity offered. So that, if A deposited £lOO in the
State Bank, and the State lent it to B on security which
turned out to be worth only £25, it could tax the whole
alphabet to make up the difference to A, or, if that did
not suffice, it could “ float a loan ” on the security of the
future taxpaying power of the alphabet and its children.
. . . Compared with the evident expectations of how a
State Bank would invest its funds, these (private) institu-
tions do not advance a sufficient amount of money upon
their securities, and they charge too high a rate of inter-
est. But if the State Bank is to advance more upon the
same securities than the present hanks will do, the mar-
gin of security must of necessity he less than that
of the present banks, and the risk of loss must conse-
quently he greater. And if the risk of loss with
private hanks is now so great that they prefer the
humiliation of reconstruction to facing it, how can an in-
crease of such risk, by any possibility, be looked upon in
the light of an increase in stability? Again, it is fair to
assume that, in the aggregate, those who borrow money
do so upon the best terms that they can. That is to say,
no one pays 9 per cent, to a bank for money if he can get
it elsewhere for 8 or 7 per cent. It follows, then, that (in
the aggregate) those who owe money to the banks pay (or
are charged) the ruling rates, because they are the lowest
at which the required accommodation can be obtained.
This contains, practically, the gist of the whole
argument against State Banks, and the argu-
ment won’t bear inspection. There isn’t, and
never was, any promise that the State Bank
would lend more upon its securities than the
private banks do. It is believed, however,
that it will lend at lower interest. In the first
place, the worst troubles of the private banks
are not through lending too much on securities
to their regular customers but through lending
vast sums without any security to the directors
and their friends. A bank with an independ-
ent State auditor —not a mere shadow like the
average private auditor who dare not aggravate
the directors for fear of losing his billet—is
practically certain not to do so. This should
stop one big loss which otherwise the interest
would require to cover. The State Bank will get
its capital at, say, 3J per cent., and beyond that
it will have no dividends to pay. Against this,
consider the dividends paid by the private in-
stitutions in their good days. In ’B7, for in-
stance, the Commercial of Sydney paid 25 per
cent. (.£150,000); 8.N.5.W., 17i (.£175,000) ;
Australasia, 12 per cent. ; Union, 14 per cent. ;
A.J.S., City, New Zealand, and Commercial of
Australia, 12J each ; National and Q.N., 15
per cent, each ; and so on. In one half-year of
1888, fourteen Australian banks doing business
in N.S.W. paid £818,172 in dividends, while a
State Bank, even if it borrowed its capital at
4 per cent., would have paid only £234,460.
That is one reason why the State Bank could
lend at lower rates than the private institutions,
and yet accumulate larger reserves. Further,
the State Bank would not tout for English de-
posits et ruinous interest. The fact that it had
State security behind it would so minimise the
chances of a run by depositors that it could
work safely with smaller cash reserves—in other
words, it could keep less money lying idle and
unprofitable. The absolute security of its
State guarantee would get it deposits at the
very lowest rate of interest. With deposits at
a minimum rate of interest, no necessity for
keeping very large coin reserves, no 15 or 20 or
25 per cent, dividends to drain away its profits
in good years so that it would have very scanty
reserves against bad ones, a solid guarantee, in
its State audit, against the lending of huge
sums on no security to directors and their
parasites, no reconstructions to saddle it with,
the responsibility of paying per cent, for many
years on money that could otherwise be obtained
at 3 per cent., a note-issue which the State
guarantee would make equivalent within the
country to gold, a large connection to start
with in the shape of the Government business—
with all these advantages it is very uphill work
for the Telegraph to demonstrate the evils of
State Banking. The institution would doubt-
less, as mentioned in the Telegraph’s jeremiad,
sometimes lend ,£lOO on security which depre-
ciated to £25, but in that case it would simply
charge the loss to bad debts as the honest pri-
vate banks do, and pay it out of profits, as they
do, and there would be an end of it. No one
ever proposed to guarantee that the State Bank
wouldn’t make losses. As to the statement
that people who now borrow at 7 or 8 or 9 per
cent, borrow at “ the lowest rate at which the
accommodation can be procured,” no one ever
denied that. It is so self-evident that it isn’t
worth mentioning. Everyone borrows at the
lowest rate he can manage whether it be 9 or
860 per cent. It is an equally-solid fact that
everybody who falls off a roof falls as short a
distance as he can manage—and one fact is just
as relevant to the State Bank matter as the
other.
Protection for Stamp Collectors.
Following upon a philatelic outcry against un-
fair traffic in obsolete stamps by the Sydney
postal authorities comes a discovery of un-
authorised and scandalous trading by certain
individuals at Melbourne G.P.O. An unsuit-
able issue of 2s. stamps in dark green was made
last year, and after eight days the issue was
officially stopped, 150 sheets of unsold “speci-
mens ” being condemned to destruction. Im-
mediately the stamp-collector’s price for the few
specimens that had gone into circulation rose to
a fancy figure, and dealers bought and sold them
as genuine rareties. Meanwhile the condemned
sheets were privately disposed of by two or
more officers of the G.P.0., who, without abso-
lutely robbing the Department —for they sub-
stituted ordinary 2s. stamps in place of the
obsolete ones they were selling made a
shilling or eighteenpence premium on each item
in the illicit deal. The craze for stamp-
collecting cannot survive many more revela-
tions of this sort. Pretty soon the obsolete
stamps that would, in the ordinary course of
things, be most valuable, will become impro-
perly common, like the five shilling Samoan
stamps, postmarked without going through the
post, or unpostmarked if the buyer prefers, and
procurable at about the ordinary price of
bananas. All the charm of collecting—-and it
seems to have charms for many people, includ-
ing Baron Rothschild and the Duke of York
—will disappear. Philatelic fanaticism needs
the romance of dirty unique specimens and
fabulous prices to keep it going. Considering
that stamps mark historical changes, the over-
throw of potentates, establishment of re-
publics, &c., and have, moreover, a particu-
lar interest for citizens of the Hempire which
issues fifty different pictures of Y.R. from its
various post-offices; and again also remembering
that the postage-stamp sprang from dear old
Hinglandand claims Queen Victoria as its patron
saint, so to speak, The Bulletin moves a vote
of condolence with every collector who paid 10s.
in the open market for a dark-green Victorian
stamp which he might have purchased surrepti-
tiously for three “bob.” These people put
faith in the Postal Department, and suffered for
it.
Brer Babbit.
Another Bill has been introduced in N.S.W.
to wipe out brer-rabbit, two previous Acts
having been utterly insufficient for the purpose.
On the first, in fact, he fattened and multiplied,
and moreover provided the means for the most
lurid razzle-dazzle that most townships in the
Never Never have ever known. The second he
could afford to treat with silent contempt.
Minister for Lands Carruthers, having filled
himself up with rabbit lore, now tries what a
city attorney can do to grapple with about the
most puzzling problem of the back-country.
On the whole, he doesn’t seem to have got quite
so mixed as might have been expected, because
even a good bushman might easily get astray
here. For (and this is at the bottom of the
whole thing) there isn’t one rabbit problem ;
there are three or four. And the problem in
the Central division isn’t much more like the
problem in the Far North-West than measles
are like typhoid. Two objects have apparently
been aimed at in this Bill—one, simultaneous,
compulsory, and continuous vermin-destruc-
tion ; the other, local administration. Down
to date one squatter, using poisoned twigs or
pollard, has worked in the winter; his neigh-
bor, using poisoned water or fence-traps, has
worked in the summer. This has been an ex-
cellent arrangement for the rabbit. Another
neighbor, generally a mortgage co., hasn’t
worked at all. Local boards (three-fifths
locally appointed, two-fifths Govt, nomineed)
may direct that destruction shall commence on
a certain date, and may say what means shall be
adopted. The latter provision will be mostly im-
practicable, because every paddock has its own
characteristics, and these should govern the
means to be employed. If a holder fails to
move, the board may do the work, and the cost
is to be a first charge upon the defaulter’s pro-
perty. The boards may strike a rate to cover
cost of administration, <fcc., up to id. per sheep.
This ought to be more than enough ; but it is
dangerous to fix a figure like that. For id. per
sheep may mean, in the least-infested country,
-Jd. per acre, whereas in the worst-infested
country, which doesn’t now average a sheep to
20 acres, it means l-40d. per acre. And it will
cost as much per acre to effectually carry out
the work in the worst-infested country as in
the other. The average pastoral expert in the
rabbit country puts the cost of repression
{extinction, they assume, is out of the ques-
tion) at id. per acre per annum—say, on
the average, l-18d. per sheep. It is,
therefore, not easy to see why any board
should be empowered to strike a rate
of per sheep, except, it may be, to repay
loans got for the construction of barrier fences,
seeing that the board does not itself carry out
the work of destruction. What is to be feared
is that expenses of administration may be piled
on, just as in connection with the adminis-
tration of the Lands Dept. One very satisfac-
tory feature of the Bill is that the duty is im-
posed upon the Minister to destroy rabbits on
public lands. Hitherto, abandoned areas in the
West have been the principal breeding-places
for both rabbits and wild dogs, and their exis-
tence has imperilled all the efforts of Crown
tenants, which discouraged many from making
any efforts at all. Even now it seems that the
destruction of vermin on these lands is con-
tingent upon Parliament voting the money. It
is, however, doubtful whether any difference
should be recognised between the Minister and
Crown tenants and private owners. The latter
will certainly never feel safe so long as there is
any better escape for the Minister than for any
other individual, especially as hitherto succes-
sive Ministers have inactively permitted des-
tructive hordes to issue from these lands. Other-
wise, and generally speaking, the Bill is better
than the inexperience of the Minister and the
complexity of the problem might have led
one to expect.
S.M. Herald on the Vic. match industry
It is published far and wide that the match industry of
Victoria has acquired two-thirds of the local trade. It is
admitted that it may have given the public matches
cheaper to the extent of 3d. per gross, or £1440 in all.
And it has also given ernploymentto 65 persons, including
57 females. But what has been the price? The earnings
of the operatives are equal to £1755 per annum, or, with
the total saved by the public, £3195. The Customs re-
venue suffers to the extent of £lO,OOO. The Journal of
Commerce, which gives these particulars as the result ot
an inquiry, pertinently asks who has obtained the differ-
ence of nearly £7OOO.
Figure out this wondrous hunk of Freetrade
reasoning seriously. The State obtains a cer-
tain amount of revenue from the duty on
matches. The industry pays ,£1755 a year in
wages. The public saves, it is estimated,
£1440 a year by the reduction in price. If the
duty was retained, however, and if there was
no local manufacture, so that all matches paid
duty, the revenue would be £lO,OOO better off,
and assuming that the importers, having no
local competition to face, didn’t raise the price,
then the country would be better off by about
£7OOO, because it would pay that amount to
itself. In other words, since the duty was put
on the revenue gains a small amount, the
public gets its matches cheaper, sundry em-
ployes get £1755 a year in wages, the local
manufacturer gets a profit, and, according to
the S.M. Herald, the country loses £7OOO a
year, not because it ever got that amount, but
because it might have got it if something had
happened which didn’t, and if something else
hadn’t naturally ensued as a consequence of the
thing that didn’t happen.
Sydney Telegraph sums up the reasons why
there should be no State Bank thus :
1. Because of the admitted evils which have accom-
panied State control of less important, less difficult, and
less complicated matters. .
2. Because of the admitted risks which are involved in
the extension of State administration in any direction.
3. Because of the new and dangerous relationship that
it would create between the State and many of its indivi-
dual members.
4. Because the proposal involves either the taking, over
of existing institutions or their extinction by competition.
5. Because, if it involves taking over any of the exist-
ing institutions, the risks of loss are too great and too
certain to warrant its consideration.
6. Because, if there are no such risks, there is no virtue
in the coveted State guarantee to depositors.
7. Because the State has quite enough to do to pay its
own way, and requires all its tax-levying power to do this
with.
8. Because the principal results expected to accrue
from the establishment of a State Bank can be proved to
be injurious to the best interests of the people, even if
such results did transpire.
As regards Nos. 1,2, 3, 7 and 8 the Telegraph
says the “evils” and the “risks” are “ad-
mitted,” and The Bulletin, on equally good
authority, says they aren’t; also, the Tele-
graph says the “ new relationship ” would be
“dangerous,” and The Bulletin says it
wouldn’t. Further, the Telegraph says the
“ State has quite enough to do to pay its own
way,” and The Bulletin replies that it has,
only this has nothing to do w'ith the case ; and
when the Telegraph says that the “ principal
results expected ” from a State Bank “ can be
proved to be injurious,” this paper asks why
the Telegraph doesn’t prove them so ? As
regards No. 5, which covers everything that
remains, the Telegraph says that there would
be “great” and “certain” loss if the State
took over any of the private banks. Now,
there could be no certain loss unless the bank
taken over hadn’t enough assets to cover its
liabilities. The Telegraph says, in effect, that
this is the state of all of them ! And after
declaring, almost in so many words, that all
the private banks are so rotten that they can’t
be taken over without loss, it demands that the
private bank system should continue just as it
is. Also, it heads this ghastly inference ‘ ‘ The
Evils of State Banking.”
A CALL is being made for an inquiry into the
cause of the increase in the number of fatal
accidents at the Broken Hill mines—l 9 last
year and 11 this year to the end of August. A
preliminary question has been asked in N.S. W.
Assembly. But any inquiry is bound to be
inconclusive. For there is no single over-
shadowing cause. Curiously, the mammoth
open-cuts, which at one time threatened to
become awful man-traps, are comparatively
free from accident. Nor are the victims
elsewhere mostly new hands—strangers to
the mines. Contract work, and especially
payment per set or foot, is no doubt re-
sponsible for a good deal, particularly since
the “cutting” of prices became general. The
most important fact probably is that the men
have nob yet accustomed themselves to the new
conditions. The precautions against accidents
are generally so complete that men forget that
they are engaged in a highly dangerous call-
ing, and when they had plenty of time and
there wasn’t any inducement to put in over-big
charges, or try short-cuts through the workings
underground, it didn’t matter so much. Now,
they do attempt these things, mostly in defiance
of some regulation that can be pointed to, or of
some instruction that can be conveniently re-
membered, and accidents naturally follow.
Asbestos towels which, when soiled, are
cleansed by throwing them into the fire are an
alleged new invention and patented. Yet Pliny,
writing his “Natural History” soon after the time
of Christ, describes identical towels.

Nat Goodwin’s comedy co., which has got
the Amurrikin accent even stronger in many
places than the departed Chinatowns, opened
through its nose at Sydney Lyceum on Saturday
night to a big house with the “Nominee,” also
with inuch dclat and a splendid view of the Hud-
son River in the background. Said “ Nominee ”
IS i * j American version of the “ Candidate,”
St vj here long ago by the gorgeously-dolled-up
Maur—the man who established a claim on
public gratitude by causing Christie Murray to be
run m by a policeman who entered him on the
charge-sheet as “imperfectly educated”—and the
Candidate” was the English version of some-
thmg else which was the original French version
of itself. All three are the story of a man who
wanted to get away from his solemn home and his
tempestuous mother-in-law, and found a chance
when a fatuous party nominated him to stand for
some place or other where a man of his principles
would be received with bricks, the party pro-
bably regarding him as a politician whose loss
wouldn t be felt. Thereupon he sends his
secretary to personate him while he goes on the
whoop, and they
miss each other
coming back.
The Nominee
arriving alone
can’t give a
coherent ac-
count of himself
to his infuriated
parent, and
doesn’t know
that he has
abjured his prin-
ciples and been
elected as a flam*
ing friend of the
people, and so
makes state-
ments which are
afterwards ex-
plained away
with great diffi-
culty.

The “No-
minee” is
broader farce
than the other
version. Good-
win departs
avowedly to get

on a tear ” with
a strange female,
while the British

out the female
and the drunk.
Further, Good-
win seems so
joyous in his
home that his
story about be-
ing a fatally-
bored man who
must depart in
search of liberty
loses a lot of its
force; when St.
Maur announced his resolve to flee from his
sad, starchy, stately mansion, where even a
card on a salver was brought in by eight
flunkeys, there seemed to be some reason in
his ambition, but seeing that the Nominee has
just got back from a razzle in the city, arriving
among the coals on a special engine at
his case doesn’t look such a very hard one.
* * * *
. Nat Goodwin, so far as Sydney has yet
sized him up, is a bright and capable comedian
with a quaint habit of seizing his nose with his
finger and, thumb in moments of deep thought.
He doesn’t look at all. like the man that any
serious-minded party would nominate for any-
thing except a poker-school and a cocktail, but
then the party didn’t see him before taking this
rash step. Miss Maxine Elliott, the beauty
actress of the co., proves to be a large, ripe
charmer about whose loveliness there is no manner
of doubt whatsoever, though her acting has no
especial points about it. Her sister, Miss
Gertrude Elliott, is a quieter and more restful
style of beauty, and does her part with a less
overwhelming mass of hair but, possibly, with
more effect. That old-time favorite, Miss Emelie
Melville, and Miss Estelle Mortimer, as the
mother-in-law, are the strongest female elements
in the co., and the rest of the male part of it is
ordinary with a respectable ordinariness which
calls for no special objurgation. The beauty
actress does her best work as She in the curtain-
raiser, a very clever little episode of two,
which fills in time while the Nicest People
are clamoring in late and behaving like savages
generally. In this piece also Nat Goodwin
runs up against a orisis and seizes his nose as one
who fears lest that feature will bolt for happier
scenes.

“A Mother of Three,” Miss Clo. Graves’s
comedy which opened the Brough season at
Sydney Cri., is, so far as The Bulletin knows,
the New Woman’s first public attempt at stage
humor. It is by no means a bad attempt either.
The Three are the daughters of an astronomer,
but as the old man departed for Peru to observe a
transit of Venus before they were born, and re-
mained there for 18 years, apparently, as after-
wards appears, without getting his hair cut, they
have never seen him. The Three have one ball-
dress among them, a lodger who never pays up,
and numerous lovers who all drop off because they
don’t believe in the Peruvian parent and regard
the family as more or less fishy. Therefore, when
the trio are engaged for the fifth time their mother
dresses up in the clothes which were left behind
by the boarder who didn’t pay ujj —she also shakes
that defaulter in the passage- and proceeds to
guarantee the respectability of the family by
being a father. The wild-haired professor comes
dreaming home from Peru just here, therefore the
girls, after a long deficiency of male pro-
genitor, have no mother, and no less than two
fathers —one who is convulsed with horrible
suspicions upstairs, and represents himself as the
new boarder, and one in spectacles who cooks the
dinner in the kitchen, and abstractedly knits a
stocking, and wants to conduct a visiting gorgon
upstairs and help her off with her things. There
is much violent collision between the two, and-the
new father defends herself with a poker against
a sea of troubles, and is scorned by the old father
on suspicion of being the person who has led her-
self astray from the path of rectitude, and is
ordered to produce herself or take the conse-
quences. Signs appear here and there that the
play is the work of an inexperienced hand ; there
are loose ends about it, and it drags a little in Act
11., and the new father has an occasional tendency
to warnder into hazardous by-paths about under-
clothing, but it is bright and fresh, and amusing

and novel. The novelty lies partly in the fact
that, in the ordinary man-comedy, men pre-
dominate, and all the really amusing parts are
written for them. Here, instead, there are four
heroines and no very prominent hero, and, in
the first act, the ladies of the co. have the stage
almost entirely to themselves. The result is
decidedly interesting.

Mr. and Mrs. Brough, as the old father
and the new one, are the wildly farcical elements
of the play, and both do it all the justice it can
expect. So do Titheradge and Miss Watt-
Tanner. Majeroni makes his first appearance
m a distinctly comedy-part as a long mili-

pretentious _ fqshion he is very successful. The
Three consist of the ever-welcome Miss Hardy
and the two new ladies, Misses Lilv Titheradge
and Winifred Austin, who both fill their characters
remarkably well. Miss Titheradge especially pro-
mises to be as clever as she is pretty, and should
maintain the credit of the family in years to come,
after her talented father has retired to dream
among his daffodils and orchids down in Ascot
Vale. Various other people --notably Miss Temple
as the orphan slavey with a chronic cold—fill their
various parts satisfactorily, and in a general way
things go smoothly and well.

Her Majesty’s is doing a week’s rest, but
on Saturday William Cosgrove appears as manager
of this gilded hall of misfortune, with “Fun on
the Bristol.” Cosgrove plays the widow, and the
probable feelings of the widow about being played
by Cosgrove will be expounded in next issue.
Anyhow, Her Majesty’s is a big handicap on a
play which is only an airy trifle in two acts. A
theatre of its bulk seems intended by nature for
the eight-act drama with tanks and elephants in it.

“ A Trip to Chinatown” booms as usual

twice on Saturday. Most of the songs continue

cannot wither. The emphatic and uninterrupted

Frank Cates has been described as an
actor on many occasions in many newspapers
whereas he was never more than a member of the
theatrical profession The tall, slim, somewhat
woodeny walking gentleman lacked the heart that
makes an artist. He didn’t act anythin? hevom-l
Frank Cates. In the (Shakespearian sense of the
phrase this was a player who could not “ force a
passion to his own conceit,” seeing that his own
conceit or self-consciousness was so much stronger
than his best attempts at stage passion. Never-
theless, Cates averaged a good salary for manv
years, and should have been able, with only a little
regard to economy, to pay his way to the last He
possessed a lucrative property in his clean-cut
features, young figure, and gentlemanly, though
not graceful, deportment, for managers can-
not easily procure practised stage - walkers
who have the same air of “good breeding”
that Frank Cates presented. The poor fellow
got a good deal of financial nourishment one
way and another, from his few drops of
blue blood. He was aggressively Hinglish, a
contemptuous belittler of, and an inveterate blas-
phemer at everything Australian. The scores
and hundreds of women who in days of yore wor-
shipped I rank Cates from the auditorium, and
thought of him as a darling man whom they would
like to befriend will be sorry to hear that he was
buried by the Yic, Distressed Actors’Fund which
excellent charity had been helping to support his
motherless family for some time before his death.
Many former female admirers have here an oppor-
tunity to pay a tribute of respect to their own
emotions, and the Distressed Actors’ Fund will be
glad to receive subscriptions, no doubt.

A special article on shipping in S.M.
Herald one day last week reported “ a forest of
masts at Circular Quay. On that morning there
were in that water four steamers and one sailing-
ship. Two steamers had four masts each, one had
three, and one had two, while the ship had three
—l6 masts in the “forest.”
Marcus Clarke’s “Australian Tales,”lately
published by his widow, are also being issued in
the form of supplements for country newspapers.
Push editors, of literary disposition, who want a
bright supplement with local color in it, are ad-
vised to drop a line to Mrs. Marcus Clarke, Mel-
bourne.
The cable reports that all foreign employes
have been dismissed from Krupp's cannon foundry
at Essen, Rhenish Prussia. Foreigners, in this
connection, means Britishers chiefly, if not entirely.
Ihe hottest work at Krupp’s used to be done, it
was said of old, by Britishers who contracted to
live on the premises and were allowed little or no
liberty to roam.
The N.S.W. public are disappointed in not
having ventilated the Withers-Burns-John Davies-
Hornsby roads scandal. Wise counsellors advised
arbitration, and the matter was referred to G. R.
Bibbs, who heard evidence in camera, and decided
against Withers. Part of the arbitrator’s verdict
appears to be that there really should never have
]U er ? a 2 y .Uornsby Estate roads scandal at all,
the land given for the purpose of making the roads
through the private domain in question having, at
the time, been worth more than the Treasury-
money laid out on them.

winch introduced Protection into Victorian poli-

■Heales Ministry in 1861, the first Australian
Bemocratic Cabinet; but a visit to England, a
C.8.-ship and a subsequent K.C.M.G.-ship “took
all the nonsense out of him. It was, indeed, a
sight to see the Brobdignagian Verdon at a Govt,
House function “ with ’em all on.”
