The Wild-Cat Column .
Considering the large number of life-assur-
ance offices in Australia when compared with population,
and the cutthroat competition which ensues, and the fact
that this competition keeps up expenses to a disastrous
point, and that, despite all efforts, several offices have
shown a gradually declining business for some years past
—considering all these things, there is urgent heed for a
process of amalgamation among the smaller concerns
which will reduce six offices into three, or two, or even
one. There is no difficulty worth speaking of about the
mere mechanical process of amalgamation. If two
mutual societies agreed to unite all they need to do is to
decide which name should be retained and which should
be dropped, to hump their books and papers into the
more commodious office of the two, unite the two staffs
of agents, sack a large amount of needless clerk, secre-
tary, director, &c., and go ahead. The funds of the
smaller concern would be kept in a separate account and
separately invested until all its customers had died off,
but no new policies would be issued in its name. All the
combined new business of both offices would be done in
the name of the other society—the one whose name was
to be preserved. In the course of years, when the cus-
tomers of the smaller office had all dropped off, the amal-
gamation would complete itself, and the business which
now requires two expensive sets of offices, managers,
clerks, directors, auditors, advertisements, medical
advisers, &c., &c., would be conducted equally well with
only one, and much expense which now runs away with
the policy-holders’ profits would be saved.
The only real difficulty in the way is that life
insurance offices exist largely to find billets and salaries
for managers, directors, and the rest. The policy-holders
3an’t- easily move in the matter; they are many and
widely scattered, and no one of them has enough interest
to make it worth while setting the matter in motion.
And if it was set in motion the managers and directors
and others, who live by keeping superfluous offices
afloat, would. probably gather up proxies enough
—from the kind of policy-holder who signs proxies
when he is asked to do so without enquiring
how. they are to be used—to vote down any such
proposal. Nevertheless, amalgamation would do a lob of
good in this over-insured country by reducing working
exs. to a reasonable figure. It is mainly too many sets of
offices, and offices’ staffs, and managers, and so on, which
make the smaller concerns “blew” about a quarter of
their premiums in salaries and oilier charges, and, if this
outlay could be reduced to a reasonable figure, it would
be an immense saving to the policy-holders. There is no
real reason why a second A.M.P. society, with all the
rigor and prestige of the present one, shouldn’t be created
in Australia by a union of sundry smaller offices which
maintain their separate existence in these depressed times
without much benefit to anybody.

* * » »
The present chances seem to be thatM’Oracken’s
Brewery Co.—which is asking the English holders of
£500,000 worth of debentures to reduce interest from 4J
to S per cent. —won’t get the concession it is looking for.
The concern lost £22,900 last year, and as the proposed
reduction would only save £7500 a year, it is obvious that
it wouldn’t do much good unless thing's improved. Pro-
bably all it would do would be to keep the tiling suffi-
ciently alive to go on depreciating so that the debenture-
holders’ security, which is none too good at present,
would have time to grow a lot worse.
* * * *
In Maoriland, a compulsory Civil Service in-
surance scheme has lately been started which ought to be
a vast improvement on the Superannuation-Fund swindle.
Every public servant appointed after the passage of the
Act has to insure in the State Life Insurance Department
on a plan which provides that, if he should die before 60—
the statutory age of retirement—his heirs receive the
amount of the policy with profits, and, if he should sur-
vive that age, he receives an annuity for the remainder of
his life. If he retires or is retrenched before the age of
60 he can either continue his policy or get a liberal
surrender value, as he pleases. The policy is abso-
lutely protected against all creditors, and, so long as
the officer is _in the service, it cannot be pawned,
mortgaged, assigned, or diverted from its purpose in any
way whatever. The system covers a lot of things. It
provides a hard-and-fast contract which no political influ-
ence can modify. The insured has—as he should have —
the best security which can be obtained ; when the State
compels any person to insure it is morally bound to give
him its own security for payment of ins policy. The
superannuated public servant can’t be left penniless, how-
ever much he may try to reduce himself to that condi-
tion. The country is saved a larg'e outlay for pensions.
The system doesn’t, like the N.S.W. Superannuation
swindle, leave the family wholly unprovided for in case of
premature death. Also, it gives cheap as well as safe
insurance; the Treasury collects the premiums by deduc-
tion from salaries, and all the heavy expenses for commis-
sion, &c., are saved. This is the sort of scheme which
Premier Reid should have initiated when the system of
compulsory contribution to the Superannuation Fund was
abolished, but then N.S.W. has no State-guaranteed Life
Office, and G.H.R. lacked the courage to create one.
By the way, when the system of compulsory
subscription to the N.S.W. Superannuation Fund was
abolished, it was announced that all new appointees to the
Civil Service must insure their lives in some office approved
by the Commissioners, or somebody. Has anyone heard
since then what offices have been approved of, or has
nothing been done in the matter? It would be interesting
to know what kind of offices are approved in this connec-
tion.
* * * *
Considering all the loose vociferation there has
been of late about the way N.S.W. was scooping the Wes-
tralian trade from Victoria through the blessings of Free-
trade, and how Sydney was becoming the distributing
centre of Australia, some recent figures are interesting.
In 1895 the Vic. exports of merchandise only (not coin) to
W.A. were £593,000, or £153,000 more than in ’94. The
same year N.S.W. exports to W.A. were £143,000, or only
£60,000 more than in ’94. In view of a recent statement
which Max Hirsch permitted somebody to make in his
paper, that for one case of boots which went to W.A.
from Melbourne ten went from Sydney, it is further in-
teresting to note that Vic. boot - exports were £6243,
against £5441 from N.S.W.
* * » *
S.A. railways yielded £3 4s. per cent, on capital
last year against £3 2s. 4d. the year previous.
* * * *
For the year ended 31st March, 1896, there
passed through the Maoriland Public Trust Office £614,000,
and the working expenses of the office, including land-tax
and losses on securities, were a little under £15,000.
When private trustees or private trust companies can
show absolute security combined with an expense-rate of
less than 21 per cent., then The Bulletin' will stop
arguing in favor of the Public Trust system.

The big Sydney brewery men are hard at work
proving that the lager-bier brewery must fail, because so

* * * *
The haughty gas-man has been humbled in
Launceston, Tas., which city now claims to have the best
system of municipally-owned electric-lighting in this
hemisphere. In addition to the street lighting, in less
than five months upwards of 5000 private lights have
been applied for, and, as a consequence, the local gas co.
is the most subservient and polite institution ever heard
of. Canvassers for the erstwhile monopoly are instructed
to stick at nothing in order to place gas cooking-stoves
with householders, and far-seeing landladies are daily ex-
pecting their former take-away-your-meter autocrat to
offer a month’s free tucker with a gas-cooker.
And the haughty gas-man of Sydney will also
be tamer when the City Corporation’s electric-lighting
scheme (now practically through Parliament) gets to work.

were “squared.”

Those in the know estimate that not more than

manded by the new Insolvency Act. Three of the tribe,
who were very prominent until recently, have assigned or
filed their own schedules during the last few weeks, and
the story goes that it has taken another eminent Vie.
firm quite a year to discover the irregularities of one of
its own partners.

calls on 5000 shares, and intend to see it out.

The Exploration Go. has at last started to mine
in N.S.W. It is handling Taylor’s Reward and adjoining
leases, at Bueca Creek. Money paid, £42,000 cash and
.50,000 paid shares in a £200,000 co. Mine examined by
several well-known experts, who, it is said, all declared
over £60,000 gold to be in sight. Which is pretty tall.
However, even if the gold is not in sight, it is ‘to be
hoped, in the interests of N.S.W., that it will come out of
the mine.
* * * *
That in the working of the Fort Bourke mine at
Cobar, N.S.W., about 50% of the gold was left in the tail-
ings, has been demonstrated by the cyanide plant now
working them. Stated that the last 14 days’ run returned
2430 z. The co. which owned the mine never had a mill of
its own, paid 15s. a ton for crushing many thousands of
tons for a 50% extraction, and sold what seems to be the best
claim on the belt for £lO,OOO. None the less, in selling
the Fort Bourke, the owners did the best thing for the
district. They had long shown their inability to do any-
thing better with it themselves. The sale has introduced
cyanide, brought lots of capital into the district, and,
with the wonderful development at the Peak, has made
things hum throughout the camp.

As here foreshadowed, the Sydney open stock

The Bulletin was in error last week in stating
that the promoters of the Welcome mine (N.S.W.) were
asking fully-paid shares for that property. The shares
allotted to the promoters are only partially-paid. The
show was tipped only five times last Sunday, and if the
mine can stand that it is all right.

Inverell, N.S.W. : “ Four barrow-loads of wash
from Boggy. Camp yielded 1089 diamonds of various sizes.”
A little too indefinite. If the stones were of any size, and
the barrows moderately small, there must have been
about half as much gem as wash. 272(4 diamonds to the
barrow is big business. At £1 per stone, Boggy could
reasonably lay claim to being a good low-grade diamond
camp.

The Overflow Co., working a gold, silver, and
copper show in the far west of N.S.W., are creatin'-’ new
shares to be offered to present shareholders to provide a
solid working capital to put the mine in good shape. Tub
Bulletin is told that no attempt at booming has been
made, that the mine will have to prove its own intrinsic
value, and the owners are prepared to put up their own
cash to thoroughly prove the lodes. Also, “in apprecia-
tion of the way things are conducted” John Howell has
joined the board.

As soon as Col. Cecil Morgan had departed, the
Buchan (Vic.) silver-mine is pronounced a duffer. The
directors “are lost in astonishment,” and all sorts of alle-
gations are being thrown around—as if the working of a
duffer mine was the most extraordinary thing in the
world. The writer is forcibly reminded of the urgency of
a Duffer Mines Commission, having one simple business—■
to furnish the world at large with a list of the rank duffers
working in Australia, especially Westralia.

The prominent firm of Melb. stockbrokers who
heavily underwrote a much-boomed and high-sounding
Vic. gold venture, the other day, are reported to be

scrip being left on their hands.
* * * *
What seems a momentous discovery has been
made at Woods Point (Vic.) : three gold-bearing reefs all
within 100yds., traced for over two miles. The eastern
reef shows 7ft. of quartz, carrying gold from wall to wall,
and giving splendid prospects. The middle reef is 4ft.
wide, and also has good gold, and the western reef is of
dyke formation, with coarse gold in solid stone.
* * * *
That famous parcel of 400 oz. Westralian gold
which proved to be 3900 z. base metal and only lOoz. of
the real article is not, it now appears, a solitary instance,
nor is it even the worst instance. Various other parcels
have proved to be adulterated, the best sample being. 90
per cent, bogus, while the worst ones were 99 and a fraction
per cent, iron and brass, and less than one per cent,
gold. This last fetched ninepence per ounce. As these
great collections of iron filings are exported as the real
metal they are included in the “gold” returns of the
Great Bogus Land, and must help to swell them con-
siderably.
One would not, of course, expect chemically-
pure gold from a mine worked by Cherubim and managed
by Seraphim. Virgin gold of very high quality has been
found in Victoria—so high as £4 4s. 3d. per ounce in value
—but chemieally-pure gold is worth £4 ss. an ounce. Bal-
larat gold might fetch £4 3s. 6d. an ounce, Bendigo about
the same. Some of the Gippsland product might be worth
£1 less. This only means that gold invariably contains
some alloy. The adulteration is always there, though
generally its quantity is of little consequence. But what
about “gold” that goes ninepence an ounce? How ever
did such “gold” come to be forwarded from Westralia
to Melbourne to be coined ? The fact that it has
been done in case after case adds a new terror to mining
speculation in the West. Was it merely a ruse to rig the
market, so that those in the know could unload
their shares ? Even The Bulletin is inclined to
doubt that so diabolical a dodge has been practised even
in Westralia. There are some undoubtedly curious dis-
turbing influences in Westralian mining. Several mines
produce very dirty gold that fetches from £2lss. to £3 ss.
per ounce. This low quality seriously interferes with
profits. The reason for the poverty of the gold is only
left to conjecture. The probability is that some mines
are attempting to deal with low-grade pyritic and other
such ores by melting instead of smelting them. The dif-
ference may not look much in print to the casual reader,
but it means a lot in fact.
# * * *
H. L. Goodman, chairman of the Westralian
Gold Districts Trading Syndicate, is reported as hav-
ing told his shareholders at a London meeting that
they might expect to do good business, as the
population of W.A. was now increasing at the rate
of a quarter-of-a-million a year. The syndicate had
secured many monopolies, most of which he kept secret,
but whispered gently that the boss one was the agency of
Bass’s beer ! Their name had so spread over the country
that “ prospectors had offered them mines of surpassing
richness for flotation, not having the capital to work them
themselves ! ” Then Mr. Goodman proceeded to enumer-
ate the merits of the Ore Automatic process as a money-
making machine. “It will cost only a few pounds to
erect, and the ore can be reduced to the finest powder at a
cost (to us) of sixpence a ton. Just you realise this fact.
Even if we charge a royalty of only ss. a ton, we will
save every co. using it 10s. to 14s. a ton, and will
make an enormous revenue ourselves. As soon as I
heard of this remarkaDle process, I hastened to
acquire a personal interest in it for my own
benefit, and, in the second place, to secure for this
corporation the first claim on the Western Australian
rights.” (In the second place is good.) “ There are now
over 600 companies working mines in W.A., so if we
charge an initial fee of £lOOO each for the use of this pro-
cess, that will mean £600,000. Add to this a royalty of
ss. per ton, and you can fairly estimate the value of this
concession. Now, what did we pay for these rights?
Expert judges have estimated that we paid from a
quarter of a million to a million of money. I .am pleased
to say we have secured the rights for'l2o,ooo ordinary
shares, 600 deferred shares, and only £30,000 cash.” The
Bulletin seriously wants to know if there is any place in
the whole world outside of London where a man can talk
like that without being killed on sight ? His speech was
“ cheered at frequent intervals ! ” And yet we are fond
of the good old mother country. Perhaps it is our mother
country’s milk we are so fond of.

* * * *
Of the General Gordon, W.A., a well-known
mining man writes “ Too much has been made of this
show. Take my word it is not nearly so rich as the public
have been led to think. There are some wonderfully good
stringers in the formation, but the bulk only samples
about an oz. to the ton, and under existing conditions
there are no divs. in that. We look to the eastern papers
to furnish mining humor. Lately we had it that ‘the
General Gordon would start crushing in Sept.; now, early
in October. Possibly it will start before the end of
the year; probably not. The mine is surrounded by
some very wild ‘ cats.’ The boom was well worked
up, and under the impression that the rapid share-
premiums of the Gordon would be repeated, they
were rushed, and most of these ‘ cats ’ were largely over-
applied. Knowing their intrinsic worthlessness, the local
subscribers were out as soon as the market began to drag.
Now the cats are all at a discount. By the way, Ex-
tended General Gordons, said to have been over-applied
for to the extent of 40,000, are selling at 2s. 2d.—a drop of
3s. on the amount paid—and calls are a certainty.”
* * * *
A movement was started in Coolgardie recently
to disprove the assertions made by the Age and Sydney
D.T. special commissioner re the "salting of W.A. "mines
and the unanimity in lying practised by a big crowd of
mining-experts to. take down the British public. Great
faith was at first placed on what the Argus man was
going to say, but it is now found that, in the main, he
has confirmed the other. The move to knock out “ Our
Systematic Slanderers” has collapsed. It has been de-
cided to treat the allegations with (good old wheeze !)
“silent contempt.” In the same issue of a W.A. paper
which shrieks over this business appears a speech made in
London by Thompson, chairman of the Coolgardie
Chamber of Mines, in which he defends the Coolgardie
mining-experts and blames the London promoters. The
Coolgardie expert had to submit to great temptations,
and the anxiety of the promoter for “good reports” was
frequently expressed. Thompson gave one instance of
what has often happened. He went under instructions to
examine a mine. The owners had an idea, after he had
sampled, that he was not going to advise purchase. They
started to pu up. He replied, “I am afraid not.” They
offered £IOOJ if he would. His reply was that he did not
make money that way. They then offered £2OOO, and,
finally, £3OOO. The purchase price was £7OOO, and, as he
still declined to recommend it, they asked what he really
thought the show was worth. He replied : “No more in
hundreds than you are asking thousands.” “Then,” said
they, “you can take it at"£7oo.” The writer wonders
what percentage of the ex-land-bank men, land-agents,
and broken-down brokers (now fully-fledged W.A. mining
expertsl would refuse to accept £3OOO for recommending
a mine ?
* * * *
Coolgardie and Kalgoorlie papers have started
to tell the cold-blooded truth of the mines in each other’s
districts. “ When newspapers fall out,” &c. Says a Cool-
gardie special who had been over to the Kalgoorlie races :
“Is it not strange, after all the strikes which the local
boomsters have telegraphed far and wide, that there are
only three mines with batteries giving regular returns ?
What are they doing with the innumerable Golcondas ?
If the Hannan’s belt, as it is fondly called, is four miles
wide and fourteen miles long, how is it that at the end of
two years there are not at least 30 mines crushing ? As a
matter of fact, there is just as great an average of duffers
at Kalgoorlie as anywhere else on the fields.” Now, the
much-abused present writer never said of W.A. anything
worse than this or more truthful.

tons.” As each hundred tons will be worth 14 mil-

* * * *
One big Westralian dry-crushing plant is a
monument of bad British engineering. Breaking a butter-
fly on a wheel is a light and airy effort to cracking clay in
the crushers. The gold volatilizes and escapes through
the side doors. The dust beats St. Kilda-road in a nor-
therly wind, and they keep the very oil-engines in glass-
cases to prevent them stopping.
* * * *
All the British mine-managers at Kalgoorlie
have neat, clean camps, built of brick because they couldn’t
get marble. Each has a mahogany bath-room, and a
tennis-court, and a drorin’-room, and an Erard piano.
They never drink water at 3d. a gallon when champagne
can be got at 255. a bottle. And, until their mines fall
into Australian hands, the British shareholders will get
nothing except reports, typewritten and in duplicate.

Phoenix Golden Pile, Gym pie, 161 tons for
10770 z. But Gympie is a very erratic field.
The Bulletin thinks the time has arrived when
it should tip something. A dozen times a week the writer
is tempted to become a tipster. His t'p is not advice to
buy shares at about 3s. a dozen that are not worth as
many farthings ; it is to locate the new London boom in
Australian mines. If there is enough boom left in the
good old mother land, after handling hundreds of Westra-
lian “cats,” the new boom will be in the Northern Terri-
tory. Already several W.A. exploration companies are
looking to that part of Australia so as to divert attention
from the cats of W.A. Old returns from Pine Creek
are being resurrected, and the cry will be “ abundance of
water and timber, and every prospect of working cheap
labor in a tropical country.” The great W.A. boomsters
must do their utmost to"change the locale. Bull’s faith
and purse are unlimited. Yes, Tue Bulletin 1 says to all
who want to join the new army of boomers, “ Go North --'
a long way North, young man—and stand not on the
order of your going, but go at once and the chances are
that if you are an accomplished liar you will make
money.”

Said that £50,000 has been offered and refused
for Walter Giffen's mine in S.A. 100 to lit is not true.
News comes that China is to have a go at
systematising her currency. At present, its meejum of
exchange bears the same resemblance to a civilised money-
system as a marine-store does to an observatory. It har-
bors no less than six “ dollars,” most of which are in a
deplorable state of mutilation, owing to the practice oi
“ shrof-tnarking.” These dollars are the Japanese Trade
(420), Japanese yen (416), Hongkong (416), British (416),
Mexican (416£), Carolus (414)—the figures in brackets
being the nominal weight in grains. “ Shrof-marking”
has some resemblance (only more so) to the deceased
custom which made bank-notes suffer endorsement from
each successive holder. In China, every merchant who
gets hold of a dollar punches his chop or shrof on its un-
fortunate face, with a brass stamp and a hammer. A dollar
that has been the rounds looks like a debauched lottery-
ticket. These mangled dollars are made to have some
remote and mystic relation to taels, and also to shoes
(ingots) of silver. What is a tael? Well, there is the
Hailuvan tael, the Amoy tael, the Chefoo tael, the Wuhu
tael, the Foochow tael—and so on ad infinitum. Further,
in many districts custom makes a tael mean a tael plus a
percentage. In other districts there is a discount just as
unreasonable. The cash or li of the common people are
nominally 1000 to the tael, or one of the taels, but the
market decides in favor of 1500 to the tael, or 1779 to the
tael—or some other reasonable proportion—week by week.
China’s currency system forms a curious commentary on
Herbert Spencer’s contention that governments should
not interfere in the currency, and that if they had not in-
terfered a world’s currency would have been evolved by
this time. In China the government has not interfered
with the currency, and, in consequence, it is a Chinese
puzzle.

COOLATA
CAMEO
“ Gaol-warders should have shorter hours,’’
says Prendergast, Victorian M.L.A.; “as it is,
they have no time to see their wives in daylight.”
This latter hardship wants a deal of thinking over.
There are so many wives who look so much better
by candle-light that it is difficult to be quite sure
whether the arrangement is cruelty or not. How
many homes which might otherwise have been
happy have been burst up through the parties
seeing each other by daylight, when the old man’s
bald patch looks large and bare and glabrous, and
the old woman’s crow’s-feet enlarge so that it
looks as if a condor had been walking on her face ?
Besides, a gaol-warder—especially if he has any-
thing to do with the flogging and hanging depart-
ment —ought only to be seen under the softening
influences of moonlight.
* * * *
One morning last week the death of a
member of a family in very moderate circum-
stances was advertised in the Sydney daily papers,
yet by 10 a.m. the friends of deceased had received
a dozen samples of memorial-cards, seven under-
takers had submitted quotations, two well-known
town drapery-firms had forwarded prices for
mourning -requisites, three monumental-masons
had left business-cards, and four florists handed
in their price-lists ; also cabmen kept calling all
day asking if their services were likely to be
needed at the funeral. To crown all, a caterer
offered to provide a post-funeral gorge at so much
a head, and a town hotelkeeper—anticipating a
possible wake—sent his bottle-department list.
Miss WINIFRED AUSTIN (Brough Comedy Co.)
It nas always been drummed into N.S.W.
State-school teachers that they are to take notice
of nothing re the schools that may appear in the
papers, but are to await advice on the matter from
the Education Department. Many a teacher has
been disciplined for closing when a holiday has
been gazetted for the district, and no notice has
been forthcoming from the authorities. On such
occasions it was vain for the poor teacher to plead
that it was a public holiday, and that all local
Govt, offices were closed. He was always told,
“You should have waited for the Minister’s
authority,” and was cautioned “not to take notice
of anything in the papers. So the other day
when the retrenchment-notices were published m
the dailies, the dutiful teachers, not having re
ceived official intimation of the reduction of their
salary, and remembering the oft-repeated caution,
promptly sent in their abstracts on their original
salary-basis—unretrenched—and anxiously awaited
results. P.S. —They got there just the same.

the Jewess who bikes takes to knickers without
hesitation, and whilst her Christian sisters
coquette with bloomers, divided skirts, and other
clumsy contrivances, the daughter of Israel
plunges into breeches, and goes cheerfully out
and about on her bike knickered and unashamed.
Why is this thus? —C.M.G.

The Echo Home, Sydney’s seaside resort
for repentant inebriates, has been re christened
Rest Haven. Shakespeare never made a greater
mistake than when he asked a question which im-
plies that there is nothing in a name. There may
be an attraction of love, a repulsion of hate, a
whisper of sympathy, or a snuffle of Pecksniffism
in a name. “ Rest Haven ! ”

The primest joke in all Bananaland was
the recent futile burgling of Bowen Railway-
station. So successfully had the Bowenites
boomed their little line that certain would-be
thieves actually imagined there were certain traffic
moneys in existence that could be stolen. The
revelation of the impossibility of the aforesaid
feat has set Bowen prospects back a generation.
While petty municipalities shut
their little parks at evening as preserves
of Virtue, the vast Melb. Govt. House
domain is ever open. Moral in that, too.

The latter reciprocate to the extent that
they marry doctors and lawyers gratis.

A Teacher : “Apropos seating ac-
commodation for shop assistants, provided
for in a Bill now before N.S.W. Parlia-
ment. Some Democratic member should
endeavor to insert a clause in the Bill with
a view to securing a similar concession for
State school-teachers. There is no depart-
mental regulation which distinctly forbids
a teacher being seated while giving a lesson
—indeed, in some few (favored) schools
specially-constructed “thrones” are pro-
vided for the teacher’s use—but in the
majority of schools the harassed teacher is
compelled to stand and is reported if found
seated any time during school hours.”
The life-task set himself by the average

lOOOdols.—will buy 30 acres of rice land, which
will yield him lOOdol. a year-and a Chinaman

* * * *
In matters of self-reliance the Caucasian
can learn valuable lessons from the Mongol
Ching-do possessed a herd of 400 cattle in the
Lower Burdekin, the most tick-infested spot in
Queensland. While Ching-do’s European neigh-
bors were clamoring for Govt, aid, that pig-tailed
heathen built a dip, bought oil, dipped his cattle,
and preserved his whole herd intact and healthy
while his neighbors’ stock died in hundreds. When
State aid did arrive the Europeans’ cattle were
non-existent, and Ching-do sat down with a placid
smile. He hadn’t lost a single beast.

The rq. nw p«« P ntinllt7 a rloULoi...!-;,,,-.
. , L“ ow lf ? essentially a deliberative
ammal ; suicide is a matter over which he
scorns to hurry himself. The other day, near Oh.
Towers, a Celestial was noticed cutting two long
forked sticks which he subsequently planted in the
ground a few feet apart, placing then a horizontal
between them. A week afterwards he mounted
the horizontal with a rope round his neck, which
he carefully secured to a branch overhead, and
then sprang gaily off his perch into perdition.
No European is capable of such calm, premedi-
tated, long-drawn-out self-destruction.

* » * •
At Maitland (N.S.W.) hospital, the other
day, a young unmarried woman was operated on
for tumour without a consultation of the surgical
staff. She had been for some months under the
immediate observation of a local doctor, who
diagnosed ovarian tumour despite the suspicions
of the matron and the suggestive previous history
of the patient. The woman proved to be preg-
nant and the child died. The doctor was acquitted
by a coroner’s jury of all _ negligence, but was
mildly censured for not holding a consultation.

Private-enquiry agents and small, petti-
fogging solicitors drive a lucrative co-operative
trade in Melbourne forma pauperis divorce-cases.
The former collects the evidence, and the legal
partner shoves the case through the court. Some-
times the dividend is big, sometimes it isn’t. In
any case, it is all “bunce.”

It was at a committee
meeting of a Maternity Hos-
pital in one of the Austra-
lias, and before a score or so
of intensely respectable mid-
dle-aged sassiety women was
arraigned a girl who desired
to avail herself of the insti-
tution’s aid. “ Young wo-
man,” remarked the chair-
woman, a near connection of
the local Anglican bishop, in
severe accents, “this appli-
cation says you are married.
Are you sure that is the
case?” “Of course I am,”
responded the applicant ;

grinning in her sleeve.
A great crowd of the Nicest People rolled
up at the wedding of Scot Inglis and Miss Ida
Hamilton in a Sydney church last week, with
the result that the tabernacle was strewn
with back hair, squashed bonnets, frills, hair-
pins, and whalebone. The reserved seats
were rushed, and all the attempted regula-
tions trodden underfoot. About 600 people stood
up on the book-boards and held on to each other’s
hair. One stout lady, who was too ponderous for
such balancing feats, remained on the floor and
saw nothing, and an agile female behind put one
foot on her bonnet to maintain her balance. Then
the stout lady indignantly got from under and the
agile one fell heavijy. There was so little room
for the happy pair that during the ceremony two
Nice People were standing on the bride’s train at
once. The church was a heaving mass of jabber
and suffocation. In the gallery the people who
came very early got front seats and leaned on the
rail, and the people who came late feather-bedded
themselves up against these early people and
squashed them fiat. Everybody was breathing in
somebody else’s ear, or resting his tongue on the
top of somebody’s hat while he gasped. The
communion-table had more people on it than
it could hold, and every time an anguished
female fell off she fetched some of the next
woman’s clothes with her, till it began to
be an open question whether part of the
crowd wouldn’t go home undressed. When
the show was over and the mob started out,
many small women were taken off their feet
and didn’t find them again for yards, and the
shoes of some of them dropped off while they
were suspended, and there wasn’t one untrodden-
on corn in the whole assemblage—the only safe
plan was to wear rational costume and come out
upside-down, and no one thought of that.
Altogether the Nicest People had a real good

loose character with their boots unlaced, ran after
the vehicles in a cloud of dust for no reason in

The charity outrage on “The Parvenu,”
at baronet Clarke’s big Melb. house, was a
thoroughly practical affair. A first performance
drew a crowd of 900 or so—as many as the ball-
room would hold —at 7s. 6d. per head, and the show
was repeated later in the week at the popular
price of three “ bob. ” The Clarke dancing saloon,
fitted with a deep stage, elegantly framed, makes
a very good theatre. Cliveden concert-room,
despite its classical air, is less imposing than the
“old English” hall, with its ancestral armour,
stags’ antlers, open fireplaces, and what not.
Apropos of statuary, the Australian small boy
puts his own practical construction on undressed
marble “figgers.” A guest questioned the scion
of one of the “ stately homes” of Melb. concerning
a nude in the family collection. “ It’s Aunt J. !”
was the prompt reply, to the horror of his maligned
relative, a well-nourished spinster of conspicuous
modesty.

man won’t be able to pay.” “Well, then,” said

The Westralian metropolitan dailies are
sparing no pains to persuade the people into a
taste for tinned dog, roupy fowls, and unseason-
able fish, in preference to tuberculous and
cancerous beef. A large section of the
community does not, however, appre-
ciate the grimness of the situa-
tion, and declares that cancer
has been present for the last
40 years in the province’s ox-
meat. Also it remarks that,
having dined for 40 years off
tuberculous beef, it has got
used to tubercles, and is
willing to dine off similar
disease and corruption for
the next 40 years. Likewise,
this fine, old-fashioned section
explains that though fairly
extensive tuberculosis may
cut off the bullock in his
prime, and malignant disease
may eat its way into all his
tissues, and pleuro-pneu-
monia the latest trouble
among W.A. cattle—may carry them off in vast
numbers, the community is still willing to eat
tuberculosis, cancer, pleuro, and all other diseases
so long as it can have bullock, which is the flesh
beloved of its soul.

Apostle of Nakedness M‘Donald has his
equivalent in a N.S.W. parson hailing from
the same dour land as the Nudity Pro-
fessor. It is the parson’s vogue 'to move
around indoors (when there are no visitors,
of course) in a solitary rag only. When a new
servant-girl once gave vent to a protesting “Oh !”
as she met the apparition on the stairs, he merely
observed, in a fatherly and admonitory way:
“ Ma gairl, ‘tae the pure a’ things are pure!”’
There should be a guid auld dram-drinking time,
wi’ profitable discoorse on the Higher Morals, if
the twa Naked Apostles ever foregather.

A lively alleged breach-of-trust and undue-
influence case, the details of which will flurry
Melbourne and Geelong society much, should be
coming on soon in Vie. Supreme Court. It will
interest most people who have heard of certain
events of the old convict-days, or who hold strong
opinions about a Melb. incident of the maritime
strike. A claim for damages for illegal detention
of the will of a man who is not yet dead will form
one item in the programme.
» * * *
Melb. Argus has been trying to excite a
correspondence on the probably bogus, and utterly
stupid, subject of a young man who admires a
young woman, and not being able to get a formal
introduction to her, would like to know what to
do in the matter. Letters to the editor, pro-
fessedly written by blushing spinsters, advised
the young man to strike up an acquaintance, with
a brother, cousin, or platonic friend of the
divinity, and get at her that way. Other in-
genuous suggestions were in favor of a gallant
encounter in the street, the young man to touch
his Heart’s Desire gently on the elbow, saying,
“ ’Souse me ! ” etc. One writer thought if the
maiden were a cyclist it would be well for her
adorer to buy a bike, and follow her everywhere
until she fell off and needed picking up by the
ardent stranger, at whom she would straightway
wink the other eye of encouragement. Then some
aged matrons weighed in with the advice that
poor Romeo should write a “ manly, straight-
forward ” letter, explaining the state of his feelings
as far as he could judge them up to date. The
Argus correspondence was very tiring. Any
young man who means business with a strange

A document issued by the Sydney Tree-
masons, returning thanks for donations supplied
at their annual ball, is interesting evidence as to
the fine, reliable thirst of the Order. Eighteen
donations are specified, and of these eleven are
whisky, six are ale, and the other one is gin. In
addition to these, five brewers and a wine mer-
chant sent donations, presumably more liquor.
There is very little to be known about fine
artistic drinking that the Masons don’t know. ’
AUTUMN LEAVES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The leaves of Autumn, shrinking
Brown and faded on the trees,
Start a thoughtful fellow thinking
Of the many gilds he sees
Who did spread a careless mesh
Whilst their charms were young and fresh ;
Who frightened birds by winking,
Or by flashing too much flesh.
They must muse o’er chances missed,
They must dream of kisses kissed
When the Springtime sun was sinking,
And the busy buzzing bees
Flying blindly with the breeze
Told, in never-ceasing humming,
Of the ardent summer coming,
And warned them to remember how
A woman’s beauty flees.
Ah ! the cynic rather grieves
For those withered Autumn leaves.
I found Belinda weeping
In the Austral month of May.
Bell sowed, and she is reaping
Crops of sorrow, lack-a-day !
Once her form was plump and green
Which is now so dry and lean,
And waking, dozing, sleeping,
She was happy and serene.
Did she think of vanished hours,
Of my true-love gifts of flowers
When my verdant heart was leaping
For the word she wouldn’t say?
Well, I coughed and went away.
In the time of adoration
I’d have offered consolation,
But I viewed that spinster weeping
With a comical dismay.
Tears dropped gently on my sleeve
As I took an Autumn leave.
Melb.
E.F.

Time deals gently with the woman who
uses “Babeskin” soap. It softly removes the
tell-tale lines of the years, and renews the delicate
bloom of youth. One shilling packages of two
cakes. Washington H. Soul & Co., Pitt-street
and Parramatta.*
“The Bushman’s Song,” “Last Week,”
and “Daylight is Dying,” the three songs from
“The Man from Snowy River,” recently set to
music, are now having an enormous sale. They
may be had from Angus and Robertson, Sydney,
and all inusicsellers. Post free, 2/2 each.*
The Eye-sight measure sent per post by
the inventors, Messrs. Greenfield and Barraelough,
the well-known opticians of George-st., Sydney,
is a wonderful little contrivance which will enable
eye-sufferers in the country to ascertain the data
necessary for obtaining proper spectacles. The
subtler forms of sight-defect cannot of course be
measured by the instrument, but it is thoroughly
effective for the more usual forms of eye affection
such as long sight, short sight, old sight. Pull
particulars from Messrs. Greenfield and Barra-
clough.*
It is not often that we make sugges-
tions to our readers as to what they
should wear, but we can’t help break-
ing our rule to recommend the Grass
Lawns and the French Millinery which
we have seen at W. T. WATERS
& CO., of George & King Streets,
Sydney. It seems to us as if this
establishment was destined to take
the lead in everything pertaining to
fashion.
Un-Artistic Artists.—Yell the Second.
“ When the flush of a new-born sun fell first on Eden’s
green and gold,
Our father Adam sat under the Tree and scratched with a
stick in the mould ;
And the first rude sketch that the world had seen was joy
to his mighty heart,
Till the Devil whispered behind the leaves, ‘ It’s pretty,
but is it Art ? ’ ”
Protests !! ! Naturally. Every honest
thought knocks the wind out of somebody, and
when he gets it back he starts howling. Was it
the con veil ient-for-quotation Dr. Johnson who
said this, and added that he never felt satisfied
with the articles that didn’t win him plenty of
abuse? If so, the thing has sense—not because
Johnson said it, but because it seems to the present
writer to have sense, and every writer is his own
ultima ratio.
* * * *
And “artists ” are tender. However their
bodies may fare, their minds are generally pam-
pered into luxurious self-satisfaction. The soul’s
lordliest pleasure-houses are built out of a tube of
01 1-color and an eigh teenpenny palette, and there th e
essence-of-the-artist sits and creates a new heaven
and a nevv earth to suit his whim, or his imagina-
tion, or whatever it is that squeezes the oil-color
on to the palette. And when some crude, objec-
tionable person throws a stone on the roof of the
pleasure-house the ar ist’s soul boils over as a
matter of course. What else could a self-respect-
ing soul do ?
* * * *
So it is objected that an “artist” cannot
get behind Nature : if he portrays Nature exactly
as she is he has achieved Art’s utmost. An ex-
ploded theory which The Bulletin dogmatist
delights in denouncing. Moreover, in practice
the artist denounces it himself. Here is B. E.
Minns outdaring Nokes and Stokes in azure feats.
In fad, or fancy, or fact, he sees Nature with a
purple glow on her, and his paintings glow with
purple. His “ Royal Exchange, London,” is
purple; so is his “ Aboriginal Female ” —an anti-
podal conjunction which goes to show that the
purple is in the artist rather than in his subjects.
(By way, that “ Aboriginal Female ”is a gem
of the Exhibition under notice, and largely because
she is considerably less purple than Minns’s other
aboriginal heads in Sydney Art Gallery.) But
the point is that Minns is not expressing Nature
as she is—or not, at all events, as she appears to
this critic and other average observers: he is
expressing Nature in terms of himself: he is
selecting and rejecting natural tints to make his
ideal color-harmony. Which he is perfectly justi-
fied in doing; though the onus rests upon him of
justifying his conception by his result—and this,
in his extremities of purple, he doesn’t do.
* * » *
There is no doubt that, so various and
continual are the changes in light and atmos-
phere and surroundings, every natural object
has at one time or another passed through
practically all the vicissitudes of color which
exist in the universe. On a thunderstormy
day, for example, a face may be green ; in flaring
sunlight it may be red ; moonlight can make it
“as yellow as a guinea.” But an artist, if he
understands his Art, will not deliberately seek
out outre lights and effects, because the es-
sential value of his Art is in its result on
the observer’s mind, and anything which
strikes the capable hormal observer as im-
probable, unnatural, lessens the force of that
result. There is a picture in the Exhibition—
“ The Old Bridge at Pyrmont (moonrise),’’ by D.
G. Reid —which is even more purple than Minns’s
pictures ; but its purple is natural and normal and,
therefore, Artistic. Everybody who crosses
Pyrmont Bridge in the evening must have noticed
the predominance of purple tints. But in
any ordinary light there is no purple tint
in an Australian aboriginal—or nothing like as
much as Minns shows ; and consequently to ordin-
ary observers his aboriginal pictures appear wrong
and untrue to the extent of his purple surplus.
I herefore, his Art is bad, for to a stranger he
would convey an entirely wrong impression of the
Australian aborigine.
* * » *
That is to say, that the idealisation of
Nature demanded from the Artist must come
along the line of probability : he must use his
imagination scientifically, especially if his picture
has value rather as a fact than as an idea. Minns’s
aboriginals have their chief value as facts :
as realistic representations to be handed down
to future times : and idealistic treatment of
them would spoil their truth and lessen their
value. But in J. H. Waugh’s pretty, dreamy
sketch of “ Calm ” nobody cares whether
he has copied a landscape exactly: with any other
landscape he might get the same charming effect.
“ Calm ” is one of the cheap and Artistic pictures
of the Exhibition : others are Alice Norton’s
“Remnants of Early Days,” and “Bulberaring
Lagoon ’’—pretty little glimpsy water-colors which
carry reality and conviction with them. That is
just what Frank Mahony’s “Sick Stockrider”
sketches do not, thanks chiefly to the lurid
coloring. Men and horses do not look like that
outside the “ Little Girl’s Own Painting-Book.”
Rightly considered, Mahony’s theme barred all
vagaries of color; he could not afford to idealise,
either ; what he had to do was to reproduce
Gordon’s vivid scenes as naturally and energeti-
cally as possible. He has the energy, but not the
truth.
* * * *
T. St. G. Tucker’s “ When Daylight Dies ”
is a pretty and harmonious picture; and it is Art
inasmuch as it is beautiful, and touches the eye
softly and musically, soothing rather than striking
the mind. And Norman McGeorge’s work grips
at once with its decision and truth; and Emily
Paul’s “Roses” are bright, and real, and well-
painted. Ethel Stephens’s flowers are as well-
painted, perhaps; but the uncolored wooden
panels are stiff and spoil the illusion. Her mis-
take is radical, since flowers, realistically treated,
can only be charming when alive; but Ethel
Stephens goes out of her way to show you that
they are. dead, and merely painted on a board.
Decorative, maybe ; but Art which is decora-
tive and nothing more is the lowest form
of Art—if indeed it be Art at all. Godfrey
Rivers’ work impresses by its neatness and
carefulness ; there is no inspiration in it, but
any amount of pains. Grace Joel’s “Youth” is
nice to look at, but one goes on and does not want
to return to it. And why ? Because, reiterating
the old saw, it is mere Nature—Nature not raised
to the Manth (or Womcintk) power. And that
will not pass for Art. The light on sea or land is
good enough in its way, but we want the light that
never was on sea or land. There is nothing in
Nature that the human mind cannot improve
upon. The most beautiful sunset is not as beauti-
ful as the fancy of the poet who watches it—
and an Artist must be a poet. The most impres-
sive scene in all history was not as great as the im-
pressive thoughts which it arouses in a genius con-
sidering it—and an Artist must be a genius. The
sweetest smile which plays in the eyes of a child is
not as sweet as the childish nature which gives
birth to it—and an Artist must be as a little child.
*•* . *
Child ! genius ! poet!—nice demands these
to make of the average “ artist ” ! Well, if he can-
not go all the way, he can at least go a little ; or,
if he cannot go a little, he can at least try to go a
little. But until he seems to try to go a little he
will not get The Bulletin’s vote. Now, Geo.
Lambert seems to try a little. In his “Bush
Idyll ” the goats are real goats, naturally
posed. But he has put too much in his pic-
ture, and his girl is growing out of a tree.
Arthur Streeton’s “Landscape ” is interesting be-
cause of the bulgy female figure in the right of
the canvas: she is exactly like a jieg-top. Who
invented the fiction that women are beautiful
creatures? And the same “artists ” “ Ilawkes-
bury Landscape ” is curious because of the large
expanse of blue-bag water in the foreground.
Two hundred guineas’ worth of such would last
the homely housewife an eternity. One would
much rather give guineas five for Will Kerr’s
“Jester ” —who has character enough to be worth
’em. And the question whether D. H. Souter’s
clever black-and-white drawings are Art with a
capital A must be postponed ; for The Bulletin’s
benediction upon the Society of Artists and
artists arrives just here.
A quaint horror is reported from Pahiatua
(M.L.), where a man is alleged to have been bailed
up on the road late at night, his-watch stolen, and
both his ears cut off. He is said to have been
more-or-less drunk at the time, and, as the man
arrested on suspicion is' a publican, it may be set
down to the Accursed Drink both ways. In fact,
it is a long time since there was a horror which
could be set down to Der-rink so effectually, and
if the ears—which haven’t yet turned up—are
found preserved in spirits, the moral will be com-
plete.
At Carlton (Melb.) police-court the other
day a publican was charged with allowing women
of bad character to assemble on his premises, and the
constable coolly swore that on entering the hotel
he found two prostitutes in the parlor. Cross-
examined by the defending lawyer the policeman
airily remarked that he “ only judged the women
to be prostitutes by their general appearance.”
The bench, by some extraordinary process of logic,
fined the. man <£2—but, upon notice of appeal
being given, the prosecuting sub - inspector
promptly asked permission to withdraw the
charge, smilingly admitting that the case wouldn’t
stand in the higher courts. How many police-
court decisions, especially in criminal cases, would
stand m the higher courts ?
An interesting description of a materiali-
sation performed by The Bulletin’s old acquaint-
ance, Mrs. Mellon, is given in the Aug. number of
the organ of Yic. spookery, This Would and the
Next. Forty people were present at the show,
which, judiciously, was held at the house of
another medium.. Mrs. Mellon invited an exam-
ination of the cabinet and surroundings, and then
“ a rosy-tinted glow ” was generated for the bene-
fit of sceptics. Mrs. Mellon sat in front of the
cabinet at first, and two female forms seemed to
appear, the medium being apparently in her nor-
mal. state and making occasional remarks. But a
falling walking-stick shattered her aura so much
that she was implored to go into the cabinet
Which she did, with the result that quite a “ Trip
to Chinatown ” procession of holy and unholy
ghosts, one of them with a wooden leg, waltzed
into existence :
Our old friend Cissy was amongst the visitors. She
came well forward from the cabinet, took a hand-bell
from the table and rang it vigorously. On being re-
quested to de-materialise she sank to the floor until only
her head and shoulders were visible. The form then
gradually rose until Cissy, fully developed, again stood
before us. At the conclusion of the materialisations
Cissy took control of the medium and entered into con-
versation with a learned Judge, who was present. An
interesting dialogue ensued between them, and if Cissy
did not tell us anything that was original, yet the rapidity
with which she answered questions and expounded Spiri-
tualism, as she knew it, was a surprise to those who had
often met her, but had never heard her in a prolonged con-
versation.
Who was the “learned Judge?” Casey is the
only Yic. Judge suspected of Windeyeresque curi-
osity.
At Poverty point.
Every married man should, as a matter of discipline,
take his wife to see Maxine Elliott and Nat Goodwin in
“ The Silent System ”at Sydney Lyceum. A more beauti-
ful satire on the deliberateness of the hysterical-jealous
woman who storms and snivels because the poor innocent
old man has stayed out late at the club was never seen.
A meeting' of married men will, it is said, be called by
private circular to organise a testimonial to Maxine Elliott
and Nat Goodwin aforesaid. By the way, the current
Lyceum bill gives the distinguished Yankee comedian a
fearful time. In “ The Silent System ” he has a 20-minutes
purgatory with his wife; in “The Nominee” he lias a
protracted sheol with his mother-in-law.
According to an Adelaide interviewer, Miss Ida Hamil-
ton told him that she judged a man by his necktie. Scot
Inglis must wear what the late Jimmy Allison would have
termed “ a necktie for the gods,” or perhaps his fair
young bride judges him by the marriage-tie, which is the
prettiest couleur de rose under Heaven.

Shifter : “ Frank Cates, who died last week, after years
of misery, was, like Capt. Humphreys, too much of the
gentleman, in a Debrett sense, for the actor. Yet he was
a London amateur Hamlet, with Beerbohm Tree in an
inferior part. Always colorless, without the afflatus.
Cates, Carden, Collier, and Bentley have been the only
Melb. representatives of Irving’s Mathias, in ‘ The Bells.’ ”
On his wedding-night Scot Inglis played Beaus4ant, in
“ The Lady of Lyons,” at Sydney Lyceum. When foiled
in his desire to possess Melnotte’s wife, Beaus&mt sarcas-
tically wishes her “ a happy honeymoon.” As Inglis re-
peated the lines of the text, a gallery-voice chipped in,
“ The same to you, old fellow, and many of ’em.”

night of their wedding, and it is said Kean had shortly

Kendal acted in “As You Like It” on their wedding
night. Says a gallery man: “How do you like it,
Madge ? ” Madge : “ Don’t know yet.”

How is that oft-repeated gag about the baldheads at the
theatre proved by a glance at the dress-circle of Melbourne
Opera House ! Nine-tenths of the men present any night
are over 45 years of age; the remaining tenth are princi-
pally Hebrews under 20.
Frank Cates was buried beside his wife, who died some
three years ago, in the “ actors’ corner ” at Melb. General
Cemetery. By the way, Cates, like all Gentile prodigals,
had a great “ down ” on the Chosen, wherefore ironical
fate decreed that his grave should be directly opposite a
Jew’s expensive tombstone.
The fascinating Mrs. Potter has left for the North,
taking with her Sydney’s best blessing. That Mrs. Potter
was a big draw is self-evident—she was a far bigger draw
even than Bellew, who played admirably, or Inglis, who
was fully as good as Bellew —in fact, she was nearly the
whole draw in herself, for, somehow or other, men and
women love to gaze upon Mrs. Potter, and even
those who revile her acting go to gaze upon her
again and again. The probable reason is that Mrs.
Potter’s acting is bad to the verge of genius;
her wonderful versatility enables her to act badly in
more different ways than any other actress The Bulletin
knows of. Even Bernhardt isn’t quite so versatile as Mrs.
Patter. Also, her affectations and artificialities are so
quaint and charming that, if the writer heard she was
going to act—and if he wasn’t a world-weary fragment
who has gone to the show three times a week for 10 years
—he would travel a considerable distance by gradual coal-
train to see her. And, even if the same b"las6 scribe was
dying, he would want to be carried into the show just
once more to see what kind of astounding hat Mrs. Potter
would wear next, and to wonder if he would see any hat
like it in the world to come. To sum her up, Mrs. Potter’s
charm apparently lies in the fact that, when she fails, she
fails in eerie ways that no one else would dream
of, so that it is a liberal education only to see her do
things wrong and wonder how she manages it. Probably
she W'ould have been as accomplished as she is “fetching”
had she begun at the bottom of the tree under some bright
dramatic tutor, and had been shaken frequently when she
was very young. As it is, the lack of being judiciously
shaken has made her only a diaphanous advt., and she
probably pays better as she is than she would any other
way.

Colborne Baber, as previously announced, has visited
England to better herself musically. Her charming rival
Frances Saville, goaded her on to this end. Latest advices
show that she will study French opera under Marchesi.
Perhaps the nearest approach in Australia to the virile
vocal chords of the late Madame Pateyis to be met w t.’i
in the person of Mrs. Usher, of Sydney, wife of the prin-
cipal assistant at Paling’s. She possesses verv rich, melo-
dious contralto tones singularly massive in the’lower
register. The “ Evening Prayer ”in Costa’s “ Eli ”is an
item which best displays the contralto’s breadth of treat-
ment and offers most scope for the exercise of a pheno-
menal compass.
Marie Elster seems to have been well-liked in Dublin
where she was singing with the Neilson Opera Co. in June
and July. Press notices praise her performances, vocal
and dramatic, in “Faust,” “Daughterof the Regiment,”
and “ Oavalleria.” The Irish Times credits her with
“highly effective ” acting as Santuzza in Maseami’s
opera ; the Weekly Independent says she sang the li.ui-
tana music “ as it has seldom been sung of late years in
Dublin,” and the Evening Herald says that she “ won
the sympathy, &c., &c., by her excellent singing and act-
ing” in the heavy role of Leonora (“ Trovatore Nnt
bad for on e who had never played a grand opera part
right through, outside of Westralia, until she went ’Ome
12 months ago. Marie Elster married in her teens, and
has a full-grown daughter.

A recent amateur performance of “Sweet Lavender”
at Hawthorn (Melb.), in aid of the Ladies’ Benevolent So-
ciety, yielded Pill net profit from £152 total receipts.
This contrasts favorably with the Lyric Club “chanty”
performances, which usually result in about 90 per ceiit.
ex’s and 10 per cent, of gross returns for the good cause.
Harrison White, pianist to Sydney Liedertafel, who is
now in England, is prosecuting his organ studies abroad.
It is his intention to put himself under Guilmant’s guid-
ance. Guilmant, the greatest living organist, will ~e
persuaded by Wiegand to give a few organ recitals on the
Sydney Town Hall instrument. Wiegand does not anti-
cipate much difficulty, provided musical Sydney will pay
a little more than sixpence for listening.

A certain curling-haired tragedian relates how he once
“had” an audience. “I was once—ha ! ha!—playing
Othello. I only played it once. Out all day—whisky -
more whisky—got to the theatre very late. In fact, only
just time to dress and get on. 1 ‘ made up ’ in a fiery
hurry, blacked my face and neck, and forgot all about
my hands. Othello entered—dark face, white hands.
Audience laughed, and when I found what they were
grinning at I got wild. The moment I got an exit I went
to my dressing-room, where I had a pair of flesh-colored
gloves. Then I blacked my hands, drew the gloves over
them, and walked on. Presently I had the stage to my-
self and when I knew the attention of the audience would
be fixed on me, I slowly drew off my gloves and exposed
the Moor beneath. For the rest of the performance, 1
tell you, that audience was as quiet as a peach. I got a
good notice in the morning paper, too, for my ‘attention
to minor details.’ ”

Who remembers Horace Bent’s farce parodying “The
Vagabond’s” New Guinea expedition, in which H.B. mas-
queraded in a white helmet several feet in diameter under
the style of Julian Tomatoes? The “ Vag.” himself was
present the first night and was one of the most amused
witnesses.

The new N.S.W. Registrar-General, who
£er forms civil marriages in Sydney, is Mr. Parry
iong. Advice to those about to marry—tarry
long.
In a matrimonial squabble the other day
at a Melb. suburban court, the parties had only
been married a week, and quarrelled over the
bride’s insistence on cycling.
Old friend Jules Joubert, working up
exhibition enthusiasm in Queensland, says an
Exhibition will bring a-quarter of a million
visitors to Brisbane, and every visitor will take
with him £5 on an average. Therefore? Well,
pay Jules.

N. : James Harrison without a memorial
in Geelong cemetery. Maybe a tombstone is an
insult to such. “ Those who, sometimes known,
far oftener unknown, really shape and mould the
New World.”

Overheard in Sydney Supreme Court.
Q.C, to junior suggesting argument: “Yes, yes,
that may do for the Full Court, but won’t do for
me.” It went down so well with theF.C. that
the Q.C. “ took his rule.”
If Queen Victoria survives till 25th of the
present month (Sept.) she will have reigned longer
than any other English monarch. George 111.
reigned 59 years 3 months and 4 days. Victoria
had reigned 59 years on June 20 last.
Melb. Australasian blames Frank Mason
for being over-anxious to avoid giving offence as
Chairman of Committees. Francis has been a
cautious man ever since the day he was suddenly
“ stoushed ” by Madame Lucy Chambers.
One of the first white men to settle on the
Lachlan below what is now known as Forbes died
last week, aged 67—Ernest Bowler, son of an old
Western pioneer, Major Bowler, who went over
the Blue Mountains and settled on Bathurst
Plains over half a century ago.
N. : “In a casual visit to Bishopscourt,
Melbourne, mansion of the Anglican prelates, I
noticed that the stairs were minus a number, of
bannisters. Significant of the three successive
bishops, Perry, Moorhouse, and Goe, being
married men without children.”
King Mahutu of Waikato (M.L.) is a pro-
hibitionist. His august father, Tawhaio Rex,
was a Chief Templar, and, moreover, wore the
blue ribbon of a suppositiously grogless life ; but
that did not prevent him having a breath strong
enough to intoxicate an exciseman.
Robert Davenport, who sat in the first
S.A. Leg. Council from 1851 to ’54, and who had
resided on his estate at “ Battunga,” near Mt.
Barker, for nearly 60 years, died this month, aged
80. His brother, Samuel Davenport, who still
lives, was a member of S.A. Leg. Council 50 years
ago.
Gov. Buxton, who favors the employment
of colored labor in the Northern Territory and m
tropical Australia generally, should be made learn
by heart the speeches of his philanthropic ances-
tor, the anti-slavery Thomas Fowell Buxton,
whose monument stands in one of the aisles of
Westminster Abbey.
A witness giving evidence in a divorce-case
before Windeyer, J., would persist in using jaw-
breaking words. “ I was present at the wedding,
your Honor,” said he, “and I saw the marriage
consummated.” “Hur-um,”remarked the Judge,
with a dry cough ; “ it is not usual, sir, to have a
witness to that ceremony.”
Latest advices say that Dr. Fiaschi will re-
turn to Sydney from the United States early in
October. The doctor penetrated as far as Kassala,
and had a good deal of surgical work there. After
revisiting his relatives at Milan, he visited the
London hospitals, and afterwards went to New
York to meet a celebrated American surgeon, Dr.
Murphy.
An aboriginal, doing “ two years’ hard ” in
Goulburn gaol, died the other day of consump-
tion. The medical officer, knowing the poor
blackfellow was dying, permitted him to have any
food he desired. The day before his death, our
countryman asked for baked ’possum, and his wish
was gratified. We all have our ideals.
Parson Bevan’s advice to Vic. politicians :
“If you believe in Freetrade teach Freetrade,,
even though you are kicked out of the House. ”
Bulletin’s advice to parsons (which they will
never accept); “If you believe in Christianity
teach Christianity, even though you are kicked
out of the church and the fat living you enjoy.”

rian scandal is common on

possible here ; and in an
Australian university one

to a salary of <£lsoo,
because the governing

instance is not a solitary

ports right. For want of
a better remedy, an age
for retirement should be
fixed and adhered to.
Many years ago J“ The Vagabond,” when
on a visit to Drouin (Vic.) got into an argument
on arrival with one M‘Lean, brother of Allen
M‘Lean, late Chief Secretary. M‘Lean had what
boxers call a “dirty left,” and it somehow got
introduced into the argument, with the result that
the “doubting Thomas” was badly disfigured.
In writing up Drouin, he made no mention of the
fight, merely stating that he “had been given
perhaps the warmest reception in his experience.”
Registrar Henry, of N.S.W. Insolvency
Court, sent bankrupt Hopley to gaol for seven
days last week for saying too often, when
under examination, “I don’t knovy; I don’t re-
member. ” Those who were present in Melbourne
Insolvency Court when Judge Noel sent gold-
digger Stubley to gaol for similar replies, and who
were sure at the time that the man was mentally
sick, as afterwards was proved, will resent the
exercise of this summary power by Judge or
Registrar.
A MOONLIGHT EPISODE.
TOM: “ Say, Maria, these folks must think we've been getting married!”
Lord Belmore, who is exciting himself in
the English Lords over the cession of Norfolk
Island to N.S. Wales, exhibited interest, when
N.S.W. Governor some 30 years ago, in that small
dependency only to the extent of rivetting liquor-
prohibition on the lonely inhabitants. He was
not a prohibitionist himself, either in theory or
practice, but he presented prohibition as the first
and last act of government in regard to these
helpless islanders.
N.S.W. Executive suffers from a unique
quandary at the present time. It finds its only
way to escape interminable trouble is to promptly
release prisoner Thomas Suffield, and release him
it would at once if only the prisoner would petition
for the Crown’s pardon or mercy. Suffield, how-
ever, declines pomt-blank to do any such thing,
but demands release on the ground of having been
wrongly convicted. The Executive is thus between
the devil and the deep sea.
Rev. Alex. Henderson, the gawky Scotch
Unitarian minister who has just gone ’Ome, never
got acclimatised in Melbourne. It is related in
Vic. medical circles that he recently called upon a
Collins-street doctor, who told him to strip.
Whereupon his reverence took off eight shirts and
a flannel binder, which binder he said was indis-
pensable as he had worn it ever since he was a
child. “ Eight shirts and only one God,” said the
doctor; “no wonder you feel sick.”
Louise Mack’s literary trifle, “ The World
is Round,” published by Fisher Unwin, got Eng-
lish notices which will encourage the young autho-
ress. The solemn, clerical Guardian describes it
as “ agreeably stimulating; ” the Literary World
said it was a “ very clever piece of writing ; ” and
London Daily Telegraph remarked that the story
was “ most cleverly written,” and augured well
for the success of the publisher’s new series. The
Times remarked : “It has the merit of being
cleverly written in the main. ”
W. H. Gray, the S.A. millionaire
miser, died the other day. He was a
big owner of Adelaide land-values, and
reputedly worth a million and a-half.
He carried his “ closeness ” so far that
he collected his own rents to save
agent’s commission, and walked to
Adelaide, five miles, to save tram-fares.
One of the sights of Adelaide was to
see Gray doing the block in a much-
patched suit, green with age.
The late N.S.W. Chief Justice
Martin was once invited out to dinner
in a Sydney suburb. His coachman —
now a Govt, messenger, who tells the
story himself —stabled his horses and re-
mained in the “servants’ hall” await-
ing his master. He imbibed —for the
first time, he says—a quantity of cham-
pagne. When it was time to return
home he was “gloriously incapable.”
The Chief, equal to the occasion, put
the coachman inside, mounted the
box, and drove to Potts Point himself.
Jehu expected the sack, but didn’t
get it.
The first Hebrew who occupied a
seat on the N.S.W. minor Bench was
the wealthy Joshua Frey Josephson,
about whose appointment there used
to be a queer story. He was Solicitor-
General in the Robertson Ministry
1868-9. The Govt, was very shaky;
so was a certain District Court Judge.
The Judge retired, and Josephson
took his place. The Govt, was recon-
structed three months afterwards.
There were then no retiring pensions
for D.C. Judges, but the man who
made way for Josephson spent the few
remaining months of his life in com-
fort.
Last week N.S.W. Premier Reid dignified
his office by appearing as counsel for the defence
in a neighbors’-backyard slangwhang. The trouble
was all about a tub. “You went into my yard
and stole my tub,” said one woman to the other
woman. Georgey-Porgey being a humorist, it is
hard to conceive why he should ever appear in a
tub case.
The Age publishes a list of successful
appeals against Supreme Court judgments during
the last three years, which must give pain to the
waxen Madden, Chief Justice, who tops the re-
cord, 18 of his judgments having been upset.
Williams comes a bad second with eight. Holroyd
is lowest on the list, only two of his judgments
breaking down on appeal.
M‘Lellan, “Dove of Ararat,” and Long-
more, member for Dandenong, are the two vener-
able enfants terribles of Vic. Assembly. The most
fluent and vehement of the younger generation of
politicians are mere mild tea-party orators com-
pared to these survivals of the Roaring Days.
Curiously enough, they never fall out with each
other. Which is just as well.

That is a tall story about Cecil Rhodes ar-
ranging to be shot by his companion sooner than
fall alive into the hands of the ferocious Matabele.
Nobody seems to know how his comrade was to be
shot in his turn. The yarn recalls the story of the
two girls who went out for a starlight ramble, and
made it up that if they met a rude man one was to
be insulted, and the other to scream for the police.
Murray Smith appeals to Vic. citizens for
the price of a decent tombstone to mark the grave
of James Harrison, one-time editor of Geelong
Advertiser, and a man of many worthy enter-
prises, who died in 1893. James Harrison was
“the first man in the world to make artificial ice
on a commercial scale for commercial purposes.”
At the time of his death he was engaged in the
apparently profitless business (in Vic.) of solar
salt making.
Professor Henry A. Ward, known as the
“ king of museum builders,” and in the front rank
in his peculiar scientific walk, is at present to be
found daily coatless and bootless pottering among
the marine vegetation of Torres Straits. The
Professor is an American, but his reputation
Europe is as high as among his own people. H*
has aschooner and a cutter wholly at his disposal

A MINISTER’S SMOKE NIGHT.
The fourth social smoke evening in connection
with St. James’ Church was held in St. James’
Parish-hall, Phillip-sltreet, last night, and proved
quite as successful amf enjoyable as its predeces-
sors. There were about 100 parishioners present.
The Rev. Carr-Smith, vicar of St. James’, is
satisfied that much good is being wrought by
these gatherings.
