POLITICAL
POINTS
A prominent Maoriland prohibitionist —
formerly an anti-compensation man—says that he
now believes in compensation, because “it is not
fair for the publican to stand all the loss.” All
the loss. If Maoriland goes in for Prohibition,
the revenue will lose <£59,000 a year beer excise
a eu- £400,000 in Customs duties, to say
nothing of the revenue from licenses. There are
q-te a lot of losses in the business which the
prohibition-party seemingly hasn’t begun to think
about yet.
JOHN’S LATEST.
“ A Chinese explained a stone tied
co his scales by saying that the scales
had only been put on the counter
while the floor was being scrubbed.
Discharged.”— NEWS ITEM.
LOO SHANG : “ All li, boss! Me tie stone to scales ’cos wind come
blow ’em away, that’s all li! You savee?"
So far as they have gone, the N.S.W.
Public Service Commissioners have recommended
the granting of pensions amounting to £6657 a
year to retrenched civil servants ; also gratuities
amounting to <£19,652, and refunds out of the
Superannuation Fund amounting to £1353. And
this is only the beginning.
The next Vic. Agent-General, to succeed
the somnolent and indolent Gillies, is to be a prac-
tical man who will care less for the showy part of
the business than for looking after the “ producing
interests.” The chances of Minister Taverner are
talked about, as he has been a busy, smart man at
the head of the Department of Agriculture.
The retired State - school teacher who
claimed £20,000 damages from the Vic. Education
Dept, for breach of contract, &c., is admitted to
have taken £l4O lump sum as a retiring allowance,
in lieu of a yearly pension of £43 7s. sd. Is the
Vic. Government an Ikey money-dealer, that it
makes such terms with impecunious clients ?
S.A. Oppositionists, with the help of the
Register, are anxious to depose Copley from the
leadership, as they do not think him aggressive
enough. They want to induce Solomon to take
the position, but he is too rich and too newly-
married and lias not enough time to spare. Besides,
he is too friendly with Kingston, who took the
trouble to go to Melbourne to attend his wedding.
Ex-Treasurer See (N.S.W.) is understood
to have publicly announced that when he and his
party come back to power the land-tax will be
repealed. The Dibbs-Lyne-See party, when it was
in power, tried to pass a much more drastic land-
tax than the present one, and the Reid party
opposed it. Then when the Reid party stole its
idea the Lyne-See party allowed itself to drift
over or be elbowed over to the other side from the
mere habit of being ‘ * agin ” the Reid party, and
the two parties silently swapped ideas, and thus it
comes about that John See is out loose upon the
face of the earth committing his supporters to
views which they mostly repudiate, and misrepre-
senting his own cause generally.
Melb. Herald has been interviewing
portly parsons and Fat Men generally on the
burning question—“ The Turk, shall we endure
him ? ” The parsons and other persons are unani-
mously of opinion that we should not bear with
the Turk, but should hotly protest at once, and
thereby “ strengthen England’s hand.” The ad-
visability “ of our minding our own blanky busi-
ness ”is not even hinted at. Some people are so
curiously constructed that they cannot hear of an
alligator without wanting to go far out of their
way to examine his tonsils.
Australia is thinking of federating, and the
N.S.W. Government is thinking of combining the
two Savings Banks into a State Bank of issue.
On the strength of these two possibilities there is
already talk of building a new Parliament House,
which can serve later on for the Federal Legisla-
ture, and reserving the best site in Sydney, at the
corner of Martin Place and George-street, for a
palatial bank structure, so that the new institution,
when it comes along, may be the National Bank
of Federated Australia. This off-hand way of
assuming that Sydney must be the Federal capital,
and that no other place is even in the running,
isn’t likely to help along Federation to any extent
worth weeping over.
The universal Saturday
half-holiday in Victoria is ex-
pected by Govt, to work a
considerable increase in the
railway returns. Peacock’s
Shops and Factories Act has
an eye to next year’s Bud-
get.
Carroll, who resigned
his N.S.W. Assembly seat
the other nightthrough bank-
ruptcy, and went back to his
constituents for political re-
habilitation, audaciously
“ cheeked ” Speaker Abbott
in his official home a short
while back. “ Better and
abler men than you,” re-
marked Carroll, “ have oc-
cupied the Speaker’s chair,
and were content with half
the rooms you own and half
the servants you require to
wait on you.” Mr. Speaker
stared. “ £l2OO a year and
perks, and seven rooms and
attendance isn’t bad,” rubbed
in Carroll.
It cannot be consoling
to Premier Reid when he con-
templates the gradual growth
of the unpopularity which
at last comes permanently or
temporarily to every politi-
cian, to remember that at
least four of his present col-
leagues rounded on their pre-
vious chief, Parkes, and
treated him to what he al-
ways considered a base be-
trayal. “My dear sir,” he
said one day to a friend, on
the names of some of his for-
mer colleagues being men-
tioned, “ I can’t trust myself
to speak of them.” Yet they
once were far more subser-
vient to Parkes than they
are to Reid. The latter
must feel like a man who has
become possessed of a dog
that bit its last owner.
The disorganisation of the Freetrade party
i”-N.S.W. Parliament is evidenced by the fact
that, at five out of six consecutive sittings, busi-
ness was blocked by a motion of adjournment
moved by a Government supporter. The party
grew so accustomed to obstruction in the days of
A REAL BLIND MAN.
the Dibbs Ministry, when it moved 13 votes of
censure in two years, that it now obstructs itself
from sheer force of habit.
Maoriland Public Health Amendment Bill
provides for shutting out foreign consumptives
unless they belong to the British military or naval
forces, or are agents sent by foreign Governments,
or “persons who are from time to time excepted
by the Governor-in-Council.” Query : Why
should the Governor-in-Council or the Governor
in anything else be allowed to make exceptions.
If a tuberculosed person has influence enough to
move the Governor-in-Council he is still just as
much tuberculosed as if he hadn’t, and it is just as
dangerous for him to come and expectorate the
debris of his lungs on the land as if he was any
other consumptive.
The case of Carroll, ex-M.P. (N.S.W.),
again illustrates the mockery of the Parliamentary
law which obliges a bankrupt member to resign
and yet permits him to be re-elected and resume
his seat before the court has made any investiga-
tion into his affairs. A bankrupt peer cannot sit
in the House of Lords until he receives his certifi-
cate of discharge.
Melbourne’s “Northern
suburban railway ” is up for
discussion for the fortieth
time. There are two rival
routes for this railway—one
through Collingwood, and the
other through Fitzroy—but
nobody knows what fabulous
sum each would cost for land
alone. If lands were assessed
by the owners for tax with
State right of resumption at
the assessment plus a percent-
age for disturbance, the Go-
vernment would know defi-
nitely in advance how much
any particular line would cost.
Under the present system
it goes into the business
blindly.
Barrister Fisher’s tilts
against the Vic. criminal pro-
cedure continue, and, it is to
be hoped, with some effect.
He points uut the unfairness
of the method of the pre-
sentment ; demands the ap-
pointment of a director-
general of prosecutions on the
English model ; alludes to the
unfortunate fact that judges,
who had never held one
criminal brief while at the
Bar, are called on to decide
in questions involving liberty
or life ; queries the justice
of the practice by which,
while records of previous
convictions are withheld from
the jury, they are supplied
to the judge on the back of
the presentment; and strenu-
ously maintains the right of
the accused to be supplied
with some means of com-
bating the often malig-
nant zeal and technical train-
ing of the Crown Prosecu-
tors.
In Dec., 1894, N.S.W. Leg. Council, by
23 to 9 votes, carried that boys shall be allowed to
work 10 hours a day in coal-mines. By “ boy ”is
meant a youth between 14 and 18. The Assembly
stood out for eight hours, but compromised the
other day and accepted nine. There is no doubt
the Upper House would have rejected eight hours
had the Assembly insisted on the clause. In an
Eight Hours country like Australia the authorisa-
tion by legal enactment of a nine hours stretch
for boys calls for early reconsideration.
“ The Federal capital,” says Chief Justice
Sam Griffith, “ should be central, easy of access,
not unduly exposed to the risks of invasion, and
its climate should not be such as to render it an
undesirable place of residence.” That knocks out,
m one act, Perth, Adelaide, Melbourne, Sydney,
Brisbane, Hobart, Launceston, Townsville, Cook-
town, and various others. They are all con-
venient for invasion at the shortest notice, and
every one of them is as far from being central as
any place can be, seeing that they are all on the
outermost rim of a circumference. Griffith de-
scribes accurately what the Federal city should
be, and as no Australian city fills the bill it is
necessary to build one for the purpose.
Sydney Telegraph proposes that the
relative advantages of Foreign-trade and Pro-
tection, as between N.S.W. and Vic., should
be settled once for all by “a comparison of
the market quotations of well-known brands
of imports . . An inquiry of this kind,” it
says “ covering the whole field of imports,
would do more to set at rest any doubt which
may exist as to what Protection costs the Vic-
torian consumer than any presentation of
Government figures.” This guileless way of
settling the difficulty, it will be seen, is to
deal with imports only. It isn’t to be a
question of retail prices all round, but simply
retail prices of imported goods. All the long
list of articles which N.S.W. imports and Vic-
toria manufactures for herself is to be left
out of the question altogether ! As a way of
settling any doubt which may exist,” this
proposition is unique.
Premier Seddon (M.L.) : “ Posterity
cannot do us any good, but on the other hand
we may do and are doing great and good
v/ork for posterity.” (Applause.) Base in-
gratitude ! Posterity cannot do us any good !
\V by, within the memory of people yet living
1 osterity has lent us some two-hundred million
pounds, or has backed our bill for thatamount •
therefore, Posterity has built all our railways
and telegraphs and most of our roads, bridges
breakwaters, lighthouses, fortifications, public
buildings, telephones, and irrigation works
and has dredged our rivers, and thinned
our forests, and done the sand-shifting job
at Sydney Centennial Park and the Shea’s
Creek reclamation, and paid for the ammuni-
tion fired off in the Maori war, and supplied
the money to fill revenue deficiencies, and con-
structed rabbit-proof fencing, and—well what
is there m all this great country that Posterity
hasn t done for us ?
Longmore asks Yic. Assembly to compel

DISCOVERER OF SINNERS: “ Has the young person who is sitting to
these artists anything on? ”
HOUSEKEEPER: “ Sure an ’ she has, most unusual; she’s wearin'
spectacles .”
The Boodlewraith is again busily bestirring
itself in the interests of Assisted Immigration to
Queensland, Dickson actually advocating free im-
migration for domestic servants. For the benefit
of intending immigrants it may be mentioned that,
according to the report of the Govt. Labor Bureau,
the rate of wages offered to farm-laborers in
Queensland in 1895 ranged from Bs. to 15s. a week,
and the supply was five to one on the demand !!!
Champion says truly that Alfred Deakin’s
political future is daily damaged by his profes-
sional duties. Deakin, the democrat, seems to be
specially unfortunate in those duties. He is
always on the side of Fat Man—defending the
unjustly accused shipowners before a Sangster
Board, or proclaiming the honesty and virtue of
boom-time bank balance-sheets. It is hard for a
Melb. barrister to act like a desirable kind of
politician. Look at Purves, Q.C. The large,
outspoken speeches he made of yore to the A.N. A.
were inconsistent with bis recent defence of the
City of Melbourne bankers.
The Sydney D.T. : “The taxpayer green
enough to accept an Act that allows workmen to
fix their own wages badly needs a kindergarten
lesson.” Yet the same D.T., when striving to
pose as the friend of Labor, can quote glibly
enough the economical scripture that a workman
has as much right to fix the price at which he will
sell his labor as a shopkeeper has to fix the price
at which he will sell his commodities. Only more
so, since labor is a commodity plus flesh-and-blood.
Also, don’t a lawyer, a doctor, a dentist, an archi-
tect, and others, fix the price at which they will
sell their labor? Doesn’t the D.T. fix the price
at which it will sell itself? (The last is meant
both ways—this way and the other way.)
The “Idler” Says,
“ When a thing that is advertised greatly is good,
it goes and goes permanently, when it is bad it
only goes for awhile. The public finds it out.”
The verdict of the public, stamping its approval
or disapproval upon the merit of things, is
generally reliable. Is it not easy to see that the
enormous popularity of that greatest of all
remedies —Warner’s Safe Cure —which has been
so prominently before the public for so many
years, is based upon invaluable merit ? Thousands
of men and women in all parts of the world are
kept alive by its use, and the millions who 'find it
a means of preserving their health and prolonging
their lives will answer unhesitatingly, “ Yes.”
Mr. J. S. Storey, Erskine St., Sydney, N.S.W.,
writes : “ I have much pleasure in testifying to
the efficiency of Warner’s ‘Safe’ Cure. I have
used it for the past three or four years for both
fevers and severe colds, and have found it an
excellent remedy if used in time. lam now en-
joying splendid health, which I attribute solely to
the free use of this medicine, and can most cer-
tainly recommend it to any person suffering from
similar complaints. The universal curative proper-
ties should make it invaluable in the bush. ”


In the Yic. railway service they have got
the system of appeals down so fine, in the anxiety
to do strict justice to everybody, that the other
day it cost the court half-a-day and over £4 of
salaries to decide that a very subordinate official
hadn’t been improperly fined 2s. 6d. for inefficient
cleaning of a carriage.
H. : “ Fitchett’s extremely well-written
Saper on Wolfe in Canada omitted an incident.
in the night before he left London a military
banquet was held in his honor. He drank deeply,
drew his sword, smacked it on the table, a la
General Boom, and swore how he would paste
Johnny Crapaud. The fogies whispered, ‘ Good
heavens, is Pitt sending this Munchausen ? ’ ”
“ While the Billy Boils,” by Henry Law-
son, with eight plates by F. P. Mahony, is already
in its third thousand, and may be had from Angus
and Robertson, Sydney. Post free 5/6. #
Melb. demise : Orkney. One of the best-
known in the city for forty years. One time nau-
tical, another boniface, another M.P., a big,
strenuous fellow, with perennial boyish energy.
He was quite a New Guinea pioneer, in a little
cutter of his own. He went a long way up the
Fly River, it is reported, and apparently his idea
was gold. Not a word would he say about the
expedition, and a long and interesting account of
it was the reporter’s Jules Yerne from first to last.
The Goold Bicycle Co., Ltd., whose cele-
brated Red Bird and Brantford cycles hold the
Canadian market, is going to make this continent
hum with the pace and number of its machines.
The Red Bird holds all Canadian records for the
last six years, and M‘Leod put up the world’s
record for a mile in 1.33 2-sths on a Red Bird.
Graeme won the time-prize in the Sydney to Goul-
burn road-race on a Red Bird ; and Pearson
romped in with first prize and fastest-time prize
in the road-race last Saturday. Deeds talk.*
Eno’s “Fruit Salt” (one of Nature’s own
products) keeps the blood pure, and is thus of
itself one of the most valuable means of keeping
the blood free from fevers and blood poisons, liver
complaints, &0., ever discovered. As a means of
preserving and restoring health it is unequalled ;
and it is, moreover, a pleasant, refreshing and in-
vigorating beverage. After a patient and careful
observation of its effects when used, I have no
hesitation in stating that if its great value in
keeping the body healthy were universally known,
not a household in the land would be without it,

would contain it.—J. C. Eno. Sold by all

A GRAND LITERARY INSTITUTION.
Cole’s Book Arcade now runs from the centre
of the block in Bourke-street to the centre of the
block in Collins-street, Melb., a distance of 660 ft.,
and contains the following 17 departments, mostly
literary and artistic: 1, New Books; 2, Second-
hand Books ; 3, Stationery ; 4, Music ; 5, Fancy
Goods; 6, Toys; 7, Perfumery; 8, Pictures; 9,
Artists’ Materials ; 10, an Ornamental Exhibition ;
11, a Japanese Exhibition ; 12, a Wonderland ;
13, a Smiling Gallery; 14, a Child’s Picture
Gallery ; 15, a Bargain Room ; 16, a Tea Room ;
17, a splendidly arranged new Wholesale Book
Department, containing half-a-million books. The
main walks of the establishment are half-a-mile
long.*

A Riot of LoVdioe&S-
Ode on the Opening of the Kalgoorlie
Railway, September 8, 1896.
No blast had shaken the reefs that lay,
■Blowing and dipping, like whales at play,
In the ocean of stratified rock :
Kalgoorlie, as yet, was a name unheard,
Except by the blacks, who had coined the word
From their tautologic stock.
The hard, earth crust had a garb of green,
Of salt-bush puny and dwarf-gum lean
And mulga with stunted stems,
Which, as the sun the landscape crowned,
All spread their weftage on the ground
With glittering quartz for gems.
No trail was famous, no wheelwrights’ fires
Had ever been cheated of waggon tyres
By the tug of the Devil’s Pinch :
The latticed wastes had not yet wrung
The creak from the passing load that swung
Sideways many an inch.
And still, in vain, for evermore
Would you have called, as the wind swept o er,
Like the ripply romp of a shelly shore,
O gold-bespattered West:
Had not your depths been honeycombed
By miners who burrowed in drives reef-domed —
The batteries tell the rest.
And you, Kalgoorlie, Hannan’s “ Show,”
With kings of mines, where, three years ago,
None wot of the Boulder lode,
Surprise with each new battery fed ;
Your dumps, like beads upon a thread,
Lie along every road.
But grieve for the stricken of your van-guard
When your cams are giving the accolade,
While fortunes leave the mill !
Baffled, uncoffined, their shrouds were spun
By the mists that rose to meet the sun
Round Maritana Hill.
Now, for this jovial week, proclaim
Welcome, in welcome’s friendliest name,
With banners gay unfurled !
At last, for you, has been grafted on
A rib to the iron skeleton
That carries the flesh of the world!
SPOUTING NOTIONS.
The last day’s play in the Australians final
match was not satisfactorily accounted for by the strictly-
partial cablegrammer. Bare particulars to hand state
that the two last men of the English team resumed opera-
tions on a heavy, “ but easy,” wicket and added 38 runs
by “ hitting out merrily ” before Richardson was caught.
Donnan and Iredale then led off the Australians’ second
innings, and made 22 runs for no wickets before lunch-
time. After lunch, J. T. Hearne went on to bowl and
either he or the wicket became so “treacherous and
difficult ” that the ten men were downed for an additional
41 runs, making a total of 63. The South of England men
followed with 45 for two wickets (presumably the ground
had ceased to be treacherous), and then the match ended
in a draw. Only 146 runs were made during the day.
There is no mention of rain having interrupted play, or of
stumps being drawn earlier than usual. How long were
the Australians batting for their 41 runs after luncheon ?
Also, how would the cable reports have been worded in the
event of the Englishmen escaping a defeat as the visitors
seem to have escaped it ?
* * * *
Dear Bulletin, —The Australians’ record for
their matches with the nine strongest teams they met
during the tour is practically eight defeats and one vic-
tory. Three of the practical defeats were drawn games,
in each of which Trott won the toss. Again, I will say
that the last Eleven was the luckiest, as well as the
steadiest, that has gone ’Ome. Throughout the dry
season, when the winning of the toss was a considerable
advantage, Trott won it nearly every time. And when
the weather happened to change during a game, the
change favored the Australians. Furthermore, not one of
the nine best English teams aforesaid had first innings on
a real “batsman’s wicket,” and in only three cases did
they bat first, anyway. —T.C.S.
* * * *
J. T. Hearne, more or less (generally more), was
concerned in three out of six English victories against
Australia, including the “off” match at Leyton. Two of
the other three were due to Bobby Peel. It. has not yet
been remarked that Trott’s Eleven departed from all
traditions of Australian cricket in England by getting
beaten on sticky pitches, not because they were deficient
in cunning bowling talent, but because they had no dash-
ing sloggers among them. They are a patient fine-weather
team, and, if they progress all right in the meantime,
Australia should win a majority of the test-matches
against Stoddart’s next invaders.
* * * *
Nearly all the Australians suffered from the
trying June weather in England, Giffen and Darling
particularly. By the way, the English seemed to
think the team was quite happy when the sun blazed ;
in some cases the intense heat was made an excuse for
English defeats. One paper, just after the test-match,
alleged that the visitors thought nothing of batting and
fielding in Australia with the thermometer at 120 deg. in
the shade! Why don’t the Pirate Bits papers say
straight out that Australians shed their skins in summer?
* * * *
Some of the English papers have promoted one
or two of the Australians. Jones, the Adelaide Exhibi-
tion ground-hand, is described as a lawyer, and Johns is
credited with having a large Melb. legal practice.
* * » *
Dear Bulletin, —The Times didn’t assert, sug-
gest, or insinuate that an English University eleven was
equal to the Australian team at all points. It said, by
way of testifying to the activity and alertness of “ our
boys,” that they fielded quite as well as the University
young men — i.e,, equal to the smartest fielding-teams
procurable in Hingland. There is really no occasion for
your ultra-patriotic paragraphists to howl about Hinglish
press-criticism of Australian cricket, which is much more
fair and generous than Australian reports of international
contests. There now ! —Bothsydes.
* * * *
Against the theory that Murdoch’s first, or
second, eleven was equal to Trott's crowd, old stagers will
always advance the fact that English cricket was half-
asleep fifteen years ago. The Australian visits woke it up.
The pro’s were lazy, slovenly fieldsmen in those times,
whereas their work nowadays is neat and clever, although
not quite as hearty as the best amateur “ scouting.”
* » * *
George Lohmann had a benefit-performance of
“ Othello” at Covent Garden Theatre, worked up by his
half-brother, Charles Pond, an enthusiastic amateur who
played the name-part himself. Charles Pond is presum-
ably the son of Christopher Pond, an original partner in
the onetime Australian firm of caterers, Spiers and Pond.
* * * *
The Australian Eleven, being top dog, can
afford to ignore the Australian Cricket Council. That
clutch of antiquated nobodies is fighting the public as
well as the Eleven, for the public will roll up to see its
gods no matter what a hopelessly ineffectual body like
the Cricket Council may protest. Herein is shown
manager Musgrove’s capacity. Having run leg-shows and
other attractions, he gauges the public temperament to a
nicety. The only regret is that the Eleven, after having
a real fat time, should open itself to the charge that it is
still ravenous for “ gate.”
* * * *
George Giffen, it is said, has conceived the idea
of a volume of cricketing recollections, and will publish.
Perhaps it will be called “ Me ! by George Giffen.”
* * * *
That 31 ’B2 of Syd. Gregory’s for 49 innings tops
Murdoch’s best of '30’62 per innings. N.S.W. won each
time. N.S.W. takes the palm in batting, bowling, and
wicket-keeping this trip. Perhaps Syd. Gregory might
take it for fielding too, though Trumble is understood to
have been sensational in the slips.
* * * *
“ Clem. Hill’s 118 against Surrey and 74 against
Kent,” writes “ An Australian Abroad ” to The Bulletin,
“ were most valuable innings, and when he becomes a
little stronger on the off-side, where he has some danger-
ous uppish strokes, he should be one of the best batsmen
Australia has ever produced.
“The match at Bexhill against Earl De La
Warr’s Eleven was a delightful picnic, but if the cricketers
intend to enjoy such outings as these, they should, cer-
tainly make sure of winning, or people are certain to
think thej r have been subordinating cricket to stomach.
“Jones sent down the wickets like nine-pins at
Birmingham in the match against Warwickshire, clean
bowling five, and getting six wickets for 33 runs. The
Broken Hill man is a very fine successful bowler, but if he
is hit at all I don’t believe he has too much heart; he
certainly feels punishment severely.
“ Now that this Eleven have established them-
selves as a successful combination, they want to keep
things going as long as it is possible to make money. A
Maoriland tour is already fixed without anyone being
consulted, and they talk of playing a match in Brisbane,
and probably also Melbourne and Adelaide before they
give up. ‘ Why shouldn’t we make some money while we
can’’they ask, and apparently as far as they are con-
cerned Interprovincial cricket can go to the devil.
“ The match the team propose to play in Paris
will be got up purely to encourage foreigners to take up
the noble game with more enthusiasm. (Apart, of course,
from the political view of the question, for it is sure to
strengthen those cordial relations, &c., &c.). That it is
not likely to come off at all unless the financial prospects
are good is mere rumor. Besides the boys want to see
Paris before they return.

* * * *
“Jack Lyons has found a wife and settled
down.” Settled down ! How is it the vernacular of
burial is always applied to matrimony ?
• * * *
The Australians’ batting pans out well against
the Britishers. Australians, 12,246 runs for 511 wickets;
average, 24 per wicket; Britishers, 10,058 for 581 wickets;
average, 17.3 per wicket. The Australians scored 18 cen-
turies against the opposing bowling, while only three cen-
turies were made against them. The Australians scored
300 or over 15 times, while the Britishers made 300 or
over twice. The biggest score of the Australians was
625 against Derbyshire, while the Britishers’ top is the
M.C.O. innings (second match) of 367. The lowest Aus.
score was 18 against Marylebone, while the lowest British
score was"Gloucestershire's 17.
* * * *
Pougher’s 5 wickets for 0 against Australia in
the first M.C.O. match stands easily first as a bowling
feat. Trumble bagged 6 for 8 against Gloucestershire,
and M'Kibbin 4 for 7 ; and M'Kibbin took 7 for 11 against
Lancashire. Hearne took 6 for Bin the last match.
* * * *
Dear Bulletin, — Re the time-test as applied to
Hinglish and “colonial” batting, I would like to add a
postcript to my previous letter in reply to “ Australia.”
He instanced the slow scoring of the Surrey team in dis-
proof of my statements, and I suggested that we should
wait for full particulars. Herewith I quote two fragments
of full particulars from Melbourne, not London, papers.
The first relates to the drawn game with M.C.O. : —“ On
afternoon of the second day, about 5 o’clock, the Austra-
lians went in 165 to the bad to fight against time, and by
securing a draw avert defeat. By very slow, very careful
and very determined play this was managed, but it
raised the antipathies of the crowd, who howled with
derision as ball after ball was carefully played and runs
came at the rate of about 40 an hour, and even these
made by sudden brilliant dashes in the midst of long and
dreary intervals of stonew.alling.” And now, touching
that slow Surrey innings, which, by the way, didn’t last
all day, as “ Australian ” asserted: —“When Surrey won
the toss there was just a question as to whether they
would bat first or put their opponents in, but Read would
not run the latter risk, and sent in Abel and Brockwell,
who made a fine start. . . . The. pitch was playing
none too well, and it could not be said that the cricket
was very interesting. . . . The innings lasted five
hours for 283, an excellent achievement, under the cir-
cumstances.” —The Culprit Scribe.
* * * *
Some of the best scores made by the Australian
Eleven were against the English reporters. “We never
play in Adelaide when it is more than 125 deg. in the
shade.”

Every public school in Paris has now its
own canteen, where warm meals are served at Id.
a head to those who can pay, and free to those who
cannot.
Analysis shows that ordinary French
brandy, such as that supplied to the French army,
consists of the commonest white spirit flavored
with a “ bouquet ” compounded of castor-oil,
cocoanut-oil, and other fatty substances, treated
with nitric acid.
N. : “Somebody writes about actors for-
getting their words through over - f amiliarity.
Here is the reverse action. Last Sunday night,
in a Melb. suburban Anglican church, I heard the
pastor read over the form of prayer for the Prince
of Wales three times in succession. Some thought
the Queen was dead, others that the pastor was
especially solicitous over Persimmon’s Derby.”

Dear Bulletin, —I met a semi-soaked
cockney to-day who gave in a crisp, jerky sen-
tence the pith and axiom of vermilion termino-
logy. I had asked him for a match, and was re-
turning the box when, through his shakiness,
fell and spilt, whereupon he poured on me a large-
sized printer’s desk of asterisks and dashes. y
sorry,” I said, “ but there’s no necessity to swear,
old man.” “Who the ’ell waits for theneshes-
sity?” he answered; “I only wants the hoppor-
tunity. ”■—G. E. D.

tion, to take place on the Rosehill Racecourse on
Monday, October 7, 1896. The value of the prizes
m the sports and art union amounts to ,£l3OO ; the
first pnze being valued at ,£5OO ; second at £l5O ;
third prize, piano, value £75, and 154 other prizes
ranging in value from £22 10s. to 10s. 6d. The

London Referee, commenting on the Stanbury-
Hardmg match, makes the subsequent downing of Stan-
bury by veteran Gaudaur appear quite natural. “Last
Monday afternoon,” says the Ref. oracle, “I found myself
ln ” e^ween Thames’ banks, wondering- what
earthly good I was as an old sport if I couldn’t under-
stand so simple a thing as a professional sculler’s race.
James htanbury, the New South Welshman, I saw smother
Richard Harding, the English boat-puller. That the said
James did worry his boat along at a great rate, and quite
outpace the little fellow from Chelsea, I quite recognised.
No one who reads my ‘ Notions ’ can find cause for accusing
•” e of having a down on Australia or Australians—rather
the other way about is my habitual tendency, is it not?
L i lu^ en^on because I am about to record
that the contest filled me with surprise and dissatisfac-
tion. ... We all know that Stanbury must be a right
good man, or he could not get such way on his boat and
keep the pace up over the Thames championship-
course, and, as his method results so profitably in
this way, I suppose _we must accept it as right for
him. But that he is what I always was taught
was a good sculler I will not have at any
price whatever. ... With all his power and length,
he carefully avoids approaching the conventional stan-
dard of good form. Not an inch does he swing, only sits
straight up in his boat, and, with a mighty shove of the
legs ana tug of the anus, drags his sculls through the
water to finish bolt-upright. Harding stuck to his bad
bargain gamely enough, but never, to my thinking,
showed any dash or life. He was, I believe, kale. lam
aware that it is not easy to dash much in sculling a stern
wager after a man who cuts you down in a single stroke,
as the big Antipodean did the little Cockney. Still one
waited and waited, expecting the Thames man to do
something more than plod along Probably Stan-
bury could have shown us much better form I
would have given something to see him hardly pressed, so
as to understand what his form is when reallv extended.”
Gaudaur really extended Stanbury, and the Waler’s form
was proved bad, seemingly.
* * » *
Stanbury coining straight back to Australia
after being’ defeated by Gaudaur! The strongest evidence
that he was beaten on his merits.
* * * •>
Towns challenges Gaudaur to row on the Parra-
matta for £5OO a-side and the championship—Gaudaur to
get £lOO expenses.
* « * *
The Yankee boys from Yale University came to
Henley Regatta in a very modest, spirit, saying “ We came
here to row, but more to learn to row than anything
else.” A fine-looking crew, pulling a short, scrappy
stroke when collared, the Yales were put out of it in their
first heat.
* * » *
Now that the Harding race is over,” writes an
Australian abroad to The Bulletin-, “ one hears a little
news. Very little money could be got on by Stanbury
and co., in spite of the fact that he was drowned, broke
his oars, and did all that was possible to give the other
side a chance. Then he began to vomit blood after every
short spurt, and did several other extraordinary things—
so report had it. Now it leaks out that Stanbury did all
his work under the watchful eye of Tom Sullivan at
Sa.m., while the spurts and daylight trials were ordinary
bluffs.
* * * *
Cyclist Elliott is a nerveful personage. The
last time he was in a big race he had to start in a final at
i; he lay down at 2, and slept till 3.30. Were Clinton
and Matthews, of Vic., able to do this, what races
those fine sprinters could show the public ! Parsons, too,
was a nervous subject; the night before his first Austral
he did not sleep a wink.
* * * *
It is alleged that the secret of a great cyclist’s
misogyny is a love affair which occurred some years ago.
He was not jilted, however ; indeed, what woman, seeing
his iovely legs plugging into his 96 gear, would dream of
jilting him !
* * * *
The valorous Bilmartin did mighty deeds at
Brisbane. While training one evening he came a fearful
slump, covering the asphalt for yards with blood and
meat scraps. Then he arose with a red, raw leg 1 and an
undaunted eye, and rested till next day. When the
truthful reporters arrived, the ’Murkan showed them his
ankle-bone, which was bare, because its hair and skin had
been left upon the track the night previous. Nathless
Bill trained next day. At the end of a week he placed
his bare ankle-bone and red, raw leg upon the pedal of
cycling history and romped in with new records from 2 to
25 miles. He did all this because he got a fee of £l2O
from the Beckman gear people.
* * * *
The Beckman gear increased the weight of
Martin’s cycle from 22J to 361 b. This gear clainis to get
power i.e., speed—out of the cyclist’s arms as well as
hands.
* * * *
Bilmartin, it is said, was struck so hard up in
Brisbane by Cupid’s dart that it raised a blister on his
chest. The blister vanished in a day, however and
Bilmartin smiled again.
* * * *
Cycling is beginning to smell. Clothes, boots,
&c., are frequently being stolen f jm training-quarters :
bookmaking on cycle-tracks in N.S. W. country districts is
rampant; nearly everybody is riding more or less “ stiff ”
in order to scoop a big pot. Ex-peds. and fly-boys are
either a-wheel or training equally immoral associates
a-wheel. Stables are growing in size and number, and
the strongest smell comes from Smellbourne
* * * *
Eor mediaeval caddishness, the cake has been
taken by certain Vic. civil servants—lordly youths who
101 l over the chairs talking horse while the public pounds
its knuckles on the counter—who have—haw !—formed a
Cm] Service Cycling Club, among the rules of which is
this gem Subject to the approval of the committee
the club shall be open to all past and present members of
the public service, other than messengers, house-keepers
court-keepers, criers, warders, guards, porters, police
below the rank of sub-inspector, non-commissioned
officers and privates of permanent naval and military
forces, or persons whose duties are of a mechanical or
unskilled character, or who are in the non-clerical divi-
sion of the public service. For the purpose of this rule
the Mint, Metropolitan Board of Works, Victorian Rail-
ways, and the Melbourne Harbour Trust shall be deemed
to be part of the public service.” The club is understood
to have emanated from the Custom House, where Com-
missioner Best’s father is still employed in a non-clerical
capacity, and is consequently debarred from membership
Well might Francis Adams have said “ I hate fashionable
Victorians. They’re such snobs. They are worse than
English people.” And the most sickening part of the
whole show is the fact that the aristocrat who runs the
cycling column of a professedly socialistic and democratic
Melb. journal is allowed to defend this rule on the ground
of its “ keeping out larrikinism ” !
* * * *
W. S. Wedd, the good all-round athlete who
won last Saturday’s Bendigo-to-Melb. road-race, was
literally unknown to the general body of cyclists prior to
that date. He received 30min. start of Middleton on
scratch (the latter retired ill) ; yet he pedalled home in
shrs. 28min. 40secs.—faster time than anyone else in the
race.
* * * *
E- A. Pearson’s 50-miles road win at Parra-
matta (N.S.W.) in 2hrs. was a good performance
over such rough and hilly country. He secured first
prize, and fastest time-prize. Pearson began cycling in
’B4, started racing two years later ; did so moderately that
he dropped it in ’BB, and went in for rowing. The last
three years he has been an interprovincial eight-oar man.
He says his recent win is greatly due to the excellent
pacing he had.
* * * *
Megson, Payne, and Lewis have, so far, shown
themselves no match for the English cracks. Apart from
the fact that they are all more or less ignorant of the
essentials of training, it is well to remember how the
sick Zimmerman left their class “■standing” in a sprint.
And Zim. found our tracks just as big a handicap as
Megson and co. would find Catford,
Pearson, who rode a “Red Bird,” won the Im-
perial Rover bicycle presented by Messrs. Bennett and
Wood in the N.S.W. 50-mile road race last Saturday.
* « » »
M'Donell, thanks to Kahlo, his trainer, will
come to the post in splendid form for his 25-roile match
with Martin. These American cyclists and trainers have
twice the brain of the local talent. Lewis, Megson, and
Payne in globo haven’t the amount of intelligence that
M'Donell possesses. The Australian, careless and con-
ceited, allows any trifling allurement to divert him from
his work. Yankee Martin is all method, and has more
force of character than any other cyclist in Australia.
The moment he is due on the track he is there—everyday.
Nothing can divert him from his fell purpose. “ I must
go right ahead,” he says, “and do what I ought and win
all I can.”
* * * *
A week or two ago a wave of perfervid loyalty
landed heavily on the rorty South Melb. football-club,
and it forwarded a delicately-tinted note to Govmentouse,
craving the presence of “Uncle Tom” at its match with
the Oollingwood. lI.E. accepted, and the South pro-
ceeded to bill Melb. with posters headed with the phiz, of
viceroyalty to boom the match. But when the South
came to reckon up accounts with Oollingwood it carefully
docked half the expense of entertaining the Governor and
printing his portrait from the latter’s div. But the
Collingwoods “ weren’t having any,” and wrote in
strong terms to the Tie. Football Association complaining
of the South’s action, more especially as the aristocratic
Southerners, though they assisted the vice-regal party to
“ scoff ” the victuals provided, did not deem the Woodsies’
committee.“ class enough ”to partake of the feed. Like-
wise there is one item of £2 2s. for a band, which played
Himperial ditties under Uncle Tom’s nose, which is dis-,
puted by the Northern suburb. Altogether, there is
every element for an amusing squabble ahead. The Go-
vernor might obviate this by magnanimously offering to
pay the disputed amount out of his own pocket. He
might !
* * • *
After three months “consideration ” that hypo-
critical body, the Vic. Football Association, has decided to
disqualify footballer Webb for life. Webb originally played
for the Essendon, and after peregrinating three' or
four other clubs, was refused a permit for another
change. Shortly after this the Essendon delegate made
a stirring speech about the purity of the sport and
the nobility- of amateurism, which impelled Webb
to write to the Association stating that during
the time he played for Essendon he was paid as a profes-
sional. Then a committee of inquiry was appointed with
the result above stated. Extraordinary to relate, the
Essendon Club and the Essendon delegate are left severely
alone, though if Webb’s letter be true, as the committee
has unquestionably decided, they are equally culpable and
answerable.
* * * *
Oollingwood, which beat Carlton 9g. 15b. to
lg. 3b., now leads for the Vic. football premiership, but
has to meet the powerful Fitzroy team (victor over
S. Melb. last Saturday), to-day, 19th. Whichever way
the game goes, Oollingwood seem likely 1896 premiers, as
P. Melb. has a very good chance of defeating S. Melb.
Should that happen, Essendon should get second place.
* * :fc *
With the defeat of S. Melb. by Eitzroy, what
would have been a unique finale to the Yic. football
season—a tie for the premiership—is averted, unless
Collingw-ood as well should happen to go down before
Fitzroy. For the sake of the sport it is to be hoped the
“Woodsies” will be beaten : a big play-off at the end of
the season would do a lot to revive interest.
* * * *
Early next month there will be an election for
five seats on the V.A.T.C. committee. Only two new can-
didates are likely to come forward, and as one of the
retiring members is not seeking re-election one of the
strangers is certain of a seat. Interest in the contest is
confined to the question whether the other outsider will
be chosen instead of Mr. “ Dowry ” O’ Halloran.
* * * *
The V.R.C. government has given way on one
point of its old conservative policy, by agreeing to leave
bye-elections of committeemen in the hands of club-mem-
bers. Hitherto, the club has been restricted to the right
of filling up the periodical vacancies on the committee.
When a death or retirement happened between general
elections the survivors put on their own man with a
private ballot, and to this absurd privilege the holders
clung tenaciously. At last annual meeting, Mr. R. A.
Molesworth made a vigorous attack on the system, and
last week the gallant defenders decided to capitulate
without parley.
* » * *
Callanan, the jockey who steered Merman so
cleverly the other Saturday at Caulfield, was mentioned
in The Bulletin as the coming boy when he rode Cydnus
and Homespun on the last day of the V.R.C. autumn
meeting. A more promising boy than Stevenson, or any
other of them since Gorry used to show the veterans how
to finish a race. Backing Callanan’s mounts would have
been a successful business up to date.
* * * *
The A.J.C. in determining to seek legislative
sanction for the introduction of the totalisator into
N.S.W. makes a praiseworthy effort to lessen the evils of
horse-racing. The totalisator has been adopted in S.A.
W.A., Q., and M.L., and should be adopted in N.S.W.’
because it is worked on a business-like basis ; because its
doings are always before the public eye in the bright
light of day; because it stops men from betting* unless
they have the cash in hand to plank down on the spot *
and because all these things favor the restriction of the
success of Ikey Mo, sport’s greatest parasite. It is idle to
hope that the totalisator, ostensibly fair and safe as it is
will extinguish the hungry man with the large lungs and
nose, because the whole history of betting evidences the
conceit and stupidity of backers in general. But the ex-
thieves, confidence-men, “guns” and touts, which in
such great measure make up the tribe of Ikey, will receive
a big set-back all the same.
* * » *
Doubts as to Newhaven’s staying power induced
“bookies” to generously take a slight shade of odds
about his chance for the Spring Stakes (R miles) in which
he was opposed by a field of seven fair performers. The
son of Newminster, however, soon set these doubts at
rest by winning with ridiculous ease by three lengths in
the fast time, 2min. 37sec. The shapely chestnut is a
gem of the first water, and the V.R.C. Derby looks at his
mercy—unless Resolute has trained on into the smasher
his admirers allege. Newhaven’s impost in Melb. Cup is
7st. 131 b., but he is such a good colt that he mav possibly
emulate the “ double ” deeds of Grand Flaneur and
Martini Henry.
The A. J.C. Derby was a simple farce resolving
itself into a six-furlong sprint in which Charge just
managed by a head on the post to pip Coil, who ran the
last five furlongs in decidedly faster time than did the
winner. In a quick-run race Coil may do much better
and his V.R.C. Derby chance is not altogether hopeless’
though he has to meet such a topsawyer as Newhaven’
The jockeys, being hampered with waiting orders, started
off at a hack-canter, and the mile and a-half took 3min.
14sec. To avert such ridiculous exhibitions a time limit
had better be instituted as in Melb. Champion Stakes.
Poor time, poor money !
The honors of Epsom Handicap belong to the
two “ponies,” Steward and Dalmeny, who fought out an
exciting- finish. The hot favorite, Hopscotch, never
flattered the hopes o! his backers, whilst the erstwnue
Melb. Cup favorite, Reka, only managed to struggle home
amongst the first dozen. Backers were all out m their
calculations, the prophets were floored to a man, ana
Ikey Mo and Co. had a real good race. At the Clubs, on
the eve of the race, 20 to 1 went begging about the
winner, and 12 to 1 was on offer against Dalmeny.
Condition is everything, and to their excellent
fettle Steward and Dalmeny owe their forward positions
in Epsom. Le Yar, Reka, and Hopscotch can all be made
lbs. better by work and time. The latter, when lie won
last year, was trained to the hour, and he ran his best
races at this meeting, Melb. attempts proving gruesome
failures. This year things may alter. Bst. 71b. is a handy
weight for the son of Grand Flaneur to carry in Melb.
Cup, and, despite his disgrace last year, he may be very
prominent this time. Racing is a curious game.
Charge and Cartridge have begun the season
well for their sire, the expatriated Carbine, and when
Cartridge has finished his racing career he should prove a
worthy substitute at the stud for ’Stralia’s_ departed idol,
as his breeding on dam’s (St. Odille’s) side is all that could
be desired. Another son of Carbine called Pinfire (out of
Needle) is in training at Caulfield, and is spoken of as
shaping splendidly ; but, being very unfinished and back-
ward, he is not to be raced till the autumn.
The popular fancy for Melb. Cup, Toreador,
completely disgraced himself in the Trial Stakes on Satur-
day. The winner, Rackarock (Grandmaster-Wildfire),
may be dangerous about Cup-time, as he has privately
shown his party that he can stay, and 7st. 131 b. is not
much for a good 6-year-old.
By grasping at the shadow the Clark syndicate
possibly threw away the substance, for had Le Var been
started in the Derby to ensure a strong pace for Coil, the
son of Abercorn might have won. By the way, Le Yar
was badly served in the Epsom, and, under the circum-
stances, his performance (third) was full of merit.
Mr. Sam Hordern’s English purchases, the two
St. Simon sires Haut Brion and Gigue, arrived in Sydney,
and were duly quarantined on Monday last. Gigue is only
four years old, and, through his dam, has the famous
Stockwell and Scottish Chief blood. A beautifully-bred
two-year-old filly by Prince Rudolph (by Prince Charlie,
sire of Lochiel) from Angola by Macheath (one of the
highest-tried and best-performing two-year-olds ever
raced in England), and another well-bred mare, Meridian,
accompany the stallions. •By the way, Haut Brion is sure
to be known as “ O’Brien,” and his stock will no doubt be
named Dooley, Finnigan, O’Hegarty, and so on.
Duhallow is doing good work at Caulfield, and
his crowd think that he will avenge Tim Swiveller’s un-
lucky disqualification by winning Caulfield Cup.
* * * *
The Metropolitan was yet another boil-over—
Skipper, the winner, which went out at very long odds,
beating True Blue by half-a-length. Owner Hordern must
have had a big win.
In reserving Response for the Metropolitan, in
which she ran third, instead of runningher for the Epsom,
Mr. O’Brien apparently made a mistake, as on her recent
running she would have about landed the Epsom.
To-day (Saturday) Toreador may show up better
than he did last week in the Trial Stakes as the Rand-
wick Plate, three miles, will be more to his liking. Should
he win, his early Melb. Cup backers will once more take
heart.
* * * »
The news so freely bruited about that champion
Wallace has gone amiss is not unexpected to those who
saw the gruellings he got at the last V.R.C. and A. J.O.
Autumn Meetings. Horses are but horses, and not
machines.
* * * *
On the day of the Epsom the best price to be got
in Melb. about Wild Olive was 6 to 1, Dalmeny fours, and
there were tons of money laid out at these prices. “Vat a
marchin ” for the Melb. layers to work on through their
pals in Sydney!
* * * *
Not the first time this year that a steward has
upset a strong public fancy. On a certain Australian
suburban course The Bulletin wots of, if the stewards’
fancy earn get within half a length of the actual winner,
those “in the know” back the actual second to get the
race, and are almost invariably justified by the verdict.
* * * *
Cartridge, on his breeding, ought to be the
fastest nnler in Australia. By Carbine-St. Odille, a mare
that always raced away in front of the field, and never
began to come back to them till the 8-furlong post was
passed.
* * * *
It never rains but it pours. Millers lost about
a score of races in succession, then won a treble and
double at two consecutive meetings.
- Pickles for Caulfield Cup ?
* * * *
R. H. Walne, a Brisbane man, sft. Bin. high
lOst 61b. weight, and 23 years of age, shared with Davison
the honors of the recent N.S.W. cycling carnival. He is
a bridge carpenter by trade ; began cycling 11 years ago,
but did not race until 12 months since. When Martin
(Martin once more!—can’t be helped) was beating all
Q ; > Walne, with 110 yd. start, was beaten by the great
Bill in the mile by a few inches on the post. And he
rode two or three other plucky seconds to Martin
Walne’s biggest prize-money was £l2 for first place
m the three-mile race on St. Patrick’s Day in Brisbane
until he annexed one of the £5O prizes at the last big
Sydney meeting. His machine, geared to 92 and weigh-
ing 261 b., is the work of a Brisbane maker.
* * * *
Tasmania has two Axemen’s Associations—one
a limited co. of axemen and others, the other a co of
limited axemen and others. Points of difference are
hardly discernible at a distance, as both advertise bin-
chopping prizes in Oct. and Nov.
* * * *
Melbourne cyclists thus far have not been able
to wreak much iegal vengeance upon the larrikin w'ho
runs them down with or without malice aforethought.
The other Sabbath a drunken fellow drove his vehicle
against a girl on a bike, injuring the damsel and destroy-
ing the machine. A fine of £lO was considered to meet
the requirements of that case. Later a race between two
milk carts resulted in the demolition of another bike and
the crippling of the rider for life, it is said. The boy jehu
responsible for the damage was let off with a IDs. fine
for the sake of the alleged aged mother dependant on his
wild career. In this case the soft-hearted magistrates
might as well have sent round the hat on behalf of the
cripple who, also the sole support of a mother, employed
his bike in his daily business. But, perhaps, the average
foot passenger in Melbourne has .so many narrow escapes
from being hiked whilst crossing streets that he feels
little sympathy with his natural enemy even when the
enemy is brought to undeserved grief. The bicvcle is
made for the roadway, but the road wasn’t created for
“scorching” purposes. At present a cvclist, being
tolerated rather than sanctified, rides at his own peril in
a great measure, as well as at the peril of pedestrians—
and W. R. Wilson’s racehorses.

* * * *
The starting-machine is good, but it needs
rational administration. Thusfar—in Victoria, at any
rate—the starter who directs the business has been bound
down to the rule that the machine can do no wrong, and
the most absurd accidents have happened now and then
to destroy the chances of certain horses. At Caulfield,
the other Saturday, an impetuous animal butted at the
rope just as the barrier was ascending, and held one end
of it down for a moment. The other end of the barrier
went up all right enough, and most of the field departed
as per usual, leaving at the post four unfortunates that
couldn’t get away. It stands to reason that the starter
should have authority to call them back in such a case
but under present arrangements he is a mere dummy star-
ing at the injustice he has innocently wrought. The
notorious suspension of Chris. Moore for “ deliberately
getting left at the post ” on Quiver in the V.R.C. Oaks',
was brought about by the starting-machine. The thin<>
was then a novelty at Flemington. Quiver thrust her nose
into the rope barrier, and played up considerably, and
when the barrier rose her head was turned the wrong way
Everybody in the stand supposed that the start was a
false one, and Moore seemingly expected the rest of the
field to be called back to Quiver. But old George Watson
had no say in the matter, it seemed. A machine-made
start is final. When the barrier goes up, properly or
otherwise, the race has commenced and nobody can stop
it. If the first half-dozen favorites for a Melb. Cup were
to get entangled in the ropes—well, their owners and
backers would be in the soup without legal <>Tound for
protest. °

The bicycle is diminishing the receipts of racing
and football clubs, and is likely to diminish them some
more. Also it will tend to knock out Saturday-afternoon
billiards.
9|C •£
The second half-yearly vol. of J. J. Miller’s
Australian Racing Chronicle—Feb. to July—contains
full details of 700 meetings, nearly twice as many as the
more expensive Australasian Turf Register included for
the whole year 1895-96. As evidence that Miller’s up-to-
date Chronicle, published in monthly and half-yearly
numbers, fills a long-felt want, a correspondent furnishes
some particulars re a racing-sire named Sunrise. The
Australasian Register credited Sunrise aforesaid with 26
winners of 48 races (value £1695) for the whole of last
year. Miller’s books, covering the same period, reveal
Sunrise as the sire of 46 winners of 144 races, 104 seconds
and 90 thirds, totalling £3OOO in stakes. Little things
like this explain why the Argus refuses to advertise the
Racing Chronicle in its respectable columns.
* * * *
“ Do racing-men live long ? ” asks a bookmakers
journalist in an Ikey-Mo organ. After which he proceed'
to mention a few early-rising sports of mature age, and
argues therefrom that a successful gambling career is ex-
ceedingly healthy. (Men aren’t vigorous because thev
rise early—they rise early because they are vigorous.) Of
course, racing-men, as a rule, don’t live long if they have
a constantly exciting time. Neither do brokers—of shares
sugar, spices, or any other goods—make very old bones!
Speculation leads to drink, and drink increases speculation
and both things are bad for the nervous system, and all
nervousness points deathward, and—well, the gambler
stands to get cut down like a cauliflower. The average
parson can give a bookmaker 20 years and a beating.
Preaching is a healthy pursuit.

N. : “I hold football to be essentially a nobler
game than cricket, and am not to be choked off by a mere
temporary irruption of larrikindom and barrakinage
True, football is made up of self-denial, but in cricket the
player, in action, is always under the electric-light,
whether batting, bowling or fielding.”
* * * *
When the famed athlete, Donald Dinnie, first
struck Ballarat, he was accorded a wondrous welcome by
the Scoatish community, every member of which left his
pants at home in honor of the occasion and swelled tbe
air with “ Hech gathers” and “We are nae fou ” foi
hours at a stretch. But the hero of the occasion re-
sponded very little to the perfervid enthusiasm, and re-
ceived the homage of the crowd without as much as a
nod of acknowledgment. At last, when the reception
committee approached him, he condescended to admit
that it was braw the day, and on the chairman proposing
a visit to the nearest bar, Donald acquiesced without a
murmur. “We weell dreenk to the health o’ the greatest
o Scoatish wroslers,” announced the chairman to every-
body’s surprise, for he was locally noted as being' a very
“ adjacent” Caledonian indeed. “ What’ll ye be takken,
Mister Deenee ? ” “ Wull,” mused Donald, stroking his
square jaw, “ A’m theenkin’ A’ll be takkin a sma’ bottle
o’ champagne.” The chairman nearly fell down, but he
paid-up like a little man, though his belief in the athletes
of his native land was shattered for evermore.

Altogether, the cyclist bites as often as he is
bitten in Sydney city traffic, and when he adopts the
ammonia pistol, the facetious driver of a vehicle will
smile at a respectful distance, instead of urging his horse
to climb upon the cyclist’s back. But what a bad time
the average Sydney citizen gets ! He is the “game” of
the scorcher, the driver, the spitter, the stick or parasol-
flourisher, the smoker, the ehewer, and the leprous-
handed person who mauls the loaf at luncheon.

Dear Bulletin, —Riding along a macadamised
road in the “ cookies’ country,” up the western line, a
peculiar, and to us a novel, “ track” was observable. My
companion, a mulga-fed cattle-man, for the first time
emerged from the scrubs to the west of the Q’sland
Warrego, was mightily puzzled. Horse, cattle, emu,
gohanna—or any decent tracks —you couldn’t have put
him wrong about. But this slewed him. “ It’s the denied
sea-serpent come ashore !”he at last decided. He had
read a newspaper or two in his time. The trail of a
pneumatic tyre is a weird affair to the back-blocks bush-
man when he drops on it for the first time.—Breaker.
* * * *
Dear Bulletin, —“ K.” is right about roping.
In Lahore I saw an officer and a gentleman (!) prepared
with a vet.’s certificate that his mare had ophthalmia and
could not run. He had been unable to buy her in the
lotteries. The public (army men) insisted on his allowing
her to go and letting S ,of the 82nd, ride her. She
won easily. The same o. and g. rode for me once, and
was 10 lengths ahead at the distance and 20 behind
at the winning-post. He had then bought the winner in
the sweeps the night previous to the race. I think he is
now a retired colonel, and still an officer and a gentleman.
I am not.— S. the W.
* * * *
Nat. Thompson, the old-time N.S.W. .cricketer
just dead, and Dave Gregory, another old-timer, now high
up in N.S.W. Civil Service, once had a tiff. Dave had a
peculiar style of bowling—half a throw it was called. Nat.
was standing umpire in a match on the old Albert Ground,
Dave bowling. Nat. “no-balled” him once! twice!!
thrice .. ! Round went Dave’s arm for another bowl.
No ball,” cried Nat. “ Why,” said Dave, “ I haven’t
bowled yet”—which was a fact. He made pretence of
bowling, but held the ball fast. Nat. umpired no more
that day.

In the States, supply has overtaken demand in
cycles; consequently, prices are declining, and a few
makers failing.
* * * *
At an Ulmarra(N.S.W.) pony-meeting, Aug. 23,
sornfe of the cronkest running ever seen in the district
took place, and the committee let things slide. Some of
the settling was also very crooked. One punter so badly
battered the features of a bookie who failed to ante-up
that the erring one’s own mother would not have known
him.
* * * *
A cycling-school advt. says, “Avoid accidents
and ridicule. Learn to ride properly.” Wily. The solid
dread of ridicule is a much more important matter than
the hazy fear of accident. All is vanity, thinks the pro-
fessor.
* * * *
Dear Bulletin, — Be a Bulletin par. saying that
boat-builder Dan Shea was 40 years before his day. The
Arabs can give even Dan a bit of a start. Last year, at
Aden, I saw an old dhow hauled up on the beach, built
after the shape which has not been altered a hair’s-breadth
by the Arabs for the last thousand years. Razee the
cumbersome poop, bolt on the lead, and there was the
exact model of the modern English yacht. Truly, there
is nothing new under the sun.—W.
* * * *
Jran Mace is still a factor in the business of the
much-barmaided Melbourne pub. which he ran long years
ago. Every now and then an inferior new-chum johnnie,
or Cockney spieler, swaggers into Jem’s old shop with a
benediction from the ex-bruiser, who has adjured the
visitor, before leaving London, to make this his first place
of call. The pub. seems a queer sort of Mecca for pil-
grims nowadays, but in Mace’s time it was occasionally a
scene of prodigal expenditure. Gilded youths, excited
by the proud consciousness of having known the boss
“at Honie, doncherknow,” would order champagne ad
lib., whereupon Jem would pour the contents of many
bottles into a bucket until it was so full that the crowd,
kneeling around, were able to drink, horse-fashion, with-
out wetting their noses. Jem artfully insisted that the
special glory of the draught lay in their ability to imbibe
without tilting the bucket or wetting their noses, to which
end it was necessary to keep the river of fizz running a
banker —just as long as the gilded youths would supply
the funds.

A Hobart cabman was lately “ pulled ” for
not walking round the corner. “Now, do you
mean to tell me,” said the prosecutor, “that you
have been driving in Hobart for 17 years and can-
not swear that you knew there was a sign on that
corner ordering drivers to ‘walk round the
corner . “ Well,” said accused, “I ’aven’t been
drivin round that corner for 17 years.”
At Stawell (Vic.), the other day, an inquest
was held on the body of a poor tramp, found dead
somewhere or other, and the doctor certified that
pneumonia was the cause of his passing hence.
Ihe only property left by the deceased was a
microscopic swag containing a pair of slippers and
a knife and fork. At this point the coroner ex-
plained to the jurymen that it was their duty to
decide as to the cause of death, and that they might
ask any questions which would tend to elucidate
matters. Then the fat, heavy juryman upheaved
himself and inquired : “ What was the color of the
handle of the fork found in the man’s swag ? ”
A male mother is an extreme rarity. Re-
cently a Paris medical student, 17 years old, re-
ceived a slight blow in the navel region of the
abdomen, and a swelling formed which in two
years reached the size of a child’s head. An
operation by Mayde and Sanger brought to light
a female foetus apparently about five months old.
The head was rather badly deformed, but the
limbs were almost regular in shape. The skin was
somewhat wrinkled and thickened, but the hair
was regularly localised and actually a foot long.
This was one of those extremely rare cases of
twins in which one is absolutely enclosed in the
other. The gradations of twinning are separate
twins, Siamese twins, twins divided from the
waist up or from the waist down, two-headed
person, included twin as in this case. The 'Jour-
nal DE Medecine DE Paris (a periodical equal in
standing to the Lancet or the British Medical
Journal) is the authority for the above.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*
“ White-Eye ” to The Bulletin :—“ One
of the largest S.A. cattle-stations has lately, from
motives of economy, given up the old style of
branding calves in a yard, and taken to the Yankee
style of branding them in the bush. The lasso is
fastened to the horse by an ordinary pack-surcingle,
buckled tight around the horse’s girth over the
saddle. (It is surprising how a small horse, with
a man on his back, can hold a full-grown beast.)
Pour posts are put in the ground—two long ones
and two short ones. The long ones are put in
about 3ft. 6in. apart, and the short ones about 4ft.
or sft. from the long ones, all in a straight line.
When the man with the lasso has caught a calf he
drags it up towards the posts and rides between
the two long posts till the calf is level with them.
The calf is then leg-roped and thrown in the usual
way. When it is branded and the ropes taken off
the fun begins. If the calf happens to be a big
Mick, you will see the men working the leg-ropes
all rushing for the two long posts, and, in their
hurry, they generally make for the same post with
the Mick at their heels. The new style has not
caught on in the district, and I don’t think it will
last where they are using it, as it makes cattle j
very wild.” |

Walter Scott, cousin of the novelist, was
a middy, and with two others had a spree at a
low-down Portsmouth groggery, compiling such a
score that the landlady locked them up. She
would only let them go on condition of marriage
with one, because she wanted a certificate. They
cast lots, and Walter won, or lost. He married
her. At Jamaica he was overjoyed with the news
of her having been hanged.
A BEAUTIFUL COMPLEXION.
Apply Sulpholine Lotion. It drives away

Is. bottles. Made in London.*
After a long and acrimonious discussion of
the qualifications of competing architects, a Vic.
congregation not long ago built a church which
may be simply described as a six-sided building
with many turrets, a high roof, a sloping floor, and
seats circled round the pulpit. The night before
the opening somebody painted on the new notice-
board in flaring red: “THIS CYCLORAMA
WILL BE OPENED TO-DAY WITH THE
SIEGE OF HELL,” which was only the begin-
ning of trouble. The worshippers found that
unless the church was full it was almost impossible
for a preacher to be heard. The regular minister
was the only one who could make anything like a
decent oratorical appearance. Unfortunate visitors
were staggered to find the building flinging back at
their heads the beginning of a trisyllable word
when they were uttering its last syllable. They
usual’y became annoyed and spoke loudly
to drown the echo; which merely resulted in
other echoes crowding to aid the first one and
piling themselves on to the perspiring parson
till there was none of his voice left. Each
member of the vestry had a different theory
a ; to the cause of the echo, and proposed an ap-
propriate remedy. W i res were stretched across
the vaulted ceiling until it looked like the interior
of a telephone exchange; curtains were hung
round the walls—the echo came up smiling. The
architect disclaimed all responsibility ; according to
architectural laws, he said, the church was of per-
fect acoustic construction. And, with every seat
occupied, the acoustics were perfect. But the
parson and the vestry couldn’t fill ’em. Hiring an
audience was suggested ; but, as it was not to be
supposed that the subsidised portion of the con-
gregation would return their subsidy to the offer-
tory, this plan was out of the range of ecclesiasti-
cal economics. But the other day a bright genius
evolved an alternative scheme of filling all the
seats with indiarubber dummies. These dummies
might be manufactured to represent very pious,
bald-headed old gentlemen. Five hundred could
be procured' at a reasonable cost. Each dummy
must be made collapsable, and when the wind had
been taken out of him he could be folded up upon
the seat, thus making a cushion for the intending
worshipper. The organ bellows could be utilised
to pump air into main pipes beneath the floor ;
from these mains, smaller pipes would carry the
air up from under the seats to each figure. Half-
an-hour or so before service the beadle must con-
nect the organ bellows with the main, and start
the gas-engine, when a magnificent congregation
of bald-headed old gentlemen would rise like one
man and kill the echo. Seat-holders coming in
had only to turn a convenient tap at the back of a
bald-headed gentleman’s neck, place a hand upon
his head, gently squash him out of existence, and
sit upon his collapsed carcase. The ushers in
similar manner could make room for strangers.
It was true the dummies could give no col-
lection, but neither could they put buttons
in the plate, nor display any inattention
nor sleepiness during the sermon. It might
be objected that they could not rise to
sing, but that difficulty might easily be obviated
by the whole congregation remaining seated
thoughout the service, and there would always be
a good congregation to cheer the parson’s heart—
not a single seat would ever be vacant. When
The Bulletin mail left, the vestrymen were on
the point of adopting this ingenious solution of
their acoustic difficulty ; and news of its success is
anxiously awaited.

1 AND OTHERS.
Increase your yields by Saving the Fine Gold
and Pyrites by the use of
Wynne and Tregurtha’s
Patent Battery Plates.
This Invention, lately patented, revolutionises the ordi-
nary battery treatment of ores, for by its use copper-
plates, quicksilver, and chemicals are entirely dispensed
with. The Wynne and Tregurtha Patent Battery Plates
are made of iron, cast with peculiarly-shaped ribs. They
are fitted in a wooden frame, and take the place of the
ordinary copper-plates and blanket tables. The tailings
on leaving the stamper boxes pass over the plates which,
from their construction, catch the fine gold and pyrites,
allowing the sand and water to flow awaj'. When the
plates are fully charged with the concentrates they are
then tilted backwards by means of a lever, throwing the
contents into a trough underneath. In practical use
much better results have been obtained by these plates
than by the ordinary copper ones, the yield of gold being
increased by fully 10 per cent. Excellent results have
been achieved in the treatment of refractory ores, where
the gold is associated with some foreign mineral, which
has proved deleterious to the mercury and causing the
loss of the gold. The plates are strong, simple in con-
struction, and easily handled, and can be obtained from
