The Last of the Pirates,
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“One of Crocky Dixon’s lot, eh? ’Pon
my soul, it’s very kind of Gorman to say so—
especially as the old villain himself’s got a lot
more the cut of a buccaneer than I have. ”
f looked up at the bridge, and compared the
bull - roaring skipper fiery- faced and shock-
bearded—witli the man beside me—lean, clean,
bi'own, and civil.
“Appearances don’t go for much,” I said.
“ Crocky himself, I understand ”
“ Didn’t look the part, eh ? Well, that’s so.
Ladies’ hosiery, or barber’s clerk—that was about
his mark, by the outside of him ! ”
“You did know him, then? ”
The lean man stretched himself out in his deck-
chair. “ Well, yes. In fact, I was in a sense, as
Gorman says, one of Crocky’s lot. Much against
my will, though. And I was with him at the
end.”
“At the end! Why—but perhaps you’re
Kirby?”
“Mr. Adam Kirby, sir ! —at your service.”
“Oh! of course. But, you see, you’re histori-
cal.”
“Or you want to make me so—isn’t that it?
But you’ve heard the story before?”
“ In half-a-dozen versions. That’s why I’d be
glad of a first-hand account.”
Kirby looked at his watch. “Got to muster
my coolies on deck in half-an-hour. Just about
time for the yarn. (Hi! steward !) What’ll you
take?”
* » » *
“You see, Crocky wanted to be up to
date. That old schooner of his was a wonder to
sail ; but, now that both Dutch and English
cruisers were on the look-out for him, he thought
it was time he had a smoke-pot of his own. I
needn’t go over the nabbing of the Hija de la Mar.
The Straits papers had that all right enough
except that they said she was a good lump of a
steamer. She was only a 60ft. Barrow - built
launch, sent out for Pareras of Manila. / ought
to know! ”
“Yes ; you were her engineer, weren’t you ? ”
“ Engineer of a hooker like that ! Not likely.
But I was going to Mindando, myself, and Jose
Parera asked me to run the Hija down for him.
And how Crocky nailed us and marooned ’em all
but me, of course you know. ”
“ Yes ! But why did he except you ? ”
“ Because there were fixtures about the steam-
gear that puzzled him—new-fangled touches that
made him think he’d got hold of a 20-knotter or
thereabouts. Sixteen she was credited with on
the measured mile, but 14 was all I could knock
out of her, with Crocky’s pistol at my ear to help
me. ‘ And that’s her top-notch, is it ? ’ says he at
last. ‘ With better coal,’ I told him, ‘ and a few
inches more down-by-the-stern, I might squeeze
another knot out of her—not more.’ ‘ Call it 13,
as a general thing,’he says. ‘Too much for the
Dutchie, or for anything the Britishers hev got in
these hyar waters.’ ‘Yes; but what about the
Spaniards ? ’ I says. ‘ You’ve put your foot in it
propexly now with them too.’ ‘ Shucks ! ’he says,
and walks away aft, full of a notion that many a
Yankee and Johnny Bull has come to grief over
before now. Got the same idea yourself, as like as
not! ”
“ And that is ? ”
“ That the Spaniards are poor, God-forsaken
creatures—not much account, afloat or ashore!
Anyhow, I could see Dixon knew nothing of the
new 18-knot cruiser that had arrived at Manila the
day we left it. She’d be after us by that time, I
expected; but, of course, I said nothing to
Ci-ocky. Sixteen of his men he had with him ;
the best, I suppose, in his estimation —which
meant pretty well the biggest scoundrels. And
the nearest thing to a white man among ’em was a
bi k- cross-eyed Portuguese, with a touch of the
tar-brush.
“ About latitude we were in; and, as we
headed away due east, I fancied Dixon was
making for the Carolines. I kept looking him
over, as he sat on the skylight witn a yellow-back
novel and a bottle of poor Parera’s Medoc, and I
couldn’t bring myself to believe he was such a
savage devil as he was made out. But I soon got
an eye-opener.
“We fell in with a little green-painted brig, head-
ingnor’-west— cocoa-nut oil, I heard afterwards, she
had, from the islands for Canton—and Crocky
runs alongside and boards her with all his cut-
throats but two. What happened in the brig’s
cabin, I don’t exactly know—or wbat Crocky took
from the tomato-faced old skipper, but, when we
had cast off and the brig had filled her maintop-
sail again, the skipper runs to the taffrail and
shakes his fist at Crocky. * You infernal Yankee
robber ! ’ he yells— ‘ you’ll pay for this, as sure as
my name’s’ what his name was I didn’t hear,
for just then Crocky fired. The old chap stood for
a bit with his fist stretched out—then down he
went in a heap. ‘ Thought I’d missed, didn’t
you ? ’ Dixon says to me, in a voice like a girl at
a tea-party, but I could see, from the looks of his
men, that his claws were out.
“ Presently he walks up to the chap at the wheel
—a half-caste Dutchman, by the look of him—
and not well used, I could see, to have the spokes
of a steamer in his flippers. Dixon just looks at
the compass, and, without a single word, catches
the man a crasher on the head with his revolver-
butt. Then he looks round—the smallest man
aboard, with a pleasant little smile on his smooth
little face. ‘ Take the wheel. Miguel,’ he says to
the big Portuguese—-‘and heave this thing over
the side, a couple of you.’ Now, the man was
only stunned—l could see his chest heaving as
they got him up on the rail, and I called out
before I could help it, ‘ Good God ! he’s not dead ! ’
Next second a bullet scored the top of the topee
I had on, and, says Crocky—smiling more beauti-
fully than ever—‘ Six inches lower next time.’
“ Well, sir, there must be some such thing as
Fate, after all. I believe it was this man’s doom
that drove him into such sudden ferocity. At all
events, it was the shooting of the red-faced skipper
that brought the cruiser down on us. The mate
of the brig kept her away from the Philippines to
report the matter, and what does he drop across,
six hours later, but the Spanish war-ship, pegging
away to the s’uth’ard, on the chance that Dixon
would make for some out-of-the-way hole in
northern Gilolo. Away comes the Don, then—
dead in our wake, with every ounce of steam he
could carry ; and, sure enough, next morning at
sunrise there was a little inky smear going up out
of the blue behind us. Crocky hardly took his
eye off it for an hour, and when he found it was
rising and spreading, he comes to me and says—
‘ Drive her ! ’ Not a word more; but you bet I
did drive her for all she was worth,
The narrator pulled up to irrigate, and I said —
“Couldn’t you have sent something astray with
your engines, on the quiet ? ”
“Wouldn’t be sitting here, yarning, to-day, if
I had ! Crocky knew a heap too much for that!
And, anyhow, it wasn’t needed. The stranger
steering for our smoke, of course—hove herself up
so fast over the horizon that I knew she must be
the new cruiser, the Cid Campeador. But still I
said nothing, and when Crocky handed me the
glass—saying he didn’t know her—l said I didn’t,
either. ‘ She kin travel a few—whatever she is ! ’
I heard him mutter. ‘ Keep her at it ! ’ he says to
me, with a tap on his pistol. ‘ And you, Miguel—
steady as you go ! ’ Then he went below.
“I suppose he still thought he’d an off-chance
of making the Carolines; in fact, I fancied,
myself, it was about time we were sighting some
of the little outlying western islands. But the
way the Don overhauled us was a caution, and, as
soon as he was near enough to show us the white-
ness about his forefoot, he let drive at us. ‘ Long
way short, my boy ! ’ I says to myself, but I
hadn’t reckoned on the latest touch in guns—the
shot pitched a good half-mile ahead of us ! Up
comes Crocky when he hears the boom, and down
he smashes his glass on deck when he makes out
the stranger’s ensign. ‘ Great Jefferson ! ’ he says
—‘ yellow-bellies, after all! ’ He took a turn or
two fore and aft, and I noticed that his pockets
were bulging with something that made him very
heavy in the walk. Then comes another shot—
not 10 feet above our smoke-stack —and at the
screech of it Crocky seems to go clean mad. He
pulls a seven-shot Colt out of each bi-east pocket—
fires at Miguel—and, as the big Portuguese falls
over the wheel, whips round and pots five of his
other men before the rest could rush him. A long
buck-nigger was the first to handle the little demon,
but, as he lay under the black man, he managed
somehow to fire another shot that set the man’s
shirt on fire and blew away part of his jaw, In
the middle of the shooting 1 had stopped the en-
gines, and, when I turned round, there was the
big nigger—with the blood pouring from his
smashed face —just forcing Crocky over the rail.
His pockets sunk him like lead.” 1
“ What was in them ? ”
“ Dollars ! I fancy he intended to sink himself
with them, anyhow, after he’d polished off his
whole crew. But now there were nine of ’em
left, and when they missed the throb of the screw
they came for me like one man. But I fought ’em
with what no man can face —steam ! —and scalded
three of them so badly that they jumped over-
board and sank within twenty yards of the cruiser’s
boat, pulling up hard. Two of the others fired
into her, and killed a seaman at his oar. That’s
what made the garotte a certainty for them, in
any case ! ”
“ Had a middling close shave of it yourself, by
some accounts?”
Kirby laughed as he jumped up. “ Not quite
so bad as that. But, you see, I’d been seventy-
odd hours without sleep or shaveI—thick 1 —thick with
grease and coal-dust, besides ; no wonder the
Spanish lieutenant took me for what I looked
like. But when I got aboard the Cid it was all
right. The captain spoke English, and as soon as
I had got freshened up a bit, he sent me back
with half-a-dozen of his marineros to run the
Hija into Manila.
“ And, after I’d given evidence against the five
remaining picaroons—who, by the way, were all
duly scragged—l asked for this ’’—Kirby lugged
out a long Colt “ navy ” —“ as a little memento of
the ‘ Last of the Pirates.’ ”
ALEX. MONTGOMERY.
At Maryborough (Q.)Police-court. Colored
gentleman was charged with drunkenness and
pleaded guilty. P.M. : “ How is it that you get
drunk when you are a Buddhist ? That is con-
trary to your religious belief. Are you a Bud-
dhist ? ” Prisoner : “ No, lam a Christian.” The
P.M. : “ Well, I suppose you are at liberty to get
drunk if you are a Christian ! You are dis-
charged.” John left the courthouse smiling : he
had found there was something in Christianity,
after all.
Dear Bulletin,— Talking about the luck
of drunken men. Brown Jones was a north Clunes
miner, a Durham beer-sinker; he was in the habit of
going home drunk, and falling down a precipitous
and rocky hill on the way to save a walk round. He
fell down this hill probably a dozen times without
sustaining any injuries worth mentioning, when
under the elevating influence. When sober Brown
Jones never took the hill route, regarding it as
dangerous, till one evening, being in a particular
hurry, he attempted it without the usual buoyancy
imparted by Ballarat beer. He slipped and fell,
and was carried home badly broken, and the local
members of the Royal Orange Lodge had a full-
dress outing at his funeral on the following Sun-
day.—Yours, Snell. P.S.— The temperance
societies are at liberty to make what use they
please of this anecdote. —S.
At the Army.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The excitement was at its height. Captain Mac-
intosh had finished singing “Nearer, my God, to
Thee,” with acrobatic leaps at the conclusion ; but
Lieutenant Hilda Smith was still swinging her
arms about, and inviting the boys to chuck then-
sovereigns to their Saviour. The body of the hall
was a dim mass, lit up by waving handkerchiefs
and alive with yelling voices. It was a friendly,
appreciative audience at the Army that night, not
a gathering of “ toffs ” come to grin and contemn.
“ God will take away your sins,” cried Captain
Macintosh, walking up and down ; “ God ’ll do it
right away ; there’s nothing impossible to God.”
Suddenly a new speaker leapt fx-om among the
flags and cymbals at the rear of the platform.
“ Glory to God!” he shouted : “ I’m six months
old to-day ! ”
He was a middle-aged man, small-made, but
evei-y inch of him alive. His step was firm, his
eyes alight with l-eligious fervor, but to the
initiated gaze there was a world of meaning in
the straggling black beard, and in the loose folds
of his ill-fitting blue jei-sey.
“Thank God for Private Jackson!” cried the
captain, and clapped him on the shoulder. Jack-
son laughed wildly, and the cymbals clanged once
more.
He turned to the audience again.
“ And why did I say I was only six months old
to-day ? Because I was dead all the years before
that, dead in my sins. But glory, glory, glory !
now I’m saved for evermore.”
“ Hallelujah ! ” shouted the captain.
“Oh ! I was a great sinner befoi-e that. Oh !
the sins I’ve done. There was one—the blackest
of them all—l won’t tell you what it was. I did
it five years ago; and oh, God ! how I’ve wept
and groaned over that sin ever since I was born
again ! ”
Suddenly his voice changed. It sank from the
usual, high-pitched Salvation tones to a lower and
a different key altogether. The effect was almost
instantaneous; in a minute the hall was like a
church.
“ Do you know it wasn’t enough for God to for-
give me that great sin ? That’s flow sinners feel,
brethren. It wasn’t near enough that He should
foi-give me. He had to make it so that it had
never been done. As Captain Macintosh has told
you, nothing is impossible to God.”
Not a sound in the hall, only a vague whisper-
ing among the lasses on the platform. Private
Jackson bent forward as if addressing the audi-
ence confidentially.
“And He did, brethren. He has turned the
whole world back five years, and so I have not
sinned that sin. Praise be to God, it is five
years, five years ago.” He straightened himself
up and shouted, with suddenly waving arms,
“All the world’s been turned back for one poor
sinner: yesterday it was 1896, and to-night it’s
1891.”
Then Captain Macintosh regained his presence
of mind, and signed the band to start up “ Sweet
Marie. ”
They had always suspected Jackson of being
“a bit off,” but it was his unorthodoxy, not his
insanity, that caused them to lay his case before
the medical authorities next day.
Maoriland.
C. CLYDE.
In one N. Queensland town it is a sort of
tradition that the local Freemasons must always
drink at a certain hotel, and no other. The origin
of the tradition is lost to the younger generation
of Masons, but the old ones tell how, years ago,
the local Grand Chief Goat, or some other poten-
tate, went into that hotel in a fearsome state of
hiccup, and backed the publican up into a corner,
and proceeded to initiate him at a fast pace into
all the different degrees in succession. He was
nearly through with the job before he was dis-
covered and removed in disgrace, and then it be-
came necessary to silence the pub.-keeper. He re-
fused to join the order, but promised inviolable
secresy if the Masons always drank at his pub.
thereafter, and they have drunk there in fear and
trembling ever since. If they stopped drinking
at his pub. the secrets of the order would be given
away to the local paper, and Freemasonry would
burst up for ever.

“Going to the Theatre."
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
She was a brisk little woman, with a wealth
of warm-colored hair, a trim form, and a ravishing
face. And her dress became her admirably.
Altogether a charming little figure. And she said,
“ Now, Number Sixteen, I want you to come to
the theatre with me, please.”
So I went. I rather fancy that I was not
dressed as becomingly as she was. I wore a long
flannel dressing-gown, which flapped uncomfort-
ably about my ankles, and was much too roomy
round the chest, and my hands were quite out of
sight in the sleeves of it. It was decorated with
the inscription “C 3” in big capitals across the
chest. I also wore a pair of No. 10 slippers, and
my thoughts were a good deal distracted by my
efforts to keep my feet inside them.
I had to pass eight or nine of my acquaintances
on my way to join the little woman, and several of
them said “ Good-bye, Sixteen !” Number Thir-
teen —thirteen, ugh!—said “ Good luck, old boy ! ”
To these kindly remarks I replied but briefly, for
my slippers had a little the best of me just then.
The little woman said, “You carry this can up
and I’ll take the other things.” So I took the big
hot-water can in both arms, and hugged it to my
bosom, and she took the other things and led the
way. Through the door, along the verandah, and
across a high-level bridge in the open air, where I
took, perhaps, my final look at the sunshine and
the waving trees and the grass-plots and the
flower-gardens and a little group of convalescents
standing in the sun underneath (from, whom came
the remark, “ There’s another bloke goin’ up from
C 3 ”), we walked in silence.
“ You’re lucky to be first, aren’t you ? ”
I said Yes, I was ; but I was not feeling par-
ticularly thankful, all the same. I did not say so,
however, for the dressing-gown tripped me up just
at that time, and for a few seconds I and the hot-
water can and the slippers and the dressing-gown
were badly mixed. At this episode the idlers
down below laughed, and one man said cheerfully :
“ Seems a bit funky, don’t he ? ”
My guide asked me if I had any false teeth, be-
cause I must take them out if I had, and give
them to her. I hadn’t, however; so we proceeded
without any further interruption, for I had got
used to the slippers by this time. We came
to a lofty hall, then went up a flight of stairs (the
can was getting heavy), up another flight of
stairs, and we were at “the theatre.’ Not
exactly on the stage yet, but in the green-room,
so to speak.
There was a kind of hand-waggon arrangement
here, with a bed on it at a very inconvenient height
from the floor. I had to climb into this as best I
could, and the little woman put the hot-water can
to my feet, and left me. The door through which
she passed was slightly ajar, and I had a good
view of the theatre proper, on the stage of which
I was about to make my first appearance. There
was rather a poor house when I arrived (mine was
the opening performance, you see), but the specta-
tors soon began to flock in and take their seats.
There was plenty of seating accommodation —tier
upon tier of semi-circularly-arranged benches,
reaching right up to the' roof. The audience
seemed to be in rare humor, and chatted and
laughed very cheerily. On the stage proper there
was a goodly array of accessories in the shape of
bottles and wadding and iodoform and basins and
scissors and knives and an electric-battery, and
many other bright and showy articles, but my in-
terest centred gloomily on a nice-looking young
man with a dark moustache and some very pretty
flowers in his buttonhole, who was flirting des-
perately with one of the theatre staff. (The
theatre nurses wear a red sash in addition to the
customary uniform, which strikes an interested
visitor as peculiarly appropriate and artistic —and
cheering.) The young man, I knew, was the
anae.-thetist, for he had been down-stairs earlier
in the day testing my lung and heart capacity,
and he presently came along, in company with
another nice young man, and did it again. He
tlien vanished for a space, and I lay still and
w itched the audience coming in, through the
chink in the curtain—l mean the door. But
someone shut the door, and hid stage and per-
formers and all from my view, and I was just
beginning to wish that my part in the performance-
would begin, or be unavoidably postponed, or
something, when I heard the voice of the young
man with the dark moustache behind me. He
had a funnel-shaped india-rubber thing with him,
the wide-mouthed part of which he put-over my
mouth and nose. He said :
“It may be a little bit disagreeable, but just try
to blow this full of air, will you ? ”
T bh-w. “ That’s right ! Now another ! ” I did
another. “ Now draw a long breath.”
I had to do this, anyway, for my lungs were
now empty, and I began to “tumble ” to the nice
young man’s craftiness. For the long breath I
drew in nearly choked me, and I felt as if I was
drowning. I held out for a long time, so as not to
have to take another swallow like it, but I had to
give in at last, and this time inhaled a rather
worse dose than before. After that, I refrained
from breathing until I nearly exploded, and then
I had to take a big gulp that made me feel as if I
was being smothered under ten thousand feather-
beds scented with druggists’ sundries. I began to
hear a singing as of tree-locusts in summer-time,
continuous and increasing, mingled with the roar-
ing of a far-away ocean. Then a voice a long way
off said, “ Long breaths, and don’t talk 1 ” I was
just about to reply that I had neither desire nor
intention to talk, when the locusts suddenly
stopped singing, and the ocean burst with a loud
bang.
* * * *
Ward () 3 was as bright and cheerful as
ever. Its 32 windows let in plenty of light, and
the furniture and glass and brasswork shone
beautifully. There was a nice fire in the middle
of the room, and half-a-dozen of the lodgers were
sitting round it, yarning. Some of the other
fellows were sitting up in their beds, reading, and
everything looked peaceful and pleasant. But
how the dickens did I come there? How was it I
was in bed ? What was the last thing I remem-
bered ? Where ?
I sat up in bed. “ Hello, you fellows,” I said ;
“ where am I ? ”
“ Sixteen’s woke up,” said the one-legged man
opposite ; “ how are you, old boy ? ”
But a nurse came along. “You lie down at
once,” she said ; “ and don’t move ! ”
She put a pillow under my head (there was none
there) and took away my old friend, the hot-water
can, which I now perceived had been at my feet.
A beautiful creature was serving out the bread
and butter and tea which formed the evening
meal. When she was passing by me, I said—
“ Can’t I have some, nurse ? ”
“Nothing for you till to-morrow, Sixteen,” she
replied, cruelly.
.** * *
There was a rumble along the verandah
outside. “ Here comes Fourteen ! ” said the
loungers round the fire. The elevated bed on
wheels was trundled in, and along to Fourteen’s
quarters. Fourteen was lying very, very still.
His head was thrown back, and his mouth was
open, and his face was very white. There were
two men in charge of his carriage, and they lifted
him off (my goodness, how limp he was !) laid him
on his bed, and rumbled out again with the
waggon. A nurse piled blankets on top of Four-
teen, and put a hot water-can to his feet, and took
away his pillow, and placed a little basin on the
locker at his bed-head. I looked at my bed-head :
there was a similar little basin there—it was use-
ful, as Fourteen could have told you, half-an-hour
later.
And now I knew how I had come back from the
theatre.
A. CHEE.
All things would. be pure to the pure, if
there were any pure.—
“To the pure all things are pure.”
That’s a maxim safe and sure—
Naught seems naughty, wrong, or rude
In the Trilby figure nude
To the eye of humankind
Beaming from a spotless mind :
Ne’er a pose appeared obscene
Till our Parents wore the green.
Uoni soit —you know the rest.
Eve and Adam went undrest,
Innocent of thoughts salacious
(If the Bible be veracious)
Ere the serpentining Devil
Breathed the cursed name of evil;
Then the lady gave a start
And felt she was indeed a “tart.”
“ To the pure all things are pure.”
Truth almighty shall endure,
Holy sir, and righteous madam,
Long as faith in Eve and Adam.
Find a woman, and a man,
Such as were when Time began ;
Set ’em up and ask us whether
They are chaste in “ altogether.”
We will say, with humor grim,
All is pure to her and him.
But that’s a question quite apart
From purity in naked Art.

Dear Bulletin, — Be the Japanese clerk
advts. in the Eastern press. I am staying for a
few days at a Gerald ton (W.A.) hotel which em-
ploys a Japanese clerk, two Jap. cooks, Jap. boots,
Jap. yardman, and Jap. lie-chambermaid. There
are Jap. fruit-shops, carpenter shops, laundries,
Ac., &c., here ; also, several establishments run by
lady Japs., who have almost driven the white
women out of competition in the one avocation to
which the former entirely confines herself in this
country. Many of the Japs, ride cycles, and it is
said they intend going in for football and cricket.
[The only count in the present indictment, so far,
calculated to infuriate Australia.—El). B.] The
“little brown man” has come to stay, and has
already got a big hold on W. A. Unlike the Chow,
he gains to a certain extent the good-will of the
white man by his disinclination to work for less
than white wages ; also, the larrikin begins to
understand that the Jap. is not merely a tail-less
Chow, but a person who, if “ put upon,” will fight
like a bull-terrier.— Washington 8., Geraldton,
W.A., 17th August, 1896.

RANDOLPH BEDFORD.
The Temperance Lesson Hook.
El). Bulletin,— Last May you allowed me to
point out the various absurdities in Richards in’s
book on “ Drink.” This aroused a storm of indig-
nation in Napier, and it turned out that in the
schools there they use a different book, namely
Richardson’s “Temperance Lesson Book!” in
the cause of education, permit me to note a few of
the more gross mistakes in this latter :
Page 15. “ Camel carries water in cells or pouches.”
This belief is confined to the ignorant.
Page 28. “ Fat brought into solution by digestion.”
Another exploded idea.
Page 102. “All strong drinks act practically as mix
tures of ethylic alcohol and water.”
This is just what they don’t.
P. 106. “No natural wines contain more than 17 pei
cent, of alcohol.”
P. 119. “ All the members of the alcohol family are got
by fermentation.”
And on p. 1311 the author mentions cetyl alcohol-
got by fermenting a whale !
P. 178. “The frog has a constant temperature o;
70deg.”
How the author came to this goodness knows.
As a matter of fact, the temperature of frogs,
fishes, etc., varies with their environment,
whereas that of mammals and birds does not.
These are taken at random, and no doubt
any physiologist using the book in class
would find more; in fact, I noted worse ones, but
not of a kind that I could expose in so few words.
I maintain that we bring all culture into contempt
by officially teaching this rubbish to children.
And the mere fact of such a book being “ success-
fully ” used in our schools throws, incidentally, a
flood of light on the mental calibre of our teachers.
No one but a “common or garden ” ass could teach
from such a book. Even if he knew no physiology
or chemistry, the interesting points raised would
inevitably lead any rational being to look up the
matter in the accepted text-books. To myself the
most irritating thing in the book is a little
excursus on p. 76, about the word “ rum ” being
derived from saccharuni by “ aphteresis.” Just
fancy if you were to interrupt a brilliant invective
against a gas monopoly by spinning the old yarn
about the etymology of “gas!” That “rum”
does not derive from saccharum is quite a minor
point.
Makaretu, M.L.
W. F. HOWLETT.
Ed. Bulletin, —Be ants living on ticks.
It’s true ; I saw it several times. Cotton-oil dips
are as likely to stop the tick-plague as I would
going around single-handed with a whitewash-
brush. The system is ridiculous. Coming tlirough
the tick-infested country between Towers and
Croydon, in some places the ground and grass are
swarming with them; so much so that, while
getting your horses in the morning in the long
grass, you will be fairly smothered with them—, o
much so that you have to change your clothes or
go crazy. They don’t fasten on a man, but the
crawling business would drive one balmy. 'lh'
plain-turkeys are dying everywhere from them,
but the dingoes and wild pigs are all fat, from the
dead cattle, &c., so it is an ill-wind that blows no
one good.—D.T.
Australia is the Land of Mysterious Death
Men step out of the great procession, “from tin'
Mother’s Arms to the Dust-shovel,” and vanish
down bypaths with eerie suddenness. Up to a
short time ago a familiar figure on one of the fa;
Northern rivers (relates a Sydney D.T. contribu-
tor) was an old old man who eked out a precarious
and hazardous livelihood by crocodile catching.
His modus was ingenious and full of weird interest.
He would place on the shore, where it sloped ur.-
obstructedly to the water and where the s.turians
were plentiful, a long, shallow box, not unlike an
elongated coffin. One end was open, and the
bait was placed inside at the far end, attached ton
cord. The hungry ’dile, having wriggled in and
seized it, thereby pulled the door down upon him-
self. The old fellow, his prize successfully
“boxed,” would then get into his boat and tow
his freight out to a passing steamer, and have
it hoisted on board, consigned to an agent in Mel-
bourne. This strange cargo found a ready sale,
and the crocodile-trapper realised fairly large
prices. One of his scaly specimens was exhibit! d
at Melb. Aquarium, and has since thriven exceed-
ingly. But a few weeks ago the old man dis-
appeared. A search-party found his boat moon d
to the shore, and his trap (empty) on the beach.
His blankets were spread out near by, and his
coat, rolled pillow-wise, was dented in the middle
as though his head were still in it. But of ti e
owner thereof no trace has since come to light.
Probably a prowling crocodile came upon him
while he slept and dragged him into the water.

William C. Herbert, U. S. N.,-Tabes Ayer’s Sarsaparilla with Him Everywhere. Disordered
Blood and Bad Stomach Cured Positively and Permanently. That is what Ayer’s Sarsa-
parilla did for Past Assistant Engineer Wm. C. Herbert, whose portrait is here given.

Mr. Herbert has been an officer in the U. S. naval service for the past 16 year*.
His duties have taken him all over the world, and he has been on a constant go for
several years, through the Dardanelles, around the famous rock of Gibraltar, in the
Mediterranean, through the Suez Canal, about the South Sea Islands, in Hawaii,
round Cape Horn, to South America, Africa—everywhere, almost, under the sun he
has cruised as a navy officer. On his return from one of his voyages the strain told
on him. His blood was in a very bad condition, and his stomach was weakened,
and, as he says, he was “ knocked out.” Mr. Herbert says:
‘ Although I look very hearty now, four years ago my health and physical con-
dition were anything but good. Then it was that I found the constant knocking
about had at last told on me. My blood was very poor and my stomach as bad. I
had been cruising about in the service for a long time without any restand without
ceasing from my duties. Frequent changes in water and climate, such as I experi-
enced, will weaken and debilitate the strongest of us, and I was no exception. In
fact, I was completely run down, and when I came home my family insisted on call-
ing in the doctor. He prescribed for me and did all he could, but nothing came of
it, and I grew worse. As my blood seemed chiefly the cause of the trouble I thought
I would give Ayer’s Sarsaparilla a trial, seeing it advertised to cure such cases. I
took a bottle in*an aimless sort of a fashion, but when I found it helped my stomach
and was commencing to clear my blood, too, I thought there might be some real
merit in it, and accordingly began on a second bottle. Well, to make a long story
short, the third completely cured me and restored me to my old-time health. My
blood was hotter tlnin ever, and my stomach did not bother me in the least. Ayer’s
Sarsaparilla cured me. Now I take it whenever I feel that my system needs it, and
I do not consider myself equipped for a cruise anywhere without a bottle of Ayer’*
Sarsaparilla. It has, I assure you, my hearty indorsement and recommendation.’*

“ My Dear Mr. BONNINGTON.
[copy.]
“Theatre Royal, Sydney, April 20, 1896.
“ Allow me, on behalf of Mrs. Holt, to thank you very sincerely for your
kind thought in sending her, on Saturday, a bottle of your famed Irish Moss. I may say that
it is an old and valued friend of ours, and I am sure our esteem of its virtues will be
strengthened by the acquaintance with it which we now renew, through your good agency.
“ With all sincere wishes for your success and well-being,
“ I remain, yours cordially, BLAND HOLT.”
